While writing the text, I have avoided, as far as possible, the use of technical terms which, for lay persons, generally impede the flow of reading. In spite of this, I was constrained to employ jargon sometimes. One reason for this was that an explanation or the meaning of the term in simple language might become long-winded and inelegant. But, in most of such situations I have attempted to indicate the meaning in various ways: giving it within brackets, adding a separate sentence or breaking the sentence into two or more, etc. But there is no escape from jargon! To acquire a vocabulary of technical terms would be rewarding, for they mean much more than the dictionary signifies. Thus in order to help people like myself, I have added a glossary for easy reference.

The terms included in this glossary are those that have been used in the text. I have also added a few which are not in the text. These are words connected with the Buddhist faith and places of religious importance. Lastly I have also added those architectural terms which baffle people like me. There is a reason for including these, though I have mostly avoided them in my text. One is bound to come across with them terms while interested in this and similar topics. I am aware of the frustration of having to repeatedly refer to a dictionary. I do not claim to have made the glossary comprehensive, but just adequate.

The Buddha in the Sanctum: The colossal figure of the Buddha in the garbha-graha (sanctum) is a fine specimen of Ajanta sculpture. The Buddha is seated in the padmasana (lotus-posture) in dharma chakra pravartana mudra (setting-wheel-of-Dharma-in-motion-attitude) is scooped in relief.

On either side is a standing chamara (fly-whisk) bearer. Both the figures are profusely ornamented, while their luxuriantly coiled tresses fall gracefully on one side. The vidyaadhara-s (flying-celestials) with garlands in hand on either side of the halo amidst clouds represented by ringlets are also well conceived figures.

Below, on a double-lotus pedestal is carved the Wheel of Law in profile. This is flanked on either side by a deer to suggest the Mrigadava (present day Sarnath) sermon. In the figures of the five monks flanking the left deer can be recognised pancha vareeya bhikshu-s (first-five-disciples).

The halo and chhatra (umbrella) over the head are painted. The door jambs of the shrine are elaborately carved with scroll-work, floral motifs, naaga-s (people belonging to the serpent world), mithuna-s (couples), female figures standing on makara-s (crocodile motifs), etc. The pillars of the ante-chamber have beautiful bracket-figures of women with attendants.

Though not an art historian or art critic, I was fascinated by Ajanta from my school days. I entered that world through the then available literature. What was on offer, for the most part, were scholarly works; there was a paucity of information for the lay, interested reader. This is so even today. This and my continued passionate interest in it are the only two reasons for my essay into this enchanting world of sublime beauty.

During the entire period of artist activity in Ajanta, the paintings were executed by Buddhist monks without any royal patronage. The caves, the excavation, sculpturing and painting, were completed in two phases: the first during 2nd-1st centuries BC and the next covering a period of two and a half centuries starting from the 4th century AD. In these caves one can see the progress from the early phase of paintings of the pre-Christian era of caves 9 and 10, reaching classical perfection by the 4th-5th centuries, then the falling off into mannerism and the baroque period and, finally, lapsing into artistic decline before the caves were ultimately abandoned by the 6th century AD.

The paintings and sculptures of Ajanta are a storehouse of information about the period: the costumes, textile design, the jewellery, the musical instruments then in use, the social order, court etiquette, ideas of beauty and morality, categories of taste, customs and mores of the people of the time, and their sense of levity and wit.

The paintings themselves, or what survives of them, tell us about the technical aspects of their art: such as the preparation of the ground, the execution of the painting itself with the sense of perspective, line, space division, colour-overlay, the material used in preparation of the pigmentation and the harnessing of the visual and tactile senses to the pacing of the narrative to be depicted. Mysteries abound: the yoking of the sacred and the profane; the adjacency of the naked and the robed, the division of the art activity between the ceilings and wall murals into geometric design and figurative narration, and so on.

The Ajanta paintings are the earliest surviving paintings of India, religious or secular, barring the primitive rock paintings. This spirit of Ajanta influenced the religious art of the whole of Asia: Sri Lanka, China, Japan and the countries of the Far East and South East Asia.

The Buddha and His disciples were Indians - of the same race as the artist-monks who did not feel the need to make a translation from foreign to familiar terms, as did the medieval artists of Europe and the Buddhist Asian countries. They painted and sculpted a world they were at home in; they painted, in a word, the life around them.

My contact with the glory of Ajanta, though not erudite, original or scholarly, has been long and continuous; what I have managed to put together here is all the information that was not available to me from any single source. This essay merely attempts to share my pleasure with you who, in turn, may find it limited or endless.

One of the finest vihaara-s (monasteries) among the cave temples of India, this cave has been dated to the end of the 5th century AD on the evidence of its architecture and sculptures.

The cave has the usual plan, with a verandah, a great congregation hall and a shrine. The verandah, with a cell at each end, is about 20 meters long, about 3 meters wide and about 4 meters high. A central door with carved jambs and entablature leads into the main hall. The ceiling is supported by twenty exquisitely carved and beautifully painted pillars, which are surrounded by an aisle. Facing the entrance, in the rear of the hall, there is an antechamber, leading into the shrine, which is 6 meters square. The vihaara has fourteen cells for the monks to stay.

The front porch has crumbled, but it still contains several bands of fine carvings. Among these are scenes from the life of the Buddha, elephant fights and hunting expeditions. The columns of the verandah and hall are also profusely carved. The shafts have vertical and spiral fluting encircled by bands of lovely tracery. The bases of the capitals are generally ornamented with mythical animals, religious stories and a great variety of floral designs.

The composition is extremely well integrated and the attention of the onlooker is focused on the main object of worship. The sculpture, however, tends to be rather heavy and lacks the grace of that on the facade of Cave 19, and the grace of the sculpture of the Gupta period.

The Great Encounters: Three out of the four encounters, usually called the Ominous Sights, in the life of Gautama which prompted him to forsake the life of pleasure are carved over the cell to the left of the entrance. The panels to the right depict the young prince Gautama on a horse-driven chariot going for a ride, where scenes of death (extreme left), old age (left centre) and disease (right) confront him. At the centre he is seen listening to the music of a lady holding a veena on her lap. Within the three chaitya-arches below, musicians, playing the vina are shown in different poses.

Four Deer with a single Head: The famous 'four deer with a single head' can be found on the central column of the right row of the hall. The rendering of the four deer with one head is so cleverly done that it looks consistent and realistic.

PAINTINGS: Large areas of the walls and the ceiling, once covered with paintings, are much damaged. But, even so, this cave along with Cave 17 contain more surviving paintings than others. The paintings below are listed following the clock-wise circumbulation (pradakshina). Some of the important paintings, which are taken up for explanation, are given below.

As one enters the cave through the main doorway and turns left the first painting one sees on the inside wall of front verandah between the main doorway and the window to the left of it, is Shibi Jaataka of Cave 1, an exquisite composition. The scenes ROYAL PAVILION containing the details, PRINCE AND HIS CONSORT and KING WEIGHS HIMSELF containing among the grieving spectators WAILING WOMEN . Another episode from the same jaataka is painted in Cave 17.

BHIKSHU, JANAPADA-KALYAANI , MAID WITH A TRAY and MAID TRIES TO REVIVE THE PRINCESS, are scenes from the narration of the Conversion of Nanda of Cave 1. This story is painted above the window on the inside wall of the front verandah to the left of the main doorway, beyond the narration of Shibi Jaataka.

An unidentified scene known as PALACE SCENE OF THE NAGA KINGDOM of which a DANCER IN THE NAAGA COURT is painted on the left wall of the inside of the front verandah. Shankha-paala Jaataka is painted on the left side of the mail wall. Most of the scenes are obliterated. Among the important surviving scenes are SHANKHA-PAALA LISTENS TO THE SERMON, of which WOMAN LISTENSTO THE SERMON is a detail, HUNTERS DRAG THE SERPENT and SHANKHA-PAALA IN CONVERSATION WITH ALAARA.

A major portion of the left wall contains the narration of Mahaa-janaka Jaataka, following the story of Shankha-paala Jaataka. The following are the principal scenes of this jaataka. In the first act there are three scenes. These are QUEEN CAPTIVATES MAHAA-JANAKA, DANCING GIRL WITH MUSICIANS and MAHAA-JANAKA GOING OUT THROUGH THE PALACE GATE. In the next act there is one scene in which MAHAA-JANAKA MEETS AN ASCETIC in the Himavali Hill. In the first scene of the third act MAHAA-JANAKA ANNOUNCES HIS DECISION to abdicate. RECONCILED QUEEN and BEWILDERED ROYAL HOUSEHOLD are its details. In the next scene of the third act MAHAA-JANAKA LEAVES THE PALACE on a horseback. The THREE MAIDS are among the royal entourage.

The last scene painted is the SHIPWRECK SCENE, which is greatly damaged. Sufficient portions of this narration still remain to stand testimony to the glorious baroque period at Ajanta.

The panel Lustration and Renunciation comprises three frames, PREPARATION FOR THE CEREMONY, CEREMONIAL BATH and ROYAL HOUSEHOLD PAYS HOMAGE and some consider this depiction to be part of Mahaa-janaka Jaataka, painted on the northwestern wall, exhibits the Ajanta artist's 'multiple vision' technique of perspective.

The two Bodhi-sattva-s, BODHI-SATTVA PADMA-PAANI and BODHI-SATTVA AVALOKITESHWARA, painted on either side of the wall leading to the shrine, particularly the former, are masterpieces. The Bodhi-sattva Padma-paani Panel is also a grand composition, the PADMA-PAANI'S CONSORT, the MITHUNA FIGURES and a KINNARA PLAYING THE LUTE are important from the stylistic and historical points of view also. On the other hand the Bodhi-sattva Avalokiteshwara Panel contains one of the most beautiful women of Ajanta, SHAKTI PANDARA.

The TEMPTATIONS OF THE BUDDHA is Painted over the entire left wall of the ante-chamber. In this, the activities of Maara, the Buddhist devil, are interpreted with a vigour and liveliness which has few parallels, the only competitor being a sculpture on the same theme in Cave 23. The characters used by the devil to distract the Buddha DAUGHTER OF MAARA and MAARA'S ATTENDENT are two of its details.

Champeyya Jaataka is painted on the wall of the rear verandah to the left of Bodhi-sattva Avalokiteshvara above the doors of the two cells. In this compact composion five scenes are painted,. They are: NAAGA KING REPENTS HIS LIFE OF PLEASURES,

KING UGGASENA WATCHES SNAKE-CHARMER'S TRICKS along with the COURTIERS WATCH SNAKE DANCE, APPEARANCE OF CHAMPEYYA'S WIFE AND CHILD, and, finally and, CHAMPEYYA GIVES A SERMON.

THREE MAIDS LISTENING TO THE SERMON, an exquisit portrayal of women in beautiful poses, is a detail from the last mentioned scene.

An unidentified solo painting QUEEN IN A PALACE SCENE, painted on the right hand wall of the main hall, is an example of the composition of the period of declining artistic standards. The WOMAN HOLDING A LOTUS is a detail of the composition.

Another unidentified scene depicts SOME FOREIGNERS, important from the point of view of Ajanta art history, for this painting is of little artistic merit intrinsically.

CEILING PAINTINGS: The ceiling, and some of the pillars, contains some exquisite animal and geometric motifs. FIGHTING BULLS painted on the capital of the second pillar in the left hand corner of the hall is one of the most forceful and vigorous drawings at Ajanta. The CHARGING BULL and ELEPHANT are splendid examples of depiction of animals by the Ajanta artist and they show how conversant he was with the technique of kshaya-vriddhi or foreshortening, a technique of showing depth in drawing.

SQUARES AND RECTANGLES - 1is a sample how the Ajanta artist composed the ceiling paintings with squares and rectangles filling them with all kinds of secular motifs, like CLOWN, MYTHICAL BIRDS and CHERUBS IN PLAYFUL MOOD. One may consider themes like ROYAL HAREM out of place in a religious place.

The caves of Ajanta are situated on the northern fringe of the Maharashtra plateau, in the district of Aurangabad of Maharashtra; Ajanta is about 100 km by road from Aurangabad and about 60 km from Jalgaon, both are connected by rail with Mumbai and Delhi. There are air services from Aurangabad to Mumbai and Delhi. An extended stay at Aurangabad would be rewarding as the equally important Ellora caves are only about 30 km away. The possible explanation for the monastic establishment at Ajanta is its proximity to the ancient trade routes.

When one approaches the site the unexpected sighting of the caves around the horse-shoe shaped gorge is breathtaking. The Waghora river springs from its source at one end, to flow in a series of waterfalls dropping as much as thirty meters and passing through seven peculiar bowls of rock called Sat Kund, before entering the gorge.

The caves extend along a concave line running from east to west for about six hundred meters. A narrow pathway will take you to the entrance of Cave 1, to start the pilgrimage to the highest achievement of Indian Buddhist art - a beginning to the end of a great tradition and a glorious chapter in the world history of wall-painting. One can have no doubt about the suitability of the location for uninterrupted meditation and contemplation.

The story of the generous King Shibi must have been very popular. Anecdotes of this king are found in Hindu and Jain lore. In Buddhist literature also several stories are attributed to the charitable King Shibi. Two incidents are painted in Ajanta, the story of the king rescuing a pigeon in this Shibi Jaataka of Cave 1, and, the story of Shibi donating his eyes to a blind person in Shibi Jaataka of Cave 17. The full 'pigeon and hawk' anecdote, with scales for weighing an equivalent weight of flesh, is not to be found in the earlier versions of the jaataka stories.

THE JAATAKA: Bodhi-sattva was born as King Shibi, the benevolent monarch. Gods Yama and Indra wanted to test his magnanimity. Indra took the form of a pigeon and Yama that of a pursuing hawk. The pigeon sought protection from the king. But the hawk claimed the pigeon as his legitimate prey and appealed for justice. The king, in order to protect the surrendered pigeon, offered a portion of his own flesh equal in weight to that of the pigeon. As he started cutting his flesh and putting it on the scale, the pigeon became heavier and heavier. Finally the king himself had to climb on to the scale. It is then that the gods revealed their true forms and showered great boons on him.

The story in this cave is depicted in three episodes on the inside wall of the front verandah, between the main doorway and the window to the left of it.

The first episode starts on the left. This shows King Shibi in a palace surrounded by ladies of the court when the pigeon alights on his lap to seek refuge. This part of the painting is greatly damaged. Towards the right there is ROYAL PAVILION containing the detail, PRINCE AND HIS CONSORT. The second and main episode, KING WEIGHS HIMSELF, begins immediately to the right of this pavilion. In this scene the king is standing by the side of the scales and appears to be on the point of sacrificing his own flesh to save the life of the pigeon. The three lovely figures in WAILING WOMEN are among the five figures on the right-hand side of the Prince. The Bodhi-sattva's ordeal is the cause of their lament.

The third episode, which is painted below the two just mentioned, is damaged almost beyond recognition; but a few fragments show that it is the climax in which the gods, human beings, animals and birds are rejoicing at the Raja's success.

In both the Hinaayana and the Mahaayana phases of development inflection of line and gradation of tone achieve the illusion of volume. The Ajanta artist could choose between two techniques to create an illusion of depth and relief. One was animnonnata (flat) style of painting, where representation is achieved by simple juxtaposition of flat areas of contrasted colours in well-defined planes and, the other was nimnonnata (high-relief) style, in which this effect was brought out by various methods of shading (vartana).

But what is more noteworthy is that the two styles, as a number of painting show, seem to overlap at every stage of the development of the art. This is in contrast with the Western tradition where the development of art is generally linear.

WAILING WOMEN and PRINCE AND HIS CONSORT, details in this narration, are examples of this animnonnata style. This has a remarkable parallel with the 'cloisonnage' of Gauguin. These figures, set against the green leaves of a peepal tree beyond which the vermilion horizon can be seen, produce a pleasing colour pattern.

In Shibi Jaataka of Cave 17 another episode from the life of the benevolent king where he gave his own eyes to a needy blind Brahmin is painted.

Conversion of Nanda of Cave 1 is an episode from the life of the Buddha and some scenes from it are painted above the window on the inside wall of the front verandah to the left of the main doorway, beyond the narration of Shibi Jaataka of Cave 1.

The Story: After attaining Enlightenment, the Buddha converted both his wife and his son to the monastic order, to lead a life of compassion and austerity. But his cousin, Nanda, was weak at heart and was initially reluctant to join the order, but later converted to monkhood. He had his head shaved and went about begging as a mendicant. On hearing of this his wife Janapada-Kalyaani swooned. However Nanda was not really happy being a monk and was brooding over his beautiful wife. The Buddha wanted to show him the right path. The Buddha took him to the heavenly abode of Indra, the lord of the gods. Nanda could see women more beautiful than his wife and wanted to stay there permanently. He was then ridiculed by the others for favouring a life of pleasure that is transitory. Bitten with remorse Nanda came to his senses. Coming back to the earth he lived the life of a monk following the Law.

There is also a view that the scene depicts the happenings at Kapila-vastu, when the Buddha paid a visit to his father and then went to the chamber of his wife Yashodhara to beg alms of her.The mural is greatly damaged, but from what is surviving we have a fair idea of the composition from the line sketch of BHIKSHU AT THE PALACE DOOR.

We have in this scene Nanda now a BHIKSHU standing at the door of his own house begging for alms. He is calm, dignified, and radiating a lambent glow of compassion. He has come to his own house seeking food.

Inside the apartment on the left, an attendant is seen announcing the arrival of Nanda as a BHIKSHU to Princess JANAPADA-KALYAANI, Nanda's wife. The attendant seems very excited. He kneels down deferentially to announce the BHIKSHU. His dress is fascinating, almost modern, and we come across this dress in other scenes. Perhaps it was the standard livery for palace attendants! To his right is a MAID WITH A TRAY in her hands. Despite the damage, her poise is exquisite. There is another female figure to her right sitting face-to-face with the princess. With her right hand she is seen explaining something to her mistress.

JANAPADA-KALYAANI, sitting on a throne with down cast eyes, has a melancholy expression. She is extremely graceful and dominates the entire scene. The graceful fingers of her left hand show the agitation in her mind. The dark-green backdrop presents a contrast to the reddish brown and yellow colours of the central figure. The colour used for backwash, it can be seen, is evenly laid without many nuances, so as not to distract from the main figure.

The custom of women decorating their forehead with a tilak, also known as bindi, is a common sight today. But, in the Ajanta portraiture we find women sporting tilak only infrequently. Perhaps this became mandatory in the Hindu way of life later. In this mural we find that both the princess and the lady-attendant with a tray are both wearing the bindi.

The confusion and excitement created within the palace and witnessed in the expressions of the attendants on seeing the king in the garb of a beggar, the agitated and melancholy look of the princes, all combine to tell the same story.There is one more detail of this story in the greatly damaged painting. In this we see a MAID TRIES TO REVIVE THE PRINCEESS.

A well-built maid with a glowing complexion tends her mistress, not included in the picture, a suffering princess reclining on a couch. She bends over the rails holding a pitcher in her hands; while water and flower-petals pour out, she literally pours herself. The scene is saturated with pathos. It is an example of one of the many damsels 'dressed in nakedness' in the Ajanta imagery. The painter has utilised all artistic means in order to emphasise the theme in its minute details, making all the accompaniments support the emotional content of the motif.

The circular lines of the pitcher balance the curves of the maid's body, though all the curves stroking the body-line, head or bust, are downward and heavy-laden. The anxious eyes of the attendant shower benediction. The upper limbs of the body, the neck, the bust and the waist, and the hips, are delineated in great detail. The pitcher-like breasts stand in incredible contrast to her slim waist. The colours are soft, and the artist has avoided sharp contrasts keeping in line with the melancholic mood of the scene.

Conversion of Nanda story must have been popular then, as we find this story is depicted in Cave 16 as well. We will be witnessing a few very poignant scenes of the narrative in this cave, like DYING PRINCESS wherein the same scene is depicted with even greater force and feeling. The composition is also different. The Ajanta artist was not monotonous.

Punyaka, a yaksha general, who was flying on the back of a winged horse, hears her song and is attracted by it. Coming down he introduces himself. This scene is painted to the left of the previous scene. The separation of this composition into two scenes follows the Ajanta convention of placing a post or a pillar between two scenes. In this second scene she is standing and leaning against a post, talking to Punyaka. Obviously Punyaka has proposed marriage to the princess. Punyaka's horse is by his right side.

Now the action shifts to the palace, depicted on the other side of the swing.This part is also shown in the line drawing PUNYAKA'S ARRIVAL. In this KING VARUNA'S PALACE SCENE, we find the king seated along with his son on a slightly raised platform talking to his wife and his daughter on the proposal of marriage. The king and his son have serpent-hoods over their heads indicative of their naaga royalty. The son is in a contemplative mood. Irandati is talking as can be seen from her hand gestures. The rich hair-dresses of the characters and the textiles in this scene, particularly the cloth on the bolster, would provide us information for the study of the textile designs of the day. Next we witness Queen Vimala and Princess Irandati having an intimate conversation regarding the marriage. This is painted above the third cell door to the right of the scene of Vidhura-pandita giving a Sermon, which is the last scene. The conversation takes place on a balcony. The princess looks coy and abashed, and the mother, we find from her gestures, is giving her some advice and, perhaps, assurance.

This vihaara, although slightly smaller, has a similar plan to that of Cave 1. The verandah is about 2.5 meters wide and has a length of about 14 meters. The facades of the chapels at each end of the verandah are carved with figures of the naaga kings (serpent-kings) and their attendants, the portly gana-s (demigods).

A richly carved doorway flanked by a decorated window on either side, leads to the main congregation hall. The congregation hall is about 14.5 meters square and the roof is supported by twelve massive, elaborately carved pillars.

In the VIEW OF THE INTERIOR OF CAVE 2, we can see the pillars having very minute carvings and painted all over.

In many pillars the paintings on the bases are still discernable as may be seen in the VIEW OF THE MAIN HALL. Rich tapestry of the paintings on the ceiling may be seen both the views. There are ten cells off four corridors. At the rear of the hall there is an ante-chamber leading to the shrine.

MAIN SHRINE: The entrances to the ante-chamber and to the shrine are richly carved. On the frames are sculpted a number of mithuna-figures, and the lower-most figures on both the sides are carved majestic gate-keeping yaksha-s. Towards the ceiling are images of the river goddesses, Ganga and Yamuna.

The shrine contains a large figure of the Buddha in the dharma chakra pravartana mudra (turning-the-Wheel-of-Religion-attitude). He is flanked by two figures of Bodhi-sattva acting as chaamara-bearers. The painting on the ceiling of this shrine, SQUARES AND CIRCLE - 3, is in a better state of preservation. On the ceiling of the ante-chamber we have in the ceiling painting SQUARES AND CIRCLES - 6 whose motif is very interesting.

On either side of the ante-chamber is a chapel, the entrances to which are adorned with delicately-carved pillars. The chapel on the right is the Hariti shrine and is important because of its sculpture.

HARITI SHRINE: The Hariti shrine is the one to the right of the ante-chamber. The story of the conversion of the cannibal Hariti into the guardian-deity of the born infants is represented in a synoptic manner. Hariti and her consort Panchika are seated side by side, with the chaamara-bearers on either side, and a woman with a bird perched on her left hand and a flower in her right hand, between them. Two incidents of the former life of the Hariti are carved in the background, one each on either side above the whisk-bearers. On the right she is aggressively arguing with the Buddha to restore her child purposefully concealed by the Master. In the other she listens attentively to the words of wisdom of the Master, who advises her to give up cannibalism and to take up the protection of the children of rajagriha-s.

On the pedestal is an interesting scene of a classroom revealing the Ajanta artist's sense of humour. Starting from the right we find three sensitive boys engrossed in writing before a teacher holding a cudgel. The next two boys are boxing. And lastly, a group of five boys is goading two rams to fight.

The cave has the best ceiling paintings that have survived. On the ceiling of this shrine we find a beautiful ceiling decoration.

PAINTINGS: The cave contains some very important paintings. The paintings in the 'Hariti' shrine are a testimony to the highest classical taste of Ajanta. The narration of Vidhura-pandita Jaataka belong to the baroque period. Finally, the paintings on the ceiling are the best in the whole Ajanta complex.

GATE IN KING BRAHMA-DATTA'S PALACE, a scene from the Mahaa-hamsa Jaataka of Cave 2, painted on the left wall of the front verandah, is an example of normal perspective.

Nativity of the Buddha, many scenes of which somehow survived, is painted on the left wall. The narration starts with the FUTURE BUDDHA IN TUSHITA HEAVEN, wherein we find the gods request Him to be born as the Buddha. Among the important surviving scenes of this story are, the KING LISTENS TO THE INTERPRETATION OF THE DREAM, QUEEN MAHAA-MAAYA MUSING about the astrologer's interpretation, and, finally, the BIRTH OF THE BUDDHA. Ajanta classicism at best can be enjoyed in the painting called VOTARIES WITH OFFERINGS found on the walls of the Hariti shrine.

Belonging to the same period and painted on the same Hariti shrine are the compositions CHILDREN PLAYING WITH HEN and CHILD TRYING TO SNATCH A TOY.

On the left-hand wall of the ante-chamber of the shrine is painted, THOUSAND BUDDHAS, a scene from the Miracle of Shraavasti of Cave 2. The painting belongs to the period of artistic decline at Ajanta.

On the other hand, the representation of the IMAGES OF THE BUDDHAS is painted on the rear wall of the cave and is artistically superior to the one earlier described.

On the wall of the right corridor are painted a number of scenes from the Vidhura-pandita Jaataka. The scenes are IRANDATI MEETS PUNYAKA, KING VARUNA'S PALACE SCENE a compact composition depicting Some Episodes from VIDHURA-PANDITA JAATAKA.

These episodes are GAME OF DICE, VIDHURA-PANDITA COUNSELS DHANANJAYA, VIDHURA-PANDITA GIVES FAREWELL SERMON, QUEEN VIMALA AND PRINCESS IRANDATI and VIDHURA-PANDITA IN NAGA KINGDOM. The last painting is DEPARTURE OF VIDHURA-PANDITA.

Ceiling Paintings: This cave abounds in decorative designs painted on pillars, pilasters, ceilings, and for that matter, on every available surface. The motifs adopted for decoration are fruits (mangoes, pineapples, pears, grapes, berries, lichies, etc), flowers (lotus, waterlilies, champaka, etc), birds (geese, ducks, cranes, peacocks, parrots, etc), fantastic and sportive figures (dwarfs, buffoons, jugglers, foreigners in exotic garments, etc) and pure geometric patterns. For the geometric patterns, the most popular composition being a square containing a number of concentric circular bands around a central lotus-design, with the four corners - called spandrels - embellished with mithuna-s (couples), birds or mythical beings. The circular bands, in turn, are filled with wreaths of flowers. The best preserved of the ceiling paintings are in the sanctum.

SQUARES AND CIRCLE - 3 has flying cherubs in the spandrels, while mithuna-s are painted in the spandrels of SQUARES AND CIRCLES - 7 and Detail of SQUARES AND CIRCLES - 7. In the ceiling painting SQUARES AND CIRCLES - 6 the row of geese in a ring is a superb rendering.

As in Cave 1, the ceiling decoration consists of filling up the space with squares and rectangle. Belonging to this mode of decoration is SQUARES AND RECTANGLES - 2.The ceiling also contain some scenes in a lighter vein, like, the FOREIGNERS DRINKING.

There are four scenes depicted in Mahaa-hamsa Jaataka of Cave 2. The story is painted on the left wall of the front verandah. The lotus scenes are on either side of the cell door. The palace scene is above the cell door.

That the Ajanta artist was conversant with both normal perspective and reverse perspective was mentioned elsewhere. It was also pointed out that the reverse perspective was employed in the scene DANCING GIRL WITH MUSICIANS. We shall come across the reverse perspective in the GAME OF DICE, a scene from Vidhura-pandita Jaataka painted in this cave.

In this panel, GATE IN KING BRAHMA-DATTA'S PALACE, the artist has employed the normal perspective. The colouring and the shading bring out clearly the form, shape and geometry of the gate and its environs. Anther example of the use of normal perspective is the panel MONASTERY, a scene from Shibi Jaataka of Cave 17.

Several scenes depicting the Nativity of the Buddha have been painted along the left wall of the hall above the lintel and also along the sides of the first and second cell doors.

The Story: First we shall follow the story of the birth of the Buddha as depicted in this cave. The Future Buddha was living in Tushita heaven. All the gods from the 'thousand worlds' requested Him to be born as the Buddha. The Great Being consented and informed them of the time, place and the family for his final birth. Thus during the mid-summer festival Queen Mahaa-maaya, queen of King Suddhodana of Kapila-vastu, had a dream. In this dream she saw a white elephant entering her womb through her right side. She related this dream to her husband, who consulted the revered Brahmins to interpret the dream. They said that the queen had conceived a male child, who would either become a Universal Monarch or become the Buddha. For confinement Queen Mahaa-maaya proceeded to the residence of her parents.

Midway, in the beautiful grove of shaala trees, called Lumbini Grove, she stood holding the branch of a shaala tree and gave birth to a son. Brahma, Indra and other gods descended on to the grove to pay their respects to the newborn. 'The Baby looked around in all directions, and strode forward seven paces, followed by Mahaa-Brahma, holding over him a white umbrella'.

This chaitya (temple) is one of the earliest caves to be excavated and is assigned to the 1st century, BC. The plan of this temple is rectangular, and it has an architectural dignity all its own. It has twenty-one octagonal pillars that are about 3 meters high. The interior dimensions of the cave are 13.5 meters long, 7 metres wide and 7 meters high. The stoopa carved at the end of the hall has a dome one metre high above a cylindrical base and is crowned by a square capital representing the reliquary, a receptacle for relics. The chaitya has a beautifully decorated facade with a berm-railing, windows and lattice frame. The large figures of the Buddha carved along the sides of the cave are later additions in 5th-6th centuries.

The chaitya contains paintings of different periods. Some of these belong to the 1st century BC, while others are assigned to a period as late as the 4th-5th centuries, AD.

A GROUP OF VOTARIES APPROACHING A STOOPA painted on the left wall is a representative of the era of pre-Christian art.

A painting of a KNEELING ASCETIC is included as an example of the 5th century AD painting and the variety of hairstyles painted in Ajanta.

Most of the paintings from the earliest periods have, unfortunately, perished. Also many of the paintings copied by Griffiths in the last century have been consumed by fire. An extant sketch, A GROUP OF VOTARIES APPROACHING A STOOPA is reproduced to give an idea of the lost treasure.

In the figure is a group of sixteen votaries who are approaching the door of a stoopa. The variety of their postures and gestures and their hairstyles intertwined with strips of cloth are remarkable. The men's headgear, consisting of a topknot with elaborately plaited terminations protruding over the forehead, and their decorative waistbands are also extremely interesting. This very closely resembles the sculptures of Sanchi and Bharhut, both belonging to the same period as this cave. The grouping of the figures is natural, and is typical of the painting of this period.

On the right, the scene depicts the stoopa, which clearly shows the typical architecture of the 1st century BC. The stoopa is enclosed by four walls, which have two gates. The gate on the left side has a barrel-shaped roof with timber rafters, like those of the contemporary caves at Karle and Bedsa, both in the state of Maharashtra. The gateway on the right side is in the style of the Sanchi gates, and this painting is therefore thought by some to be contemporaneous. In the middle stands the stoopa under a number of umbrellas, which are Buddhist emblems of dignity and sanctity. Outside the enclosure can be seen the devotees and a banyan tree.

From the paintings of Ajanta one can get a fairly good account of the life style of people of the period. KNEELING ASCETIC is included as an example of the style of painting during the 5th century AD painting and the variety of hairstyles painted in Ajanta.

This is a painting done over the pre-Christian era painting. It depicts an ascetic at his prayers. The posture of kneeling, his hands of prayer and, finally, the matted hair, all these are very similar to present day practices.
