## MDGAFEEBC@?EB>?<?@DAAB>BE@ADDGLGBBHFDGGHFGLCDG?==>@BA@;>??=@<B@;DEC@??@>@AEFACFBGHV

t@C=9>FCACA;:BB?AC@??;=?>FEGFEDBKGEHICJGIGDD=AFFB>>==<;<>>?:<>>AD?;?9;CA>CF@FJGGR

qLJuve`[Y]XW]erbTdmsmgmpqaZWTVU_^VIPQPMKPTTS]_b_`]\_Zclohegg]]_ffeeihi[JPNMOMNNMJNPi

On the strength of an inscription this chaitya (Sanctuary) is considered to be the oldest of the caves and has been assigned to the first half of the 2nd century BC.

It has an impressive, lofty ceiling and a spacious nave. Altogether it is about 29 meters long, 12 meters wide and 11 meters high. Thirty-nine pillars, which divide the aisles from the nave, support an entablature that rests on the vault of the barrel-shaped roof. The pillars are 12 meters high and lean inward in imitation of the posts of the ancient bamboo structures, to support the weight of the curvilinear roofs made of wooden rafters and crossbeams.

The chaitya, like Cave 9, contains paintings of different periods. On the strength of two inscriptions the oldest are thought to be roughly contemporaneous with the excavation of the cave in the 2nd century BC.

The paintings in this cave are distinguished by the fact that even though the two groups of surviving pieces are separated by a period of nearly six hundred years, both groups were created at period of stylistic transition. In this cave two changes in styles can be noticed. The earlier paintings belonged to the pre-Christian era, and the change from the style of this period to the classical phase is the first one. The 5th century paintings such as GAUTAMA, THE BUDDHA and THE BUDDHA AND THE ONE-EYED MONK, painted on the sixth pillar on the right-hand side of the cave, were created in the course of transition from the classical phase to the baroque phase. The Gandhara influence can also be seen in these compositions.

We are fortunate that there are traces of surviving paintings, RAJA WITH HIS RETINUE and the accompanying DANCERS AND MUSICIANS, to give us an idea of the pre-Christian era paintings at Ajanta. This painting is important in the history of Indian painting, for this is the earliest survivor.

We have also some excellent line sketches of SHAD-DANTA JAATAKA of Cave 10 and Shyama Jaataka. Both these examples are impressive for their dynamic modelling and story-telling.

The composition, SHAD-DANTA JAATAKA of Cave 10, is important for another reason. The same jaataka is narrated in the 5th century AD in Cave 17, and this offers a comparative study of styles separated by six centuries.

A painting, TWO FIGURES OF THE BUDDHA, is included to as an example of the painting belonging to the period of artistic decline at Ajanta.

This painting RAJA WITH HIS RETINUE is one of the rare surviving masterpieces of the pre-Christian era. A true understanding of the evolution in style that took place in the subsequent period is not possible without making some reference to it. The painting enshrines the pictorial manner as well as the social mood of the time. It is painted on the rear side of the left aisle of this chaitya.

This painting, like those belonging to the pre-Christian era paintings of Ajanta, is painted longitudinally in narrow horizontal panels, maintaining the same tonal level. The scenes unfolding on a ribbon-like space on the walls are not subdivided as in the later period except for an occasional tree, or a building or a group of rocks, which vaguely defines the end of one scene and the beginning of another.

In this painting the Raja, accompanied by ten ladies and a child, is seen approaching the Bodhi tree decorated with banners. The women, nearly naked from the waist upward, are wearing simple ornaments comprising earrings, necklaces, armlets and bangles. Especially notable are the ivory bangles and those made of conch-shell, which cover almost the entire forearm. These are still in fashion among the Lambadi tribes in the Deccan. The women's hairstyles vary from the highly elaborate to the quite simple. On their foreheads a few ladies sport the tilak, circular colour-marks, such as Indian women still wear today.

Another notable feature of the dress of this period is the veils over the women's heads. In the later period these are conspicuously absent, perhaps because of the influence of the North on the Deccan in the 3rd century, AD after the disappearance of the Andhra rule and the establishment of the Vakataka-s.

Their round faces with small bright eyes, short pointed noses, narrow mouths and full lips are southern features. At Ajanta the darker skins and non-Aryan features completely disappeared for quite some time, and reappeared possibly in the second half of the 5th century AD.

The Raja has a band of jewelry round his topknot of hair, which is arranged like the hood of a serpent. This was emblematic of his mythical descent from a serpent-king. On the other side of the Bodhi tree is painted a group of women DANCERS AND MUSICIANS.

In the entourage are a group of DANCERS AND MUSICIANS, painted to the right of the panel RAJA WITH HIS RETINUE.

Bare to the waist, these women appear to be a party of common folk who have improvised an orchestra of their own to please the Raja and also to pay homage to the Bodhi tree. Two in the party have long trumpets and the rest are clapping their hands or dancing. The elegance of these figures conveys an atmosphere of carefree, uninhibited joy.

With their outstretched arms, simple gestures, their obviously improvised movements and the way in which every girl leans in the direction in which the group is moving, produces a delightful rhythm.

To understand the landmarks in style, this Hinaayana masterpiece may be compared with DANCING GIRL WITH MUSICIANS, a scene from Mahaa-janaka Jaataka of Cave 1 belonging to the Mahaayana group done five hundred years later. The later though technically sophisticated and advanced, as can be seen in the elaborate sketches drawn in varying thickness of line and filled in with a variety of bright colours, betrays an affected style.

The personages in this painting are painted in dark brown or burnt sienna, the soft hues of a few colours, red ochre, yellow ochre, terra verde, lampblack and lime white. The themes are forcibly expressed in a style closely related to the contemporary sculpture in the round and in relief at the great Buddhist shrine of Sanchi.

The costumes, the veils over the women's heads and the architecture depicted are typical of the Sanchi sculptures of the 2nd-1st century BC. Drawn in long, bold and vigorous curves with great precision, in a line of unvarying thickness, these representations speak of the artist's dexterity of drawing and expression.

An attempt to provide depth and relief is seen in the slight variation in tones, though the number of colours used is limited. Experiences would be gained in the next five centuries to master the art of relief modelling, through various means, like, vartana (shading) and ujjotana (adding-highlights).

Almost all the paintings belonging to the early phase of Ajanta, that is, 2nd-1st centuries BC, are lost. One surviving painting is RAJA WITH HIS RETINUE in this cave. The Ajanta pioneers have made copies of the then existing paintings, both as line drawings and in colour. A notable painting illustrated here by a line-drawing is the story of SHAD-DANTA JAATAKA of Cave 10 depicted on the back wall of the right-hand aisle behind pillars two to twelve. (shat = six, danta = tusked) This picture has suffered much from vandalism.

This painting, like those belonging to the pre-Christian era, is painted longitudinally in narrow horizontal panels, maintaining the same tonal level. The scenes unfolding on a ribbon-like space on the walls are not subdivided as in the later period except for an occasional tree, or a building or a group of rocks, which vaguely defines the end of one scene and the beginning of another.

The story is not told in a strict chronological order. This feature we find in the paintings belonging to the early as well as the later periods. The sequence of narration has been shown in the figure SHAD-DANTA JAATAKA of Cave 10.

The Jaataka: The jaataka is the story of Bodhi-sattva born as an elephant-king with six tusks, hence called Shad-danta. One of his two wives, Chulla-subhadda, became angry with him as she felt that he neglected her in preference to the other queen. She, in the next birth was born as the Queen of Varanasi. She commanded that his tusks be produced before her to quench her vengeance. But when the tusks were actually brought before her, she breaks down and repents.

It is an excellent picture, full of love for life and nature, in which the artist has made full use of his proficiency in the drawing of animals and birds. The elegance of the figures and facial expressions reflect the late Sanchi sculpture.

A comparison of this composition with the one depicting Shad-danta Jaataka of Cave 17 during the 5th century AD, brings out both the continuity and the variation in the artistic styles over a period of six hundred years.

1. Shad-danta's Life in his favourite Resort under a Banyan Tree: In this drawing is shown a herd of elephants disporting themselves in the jungle amidst lotus flowers, ashoka and banyan trees, among them being the white, six-tusked elephant. The body of this elephant is dotted all over with small brown spots.

This vihaara shows the vigour and elegance of Buddhist architecture in the 5th century AD. The spacious verandah, about 20 meters long and about 3 meters wide, is flanked in front by six octagonal pillars and two pilasters. The hall, which is about 22 meters square and 4.6 meters high, has three entrances. The pilasters on both sides of the main doorway have sculptures of beautiful women standing on makara-s (crocodile-motifs). The hall contains twenty columns. The shrine, with a colossal Buddha, is scooped out from the back gallery of the hall, which has two side-chambers. The vihaara has no antechamber.

An interesting feature of this cave is that its plan is incised in an inscription on the left-hand wall near the end of the facade.

SCULPTURES: The front aisle has its ceiling carved in imitation of wooden rafters and beams. Carved on the bracket-capitals are some of the fine gandharva-sculptures, perhaps intended to convey the effect of a celestial region. These figures - dwarfs, musicians and flying couples - are shown in varying moods, a few groaning under weight, some serious, others taking it lightly and still others enjoying it. Specially interesting are the two vidyadhara-s (flying couples) notable for their graceful poise and features.

PAINTINGS: There are a number of paintings that have survived the ravages of time and vandalism. The following have been taken for detailed discussion.

MAHO-SADHA AND FOUR ENVIOUS COUNSELLORS, a scene from Mahaa-Ummaga Jaataka, the story of the supernatural child Maho-sadha, is painted on the left wall of the front corridor, and is continued on the adjoining pilaster.

The story of the Conversion of Nanda is narrated in this cave also, and more forcefully than in Cave 1. It is painted in the left corridor, above the doors of the first and second cells from the left. The scene in which NANDA SHAVES HIS HEAD is a realistic portrayal, and NANDA IN DEJECTED MOOD is full of pathos. But the most important scene and one of the best compositions of Ajanta is DYING PRINCESS. Several scenes from this episode are found in this cave and are worth studying in detail.

Maha-Ummaga Jaataka is painted on the left wall of the front corridor, and is continued on the adjoining pilaster.

The Jaataka: Maha-Ummaga Jaataka is the story of Bodhi-sattva; who as a mere seven-year-old the boy, Maho-sadha, successfully solved the riddles which were posed to him by the people to test his supernatural powers and preached the Law.

The painting was praised by Griffiths in 1874. He could not copy it because it was covered with mud and grit. It was partially cleaned by the two Italian restorers in 1920-21. However, the gelatine they used to brighten the images soon turned yellow and opaque. The Archaeological Department of the Government of India has recently removed this layer of gelatine.

The explanation of the jaataka is supported by a line sketch SUPERNATURAL CHILD MAHO-SADHA and a colour picture MAHOSADHA AND FOUR ENVIOUS COUNCILLORS, which is a detail of the mural.

Let us look at the line sketch SUPERNATURAL CHILD MAHO-SADHA. The lake, garden and building shown in the painting are apparently the tank, grove and palace built by the supernatural child, which are mentioned in the jaataka.

The top left-hand corner of the picture shows a rider, representing the councillor of King Videha, who was deputed by his master to test Maho-sadha's wisdom. He wears a coat with full sleeves and striped stockings, while a straight sword hangs by his side. One of the horse's hind legs is stretched backward and the other raised forward to convey the feeling of rapid movement. Foreign influence can be seen in the rider's uniform; similar costume and accoutrements are frequently depicted in the other cases.

At the bottom barely visible is a scene depicting 'the riddle of the son' where Maho-sadha was called upon to settle a dispute between a woman and a goblin over the motherhood of a child. Maho-sadha ordered the child to be cut into two so that each could have equal part.

Realising this would lead to the death of the child the mother of the child gives up her claim and thus Maho-sadha establishes the rightful owner. In the surviving part of the painting we see a man and a woman holding out the child's body while a soldier raises his sword to cut the boy in two.

In a plantain-grove below the royal rider is the scene MAHO-SADHA AND FOUR ENVIOUS COUNCILLORS. On the left there are four figures apparently representing the four envious councillors who attempted to destroy Maho-sadha. The two men nearest to the boy have long hair, while the third wears a Persian cap. The expression of the fourth is especially sinister; his hair is dishevelled and his hand is on his sword as he looks at the boy in a wild and threatening way. The boy, wearing a small cap on his head, is gesturing to emphasise a point.

The painting is especially interesting because an illusion of distances is conveyed by depicting the group of men against a comparatively spacious background that is fringed by the conventional bands of hills. The mountains are in perfect harmony with the equally stylised water, which is represented by semicircular arches. The barrel-shaped roofs and heavy curvilinear eves bear a striking resemblance to the rock-hewn caves of the early 5th century.

Three scenes from the Conversion of Nanda of Cave 16 are painted on the left wall above doors of the first two cells of the hall. They are, NANDA SHAVES HIS HEAD, NANDA IN DEJECTED MOOD and the DYING PRINCESS. Of these the last, showing the death-scene of Nanda's newly married wife, heart-broken at Nanda's renunciation is very famous.

NANDA SHAVES HIS HEAD is the first scene, in which Nanda is seen sitting on the floor and a barber is shaving his head. Only the lower part of the body of the barber is visible. But a noteworthy feature of this panel is that the posture of Nanda and the inclination of his head show a keen sense of observation by the artist. A young monk is watching the scene.

We see NANDA IN DEJECTED MOOD in the next scene. The tonsured Nanda is seen sitting in a pillared pavilion brooding over his pathetic condition and is filled with remorse thinking about his beautiful wife. His sitting posture is realistic, with his knee supporting his right elbow and his hand on his right cheek. The pillars are beautifully ornamented.

Finally, we come to one of the most beautiful paintings of Ajanta, popularly known as DYING PRINCESS. The painting is greatly damaged, and to assist studying the composition a line sketch, DEATH OF THE PRINCESS, is also included.

The lady is seen in a faint on a couch at the sight of a crown presented by a messenger at the left-hand bottom corner. The entire panel is gloomy and melancholy. The expressions of the characters in the scene, together with their gestures, reflect extreme anxiety and the seriousness of the situation.

The messenger holding the crown is fully aware of the grave news he has brought. His eyes are full of sympathy for the unhappy lady. The other male attendant above him, wearing a light skullcap in the Persian style, has raised his hand in excitement. To emphasise the expression of pathos, the artist places highlights on the nose, lips and chin of these two figures.

The central figure is that of the dark complexioned princess who has fainted on a low throne. Her back is resting on an embroidered cushion and her legs are stretched out in front resting on the floor. She has her left elbow on the cushion, and a female attendant behind the throne holds the lady with both hands so that she may not fall down. The head of the lady hangs limp and is slightly inclined towards the artist to reveal a three-quarter frontal view instead of her profile. Her half-closed eyes indicate extreme mental agony and physical collapse.

A second maid on the right of the lady is eagerly looking at the mistress with her mouth half-open. She has a ribbon tied around her forehead. A third maid, wearing a tight upper cloth is fanning the mistress with a palm-leaf fan. Through utter panic she has twisted her figure in an ungainly and somewhat awkward manner. Her gawkiness and the half-open mouth of the other girl are certainly not things of beauty, but the artist appears to be aware that at moments of tense anxiety even a female beauty may well forget how to look her best.

At the back of the pavilion, a little higher up, there is a roof enclosed by a parapet under a banana tree. Two maids are seen there engaged in intimate conversation. One of them has a veil over her head. She has a pitcher in her left hand and with the right she makes the sign of the figure two, by raising two of her fingers. She is of a fair complexion, has a youthful face, but her left eye is damaged. The other maid of dark complexion has a muffler round her neck, the knot of which is prominent. Her extended left hand wearing a bangle is significant. It appears that the fair girl has raised her two fingers in reply to the other's question.

On the roof of the pavilion perches a gorgeous peafowl with its head bent low. The peacock with its head raised high and its chest thrown out is an accepted symbol of pride in beauty. But as the theme of the picture is its utter defeat, the peacock is seen with its head drooping in sorrow. The entire panel is gloomy and melancholy.

This vihaara, perhaps excavated a quarter of a century after Cave 16, has a similar plan. The verandah, about 20 meters long and about 3 meters wide, has massive pillars in front. The main hall has three entrances, and the central door is adorned with carving. The twenty columns dividing the corridors on the four sides of the hall, which is about 20 meters by 6 metres has an ante-chamber, which is about 5.6 meters square.

The shrine contains a massive figure of the Buddha in the dharma chakra pravartana mudra (turning-the-Wheel-of-religion-attitude). The central figure is flanked by the Bodhi-sattva Padma-paani on the right and the Bodhi-sattva Vajra-paani on the left.

A VIEW OF THE MAIN HALL OF CAVE 17 shows the cloth canopy-like ceiling painting in the main hall along with glimpses of decorations on the pillars and the walls. On the other hand a sample of the murals on the walls, in balance with the ceiling paintings as seen in the left aisle is given in A VIEW OF THE AISLE OF CAVE 17.

The paintings in the vihaara are in a comparatively good condition and this cave, like Cave 1, is a veritable paradise for art-lovers. Some of the important paintings are:

The long narration Vishvaantara Jaataka is done in two parts. The story starts on the left verandah to the left side of main entrance and the first episode, VISHVAANTARA DISTRIBUTES ALMS is painted here. The next two scenes, VISHVAANTARA CONSOLES HIS WIFE and VISHVAANTARA'S DEPARTURE are depicted beyond the door.

The second version of the story occupies a large part of the left wall of the hall. The principal scenes are: VISHVAANTARA INFORMS HIS WIFE, the departure of the royal couple, of which the CITIZENS WATCH VISHVAANTARA'S DEPARTURE is a poignant detail, the RESTORATION OF CHILDREN in King Sanjaya's Court and, finally, the CORONATION OF VISHVAANTARA. An interesting detail of the palace scene is MAID BRAIDS THE HAIR.

DESCENT OF INDRA and other celestials flying to adore the Buddha may be found on the rear wall of the verandah to the left and right of the door. Among these celestials are a YAKSHA-COUPLE and the famous BLACK APSARAS.

The Miracle at Raja-griha, is an incident in the life of the Buddha when he subjugated a mad elephant. The miracle is depicted above the two windows to the right of the main door in the rear wall of the verandah, of which DEVA-DATTA HATCHES A CONSPIRACY, MAD ELEPHANT NALAGIRI and the ultimate SUBJUGATION OF THE ELEPHANT are the surviving important scenes.

Shad-danta Jaataka of Cave 17 is painted on the outer wall of the front corridor, to left of the main entrance. The principal scenes of this narration are, QUEEN PLANS HER REVENGE, SPORTING SHAD-DANTA with his herd, SONUTTARA BRINGS THE TUSKS of Shad-danta to the royal court and the QUEEN FAINTS on seeing them.

Two anecdotes from Mahaa-kapi Jaataka are painted in this cave. The first one is depicted on the wall of the front corridor to the left of the main entrance MAHAA-KAPI SAVES HIS HERD. The other episode is painted on the left-hand wall of the back corridor and the MONKEY HELPS A MAN and MAN THROWS STONE ON THE MONKEY are two of the important scenes.

Sutasoma Jaataka is narrated on the wall of the back corridor and continued on the bend of the wall towards the left above the cell door.

ROYAL KITCHEN OF SAUDAASA, in which SAUDAASA TASTES HUMAN FLESH, ARMY DRIVES SAUDAASA TO THE FOREST and KING FIGHTS AGAINST HIS ARMY are some of the surviving important scenes of this story BUDDHA PREACHES TO THE CONGREGATION, MAHAA-MAAYA LISTENS TO THE SERMON, A WOMAN ELEPHANT-RIDER and the AUDIENCE are the principal scenes of the episode describing the Enlightened One preaching the Law in heaven and its sequel, both of which are painted on the left wall of the ante-chamber.

The masterpiece of the classical period, MOTHER AND CHILD BEFORE THE BUDDHA may be seen on the rear wall of the ante-chamber to the left of the shrine door. This is a scene from the visit of the Buddha to Kapila-vastu, of which MOTHER AND CHILD is a detail.

The story of an elephant's love for his blind parents, the Maatri-poshaka Jaataka is painted on the rear wall of the back corridor between the first and second cell-doors to the right of the ante-chamber and some of its scenes are painted in the CAPTURE, FREEDOM AND REUNION, of which THE HAPPY REUNION WITH THE BLIND PARENTS is a detail.

MAHISHA JAATAKA, the story of a benevolent buffalo, is narrated in the right-hand wall of the rear corridor, to the left of the cell door.

Simhala Avadaana is a grand composition and belongs to the classical period of Ajanta. It covers the entire wall of the right-hand corridor and continued on the upper part of the pilaster between this corridor and the one behind it.

The principal scenes are RAAKSHASI-S AS BEAUTIFUL MAIDENS with details like RAAKSHASI FEIGNS LOVE and MERRY COUPLES UNDER TENTS and a detail DALLYING COUPLE UNDER A TENT the havoc caused by CANNIBALISTIC RAAKSHASI-S, ESCAPE OF SIMHALA and MERCHANTS SHOW THEIR GRATITUDE TO BODHI-SATTVA, INFATUATED KING, MASSACRE IN THE PALACE, VULTURES ON THE GATE, Simhala's ARMY GETS READY, SIMHALA RIDES A WHITE ELEPHANT, and final victory over the raakshasi-s.

A solo-picture, A LADY AT HER TOILET is painted on the upper part of the pilaster between the front and right corridors.

The episode from the Shibi Jaataka of Cave 17, of a king giving away his eyes, is painted on the pilaster between the front and the right corridors and continued on the on the wall of the right corridor. Of this narration A MONASTERY set up by the king to distribute alms and the royal scene after the king has donated his eyes, KING SHIBI IN PAIN and its details WAILING WOMAN -1 and WAILING WOMAN - 2 are taken for discussion.

The Mriga Jaataka, or the story of the golden deer, of which the KING ARRIVES IN THE FOREST, a HUNTER ATTEMPTS TO CAPTURE THE DEER, THE DEER CARRIED ON A CHARIOT and the KING RETURNS ON A HORSE-BACK are some of the important scenes narrated on the wall of the front corridor.

And, finally, Mahaa-hamsa Jaataka, of which a FOWLER BRINGS THE GEESE TO THE COURT and the GOLDEN GOOSE GIVES SERMON are two important scenes painted on the left wall and the adjoining pilaster of the front corridor, to the right of the cell door.

The cave has a number of surviving painting on the ceilings. The following are some of them. SIX GANDHARVA-S, each pair of adjoining gandharva-s having one arm common to both is one. The shamiana-like painting shown in the INTERIOR VIEW OF CAVE 17, covering the ceiling of the main hall is a grand composition. Its detail is given in GEOMETRICAL DESIGN.

A VIEW OF THE AISLE OF CAVE 17 shows the decoration on the ceiling over the aisles, a detail of it in the common motif, THE SQUARES AND RECTANGLES - 3. And RESTING BULLS, an interesting study of animals in repose, painted in one such square.

A number of episodes from the Vishvaantara Jaataka are painted in this cave and cover a large portion of the wall on the left aisle, except for the first scene which is painted on the left side of the main verandah. Belonging to the classical period of Ajanta, the lovely composition provides a wealth of information about the prevailing taste of this period.

The Jaataka: It is the story of Bodhi-sattva born as Prince Vishvaantara who became famous for his generosity. It may be mentioned that as per Buddhist belief this was the last Bodhi-sattva incarnation before being born as the Buddha. Vishvaantara was the son of King Sanjaya and queen Phusati in the city of Jetuttara. He exhibited his generosity from the time of his birth. But what angered his subjects was his gifting of his magical elephant, which could bring rain to the neighbouring kingdom of Kalinga, which was going through severe famine. To assuage their feeling King Sanjaya was constrained to send his only son in exile.

Vishvaantara, who left the kingdom along with his wife Maadri and two children Jaali and Kanhajina gifted away his chariot and horses to needy persons. A Brahmin, Jujuka, then, begged him to give away his sons to alleviate his sufferings. The benevolent prince complied. Jujuka ill-treated the boys and took them to Jetuttara, where people recognised the captive boys as the grandchildren of their king and felt deeply remorseful for banishing their prince.

The king summoned Vishvaantara and Maadri from their hermitage. He paid a handsome ransom to the greedy Brahmin to release the two boys. Thus the family came to be happily reunited.

The drama starts with VISHVAANTARA DISTRIBUTES ALMS painted on the left side of the outer verandah. A large congregation of beggars, ascetics and attendants surround Prince Vishvaantara and Princess Maadri. Dark in complexion Vishvaantara is sitting on a low stool. The lower part of his figure is damaged. The expectation in the face of the assembly is well portrayed. Of some interest are the square shaped umbrellas some of the people are carrying. The scene faithfully depicts the usual commotion associated with any alms giving ceremony and the attempt by the royal attendants in trying to maintain order.

The second scene of Vishvaantara informing Maadri of his banishment and subsequent departure is painted above the entrance to the first cell. This is shown in the line sketch BANISHMENT OF VISHVAANTARA and two colour reproductions VISHVAANTARA CONSOLES HIS WIFE and VISHVAANTARA'S DEPARTURE.

In this scene, VISHVAANTARA CONSOLES HIS WIFE, we find the prince, seated on a low throne in the pavilion is supporting Maadri, his wife, with his left hand and with the right offering her some stimulating drink. The expression of the couple shows anxiety and concern. The demeanour of the princess is extremely graceful. She leans on her beloved with complete submission. Her face is ravishing. With her dreamy half-closed eyes she looks obliquely at her consort. Her distress is indicated by her clinging to her husband for support and the prince's concern by his proffering her a cup of wine. The figure of the prince, badly damaged, registers a look of anxiety.

Beside the royal couple there are three figures inside the pavilion. The ubiquitous dwarf holding a vessel with raised eyes is standing in front. We have seen similar dwarfs in almost all the royal scenes. The third attendant in the composition is the one standing beside the pillar, wearing a greenish jacket and a Persian skullcap, and holding a liquor-jug, ready to pour out the contents into the cup of the prince. It will be noted that the glances of all the participants of this high drama converge on the hapless prince. There is one more female figure squatting Indian style with a water-pitcher on her lap.

The pavilion is supported by four round pillars. The wall and the pillars are reddish, while the parapet is yellow and the floor green with a sprinkling of white flowers here and there.

What follows is the scene of VISHVAANTARA'S DEPARTURE to the left of the earlier picture. In this we witness the young royal couple leaving the palace watched by the attendants.

The central figures are identified by the umbrellas held over their heads, signifying their royalty. Also the physiognomy of Vishvaantara and Maadri remains unchanged: Vishvaantara depicted as dark complexioned with hair falling over the shoulders and with hair-band. The princess has two maids-in-attendance on either side, in addition to a male attendant with a drooping moustache. The prince is barefooted and clad only in a loincloth and some flowers in his hair.

Among the people present we see prominently a beggar with a crooked staff, possibly testing the generosity of the prince. Between the pavilion and the garden is painted a window, through which the king and the queen watch the departure of their beloved son.

The narration continues above the space between the third and fourth cell-doors, with Vishvaantara and Maadri taking leave of the king in one pavilion and then the queen in another pavilion.

To the left of the previous scene is depicted VISHVAANTARA INFORMS HIS WIFE. This is a repetition of an earlier scene. Here the artist has tactfully placed them at right angles to each other, as if they are seeing each other. Why this scene was necessary, when the same scene was enacted before, and more forcefully, we may never know! It may be that the story is told again starting from this scene. This guess is prompted by the fact that the narration is continuous from here to the end of the jaataka. The portrayal of both Vishvaantara and Maadri is superb bringing out the compassionate and gentle nature of the royal couple.

The Ajanta artist did not just paint scenes of religious themes on the wall, but embellished them with beautiful details of everyday happenings which may in no way be connected with the main story. For we have an exquisite painting of MAID BRAIDS THE HAIR of a woman.

In the middle of the wall between the second and third cell-doors, that is somewhat below the previous scene, the prince and his family are seen departing in a decorated carriage drawn by four horses. Among the crowd are disconsolate CITIZENS WATCH VISHVAANTA'S DEPARTURE portrays the departure of the prince and his family leaves the city. The composition is one of the masterpieces of Ajanta.

Let us now follow the procession. In front of the chariot are four Brahmins. The composition is interesting for the depiction of the market place through which the chariot passes. We see a shop with the milkman with two pails of milk and a basin of curd. There is shop where the oil-vendor is ladling oil into a cup. A weighing machine can be seen in the next grocer's shop. The buildings are two storied with shops in the ground floor and residences on the upper floor. Women are watching the departure of the prince from the open windows of these upper-floors. This reminds one of a similar scene in MAD ELEPHANT NALAGIRI in the episode called Miracle at Raja-griha, also in this cave, where a market scene is depicted.

In the next scene we find the royal family standing on the ground, and we should presume that the chariot has been donated to the four Brahmins we saw in the last scene.

Then follows the depiction of their life in Mount Vamka. In the damaged mural we can barely see the daily chore of Maadri, like collecting fruits in the wilderness, etc.

The next scene depicts the gifting away of his sons to a greedy Brahmin, Jujuka. Vishvaantara is seen in front of a hut made of leaves (parna-shaala). Vishvaantara informs Maadri of the development. Vishvaantara is sitting in front of the same hut and the figure of Maadri is drawn with the greatest care. She is a personification of sorrow.

In the next scene of happy reunion of the royal family, painted above the fourth cell door entrance, we see the events that take place in the court of King Sanjaya. The description of this scene is supported by a sketch KING SANJAYA'S COURT and a colour picture RESTORATION OF CHILDREN.

At the left bottom corner we see the earlier sequence that of the guard enquiring of Jujuka, the greedy Brahmin, about the children. The guard is adorned with jewellery and the bejewelled sword hanging from his belt is very prominent. The posture of this guard recalls that of the guard of Avalokiteshwara in Cave 1. King Sanjaya is seated on a low throne with a rich diadem over his head and some precious jewellery on his person. He has a conventional lion-waist (simha-kati). In front of him Jujuka is seen receiving the ransom from the royal treasurer with his hands extended. To the left of the king is seen Queen Phusati seated on another stool. Between the king and the queen the artist has drawn Jaali, one of Vishvaantara's children. The figure of Kanhajina, the other child, is on the left of the queen. At the foot of the king there are three noblemen apparently discussing among themselves the miraculous return of the children.

The finale of CORONATION OF VISHVAANTARA is mostly damaged, and what little remains, it is claimed, seems to have been retouched in a later period. The coronation scene, however, is well conceived. Vishvaantara is seen sitting in a throne while holy water is being poured over him. Queen Maadri is watching it from a pavilion on to his right. There are two women carrying offerings on a tray, both of them in striking graceful tribhanga (triflex) poses.

While describing BODHI-SATTVA PADMA-PAANI in Cave 1 it was mentioned that, according to the canons of Indian painting, the limbs of human characters are related to the shapes and forms of the vegetal and animal worlds. Among the examples cited was the comparison of torso with the body of a lion. The torso of King Sanjaya in this panel is lion-waisted (simha-kati), and is an example of such sadrishya (similitude).

The figure of Jujuka in this series of paintings shows the mastery of the Ajanta artist in character-study. The artist has delineated all his ugly features - the broken front teeth, the goatee beard, the bald forehead and the spiky hair at the back of his skull, the parrot-like nose and small uncanny eyes, combined with the brightness which has come over his face at the jingle of the money which the royal treasurer is pouring into his outspread scarf.

Two more points arrest attention. Firstly, the continuity of costume and of make up. The artist has not forgotten that a corner of the Brahmin's umbrella is torn! This has been repeated carefully in all the scenes. Secondly the posture of one's 'hair standing on end in expectation' has been literally given expression to while drawing Jujuka at the moment of receiving the golden coins.

The scene, DESCENT OF INDRA is painted on the back wall of the veranda to the left of the central door and by the side of the paintings depicting the alms-giving episode of Vishvaantara Jaataka. In the continuous panorama of murals on this wall, this group of six figures could be singled out showing the DESCENT OF INDRA with his troupe of celestial musicians from the heavens above. This may be taken as a solo-picture for this can neither be connected with any incident in the life of the Buddha nor associated with a jaataka.

A line sketch INDRA WITH HIS ENTOURAGE and the colour reproduction DESCENT OF INDRA may be consulted while following the following description of the mural.

The central figure, that of Indra, is the biggest in size. He is of fair complexion, embellished with rich jewellery in the form of necklaces, armlets, bangles, earrings and a magnificent crown over his head. He has a dagger and a sword attached to his belt. The painting of Indra illustrates the extraordinary breadth and sureness of the draughtsmanship. The individual features, eyes and nose and mouth, are defined with free, flowing sweeps of the brush. The varying thickness of the lines provides a kind of shading, and the very freedom of the drawing imparts both freshness and boldness to the forms as well as the decorations.

The suggestion of movement is unmistakable. The strings of pearls round Indra's neck all swing in the opposite direction to the movement. The ends of ribbons tied round the hair are also flying. To emphasise movement towards the right-bottom corner, the artist has skilfully drawn a cluster of clouds, piled up in the opposite direction that is, receding towards the left top. The delineation of the cloud in the form of white patches with blue rims of varying thickness are so drawn as to appear as successive columns of cumulus cloud-heaps.

To the right of Indra, is shown a gandharva (celestial) with coarse features, a bulbous nose and sunken eyes, carrying a slender musical instrument. The up-turned loose ends of his upper garment with yellow stripes over green background, also suggest movement. The illusion of movement in space is skilfully created through these suggestions and the bent knees of the figures.

During this descent, Indra is accompanied by celestial musicians. To the right of Indra, there are two celestial nymphs. The one nearer the central figure, with a greenish or greyish hue, holds a pair of cymbals in her hands. Her eyes are downcast. She appears to be engrossed in music, oblivious of her surroundings. The dark girl next to her, also playing a pair of cymbals, is looking back at her companion while levitating forward. These two girls are loaded with sapphires, diamonds and pearls, although their garments are scanty, exposing the contours of their full breasts. The younger person near the left leg of Indra has a musical instrument, a sort of drum. With a twist of the neck he is looking back out of the corner of his eyes.es.

The most striking figure of the entire group is that of the flute-player who is a little ahead of the floating group. She is in her early teens, and since her head is turned away the artist has availed himself of this opportunity of displaying her coiffure by dividing the dark chignon into four quadrants by means of ribbons.

Musicians have been shown in the scenes of DANCING GIRL WITH MUSICIANS and MAHAA-JANAKA LEAVES THE PALACE in the narration of Mahaa-janaka Jaataka in Cave 1 and in the BODHI-SATTVA PADMA-PAANI PANEL.

We have seen in the other compositions, like the scenes QUEEN CAPTIVATES MAHAA-JANAKA and MAHAA-JANAKA LEAVES THE PALACE of Mahaa-janaka Jaataka in Cave 1, that the Ajanta artist has used a variety of sthaana-s (body positions) while portraying characters

The suggestion of weightless grace and elegance in these figures reflects the postures of Indian dance and reminds us of the effortless flight in the Gupta sculpture or the Pallava sculpture at Mahabalipuram. The entire composition is rich in tonal harmony creating that gay mood, that idyllic charm mellowed with vibrant movement and music, which is the essence of this mural.

To the right of the central door we see a few gandharva-s and kinnara-s being a part of Indra's train, floating through clouds towards the earth to worship the Buddha. Among these celestials proceeding to pay homage to the Buddha are a YAKSHA-COUPLE and a BLACK APSARAS flying effortlessly through clouds, strong winds swaying their ornaments and tassels.

Flying celestials are common in Indian paintings and sculpture. Flying vidyaadhara-s are frequently seen behind the main deities, on the brackets, and in any place the artist feels would highlight the theme. We saw Indra descending to the earth to adore the Buddha. The treatise Vishnu-dharmottara prescribes the qualifications for the painter. It says: "He who paints waves, flames, smoke, streamers fluttering in the air, according to the movement of the wind, should be considered a great painter." Accordingly Ajanta painters took great pleasure in composing scenes involving movement with great zest.

The floating clouds, the swaying foliage and apsaras and gandharva-s flying swiftly through the air, produce a fantastic movement and excitement, and every connoisseur of art was intoxicated by its beauty. Often this depiction is considered to have been influenced by Kalidasa's poetry. Or is it the other way about?

We shall start with the BLACK APSARAS. A line sketch BLACK BEAUTY may also be referred to. One of the most beautiful single heads in the entire Ajanta complex is that of this flying apsaras, commonly known as Black Apsaras (nymph). She drifts through the air while beating out a measure on tiny cymbals. Her beautiful jewelled ornaments would seem to place this picture in the class of painting described in the shaastra-s (canons) as most admired by women. Around her neck is a necklace of diamonds, sapphires, and pearls, its heavy pendants swaying with the nymph's movement. She wears matching strings of jewels in her hair. The pendulum movement of all the dangling ropes of precious stones to the right is echoed in the direction of the stiff white riband behind her head: the direction of all these ornaments subtly indicates the apsaras's flight towards the left of the composition.

The face of this apsaras is in the form of an elongated ovoid, almost the leaf shape often recommended in the shaastra-s. This, along with her narrow eyes and tiny mouth, suggests wistfulness and ethereality. The head has a haunting evocation of sensuous warmth, and, as so often at Ajanta, the hands pliant, elegant and infinitely graceful. Both in the lyrical beauty of the physical types and rich contrast between the dark flesh and the shining jewels, the paintings of Cave 17 are close in style to the murals at Bagh, in the state of Madhya Pradesh, some 250 km away.O

Regarding the dark complexion of this apsaras, it is worth recalling that we come across with many black beauties in Ajanta. In fact, most of the beautiful women we see in Ajanta are dark complexioned. Thus, SHAKTI PANDARA, the dark princess, painted in the panel of Bodhi-sattva Avalokiteshvara in Cave 1, Princess Irandati of Vidhura-pandita Jaataka in Cave 2, The DYING PRINCESS of Cave 16 and this famous Black Apsaras, who are all painted 'as dark as the night' have already been referred to.

This may be, it was felt, due to the southern influence. This line of thinking was due to the fact that Bodhi-sattva Avalokiteshwara depicted in the same panel as that of the dark beauty, SHAKTI PANDARA, is attributed to the southern influence.

Perhaps, at some time in the past Indian aesthetics accepted black as beautiful. Weren't both Rama and Krishna dark complexioned?

In the entourage we find a YAKSHA-COUPLE. The composition is really sculpturesque!

This painting is another example how the Ajanta artist has handled the various techniques for plastic modelling, particularly in making a choice between vartana (shading) and ujjotana (adding-highlights) to suit a given theme. The sparkling diamonds, sapphires and pearls in her necklace and other jewellery proclaim this.

The ujjotana method of highlighting applied to the chin and cheek is soft and subtle, keeping in view the mood of the subject matter, an example of classical restraint. This can be contrasted with a more liberal application of white patches to the characters of Vidhura-pandita Jaataka in Cave 2, heralding the beginning of mannerism in Ajanta.

Above the main entrance leading to the main hall of this cave there is a row of eight figures, the seven MAANUSHI BUDDHAS and Maitreya, the Future Buddha. Below this row, on the lintel, are eight panels of MITHUNA FIGURES in amorous attitudes. Sculptured figures of river-goddesses Ganga and Yamuna on a makara (crocodile) flank each side of the doorway.

Maanushi Buddhas: The eight Maanushi Buddhas (Mortal-Buddhas) are Vipashyin, Shikhi, Vishva-vasu, Kraku-chchhanda, Kanaka-muni, Kashyapa, Shaakya-simha (Gautama) and Maitreya. The first three show three different mudra-s, namely, dharma chakra mudra (turning-the-Wheel-of-Religion-attitude), dhyana mudra (meditation-attitude) and abhaya-mudra (assurance-attitude), respectively. The next two figures are deep in meditation. According to the cultic conventions, all the figures have some common features, such as curled locks of hair with an ushnisha (turban, actually, a characteristic mark of hair on the head of the Buddha), long ear-lobes and down-cast half-closed eyes. The sixth Buddha, Kashyapa, is painted with a pale reddish hue and the seventh Buddha, namely Gautama, has the conventional yellow robe on his person. The last in the list, Maitreya, the Future Buddha, is the only one wearing a magnificent crown and other jewellery. This is because, unlike others, he is yet to renounce all worldly possessions.

Mithuna Figures: Under each of the above Buddha figures is a small rectangular slab showing mithuna figures (couples). The varied tones of their skins, display a remarkable sense of colour and a controlled rhythm in the draughtsmanship.

Sportive and jolly, they are sipping wine, caressing or wooing the partner in love, although one cannot find any coarseness in their compositions. In one of the panels a girl offers a cup of wine to her beloved; in another, the male figure, recumbent on a bolster, is caressing his ladylove; in a third the two lovers are almost embracing each other.

One wonders the propriety of selecting eight amorous couples under the Buddha-figures in a vihaara, a chapel-cum-dormitory for celibate monks. Admittedly, the figures are realistic, and the composition, drawing and colouring are excellent; yet, with our contemporary sensitivity, we cannot but feel a little ill-at-ease at the awkward juxtaposition of the incompatible ideas conveyed by the two parallel rows. Perhaps, the possible explanation is that the artist-monks had no inhibition in portraying the pleasures of life.

Kinship between Sculpture and Painting: Two exquisite sculptured figures of riverine deities, Ganga and Yamuna, lend support and add welcome grace to the doorway. The kinship we find between sculpture and painting, as that between painting and dance, in India is something altogether unique in the history of world-art. Most impressive is the way the two art forms co-exist at Ajanta, complementing each other. Artistic ingenuity has reinforced the relationship.

This kinship is apparent in this complex. Each figure is in concord with the other, sculpture blends with the murals, colours with the colour scheme, flower-studded door-panels with the glowing wallscape and the decoratively painted ceiling. A yearning for mutual exchange and unity that we find here permeates the walls of Ajanta, resounding with the story of this unity bonded in faith.

It may be mentioned here that in Ajanta and elsewhere even the sculptures were fully painted, though most of the paint has disappeared. The blending achieved must have been astonishing when the stone carvings were bedecked in colour, falling in line with the hue and colour scheme of the murals. One can only imagine the riot of colours of the original scheme.

PALACE SCENE OF THE NAGA KINGDOM is from an unidentified scene, and is painted on the left wall of the inside of the front verandah. The line sketch NAGA COURT also helps us in appreciating the composition. The king, in whose court a dance is being performed, is apparently naaga raja (the serpent-king), since he and his queen have halos of cobra-hoods over their heads. The crowded scene, composed of beautifully attired ladies of the court, female attendants, dwarfs and other courtiers, is typical of the late period.

The principal DANCER IN THE NAGA-COURT is sketched in extremely graceful curves, with long sweeping brush-strokes, creating a perfect rhythm and balance. The silky shine of her blue jacket is complemented by her pinkish complexion, and her bangles and coiffure are depicted with great care. The colour scheme of clear blue, deep purple-brown and rich malachite-green with touches of clear red, is very beautiful. She is drawn in the paraavrita ("head and one shoulder turned backwards") described in the post-Gupta treatise, Vishnu-dharmottara.

A new colouring technique in the use of lapis lazuli, a cool transparent blue, came into use in Ajanta only in the 4th-5th centuries AD.

This is employed during the Mahaayana period to considerable effect in a number of paintings to produce a sense of distance and depth, and thereby to enhance the feeling of movement and animation. The attire of the dancer, her entourage and the dancing pose she strikes may be compared with those of DANCING GIRL WITH MUSICIANS, a scene from Mahaa-janaka Jaataka in the same cave.

The treasure of Ajanta came to light thanks to the manoeuvres of a company of British soldiers in the year 1817. Soon pioneer archaeologists were attracted to the caves that were lost to civilisation for more than twelve centuries. James Burgess and Major William Gill made copies of some of the paintings and exhibited the first copies of the Ajanta paintings at the Crystal Palace in London in 1866. Unfortunately almost all of these replicas of the pictures in their original state perished in a disastrous fire. Later far less adequate copies were made by Griffiths and Lady Herringham, to be published in 1896 and 1915. Before Indian independnce, Ajanta was a part of Hyderabad state. Under the patronage of the Nizam, the then ruler of Hyderabad, Yazdani, who was the director of archaeology, edited and published two volumes on the paintings of Ajanta in 1933.

The possible explanation for the monastic establishment at Ajanta is its proximity to the ancient trade routes. When one approaches the site the unexpected sighting of the caves around the horseshoe shaped gorge is breathtaking. The Waghora river springs from its source at one end, to flow in a series of waterfalls dropping as much as thirty meters and passing through seven peculiar bowls of rock called Sat Kund, before entering the gorge. The caves extend along a concave line running from east to west for about six hundred meters. A narrow pathway will take you to the entrance of Cave 1, to start the pilgrimage to the highest achievement of Indian Buddhist art - a beginning to the end of a great tradition and a glorious chapter in the world history of wall painting. One can have no doubt about the suitability of the location for uninterrupted meditation and contemplation.

The beginning of the Ajanta chapter is a tribute to the religious tolerance of Indian rulers.

The earliest caves in Ajanta (Caves 9 & 10) were excavated during the rule of the Saata-vaahana-s, who had their capital at Pratishthana (called, Paithan, to day). During their rule there was brisk trade and commerce within the land and with the Mediterranean world, which brought in enormous riches.

The second phase was of greater artistic activity at Ajanta during the rule of the Vaakaataka and Chalukya dynasties from the 4th to the 7th centuries AD.

The rulers of the Vaakaataka and Chalukya dynasties, like the Saata-vaahana-s, were themselves Hindus but all these kings actively allowed Buddhism to flourish in their territory.

However it must be conceded that there was no direct royal patronage during almost the entire period of Ajanta's artistic activity. But the rich mercantile community, organising itself into guilds, had provided the requisite patronage.

Prof. Walter Spink of the University of Michigan at Ann Arbour, has devoted his whole life to unravelling the secrets of Ajanta. According to him Ajanta represents the apogee of the classical age and gives valuable insights into the material life, aesthetics and spiritual values of the period. Using contemporary detection techniques, he claims that he can date each painting and each sculpture on a year-to-year basis. According to his research, the second - that is the maharani - phase of Ajanta building activities began in 462 AD and was completed in just 18 years!

The Ajanta caves represent only the culmination, not the renaissance, of a very ancient Indian tradition of painting. Even the very earliest paintings at Ajanta, from the second century BC, demonstrate a sophisticated technique in every way comparable to the achievement of sculpture of the early classical period (4th-5th centuries AD).

For example, the Kaama-sootra of Vaatsyayana, the well-known treatise on eroticism, enumerates 'Six Limbs of Painting', whereas Samaraangana-sootra-dhaara, a 11th century treatise on architecture prescribes 'Eight Limbs of Painting'. Brihat Samhita (6th century AD), and Vishnu-dharmottara (7th century AD) also set forth the rules and canons of painting. It is in these that we find classifications of the types of painting suitable for temples, palaces, and private dwellings and a distinction made between 'true, lyrical and secular' paintings.

Just as the sculpture of the Gupta period provided a standard for the entire Asian world, so the paintings of Ajanta, in style, in type and in technique, exerted their influence on Buddhist art for centuries to follow. The paintings in the Bagh caves, about 400 km from Ajanta in the State of Madhya Pradesh, the paintings at Ellora, the 8th century Jain paintings of the early Pandya-s at Sittanavasal near Pudukkottai in Tamilnadu, Hindu paintings of the Chalukya-s at Badami in Karnataka are perpetuations of the refinement of the great murals of the Ajanta caves. The famous paintings of Sigiriya in Sri Lanka, contemporary with the great period of activity at Ajanta, may be regarded as rather bold provincial reflections of the Ajanta manner. This is true of the 12th century decorations of the northern temple at Polonnaruwa, in Sri Lanka, represent a conventionalised derivation from the narrative panels in Caves 1 and 17.

The spread of Buddhism northward and eastward over Turkestan to China brought with it a diffusion of the great styles of Indian sculpture and painting. The vast painted complex that once decorated the niche of the 175-foot Buddha at Bamiyan in Afganisthan represents the Ajanta style modified by a somewhat more linear and decorative Iranian idiom. The 7th century sanctuary of Fondukistan, also in Afganisthan, was decorated with paintings of Bodhi-sattva-s and Taras, which in their sensuous elegance are directly derived from the Ajanta style.

The creative period of Ajanta ended as mysteriously as it had begun. Some of the unfinished caves, which were quite obviously abandoned unexpectedly, show that the emigration took place over a comparatively short span of time. Even if some of the caves continued to be lived in for a few hundred years after the 6th century, the merchant wealth that financed the decoration and ornamentation of the shrines had apparently been exhausted. In any event, the administrators of the monastic establishment could not have foreseen the impending misfortunes and privations, or they would not have planned new vihaara-s in the hard rock, only to leave them incomplete.

A parallel to such an unceremonious end can be seen in the abrupt cessation of sculptural activities in Mahabalipuram almost at the same time as that of Ajanta, leaving behind almost all the sculptural masterpieces incomplete.

The caves are aligned in a horseshoe form. There are a total of twenty-nine caves. The caves are numbered not on the basis of chronology, but on the basis of their physical location. The first cave in the outermost extremity serves as an entry point and is numbered one, and then the other caves follow sequentially. The general arrangement was not preplanned, as they sprang up sporadically in different periods.

The caves are of two types: Buddhist temples called chaitya-s and Buddhist monasteries called vihaara-s. There are five chaitya-s and the remaining are vihaara-s. Some of the caves are unfinished. The caves belong to two distinct phases of Buddhist rock-cut architecture, separated from each other by an interval of about four centuries.

The earlier group of caves is 8, 9, 10, 12, 13 & 15A belonging to the pre-Christian era and belongs to the Hinaayana sect of Buddhism. The earliest is Cave 10, which was excavated, in the 2nd century BC. After a quiescence of about four centuries, excavation was revived on a much more ambitious scale, resulting in the remaining twenty-two caves that now stand as testimony to the Mahaayana sect of Buddhism. Caves 1, 2, 6, 9, 10, 16 and 17 are important in order to appreciate the Ajanta paintings. Caves 1, 2, 4, 16, 19, 20, 23 and 26 contain some exquisite sculptures.

<7DH<H@HHC999911/114Y.D999DD<799779I7<9H<:HHH<LHj*AJj:AZjEBNjJB<LdJNpZbbjh[~pJbbbbR

Later during the Mahaayana phase it gave way to upright, ornamented pillars in the Mahaayana group of caves.The stoopa-dome also changed from a plain hemisphere to a cylindrical form with elaborate carving and sculptures. The vihaara-s are rectangular halls with cells for the monks on the inner sides, leading out to verandahs. The oldest vihaara-s at Ajanta, Caves 8, 12, and 13, in that order, were the monasteries attached to the earliest Hinaayana chaitya-s, Caves 9 and 10, which have a square central hall, without pillars, opening into small living-rooms with stone beds. The vihaara-s of the Mahaayana group consist of Caves 6, 7, 11, 15, 16, 17, 18, 19 and 26, which are said to belong to the period between AD 450 and 550; whereas Caves 1, 2, 3, 4, 27 and 29 were perhaps hollowed out at a later period. By the middle of the 5th century the plan of the vihaara included the function of the chaitya by introducing imposing statues of the Buddha in place of the stoopa-s. And in order to accommodate a more numerous laity an antechamber and a shrine in the back-wall called the chaitya mandiram were added. The magnificent Caves 1 and 2 are both assigned to the 6th century AD, and represent Ajanta's resurgence of sophisticated painting in the face of the Hindu revival. Caves 1, 2, 4, 16, 19 and 26 contain some of best sculptures of Ajanta.

Chalukya dynasty: In the 6th century the Vaakaataka-s succumbed to the mighty power of the Chalukya-s. It is probable that the decorations at Cave 1 at Ajanta were done during the reign of Pulakesin II, who died in 642 AD.

The subjects of the pictures are chosen mostly from the jaataka-s, Buddhist mythological stories of the previous lives of the Master. Episodes from the life of the Buddha form the next important theme in Ajanta.

The famous episodes are THOUSAND BUDDHAS, a scene from the MIRACLE OF SHRAAVASTI, of Cave 2, the famous scene painted in Cave 17 depicting the Buddha meeting his wife and son after His Enlightenment titled MOTHER AND CHILD BEFORE THE BUDDHA and the Nativity of the Buddha of Cave 2. Some of these, like the first two are mostly self-contained, whereas some like the last one is narrative.

A few of the solo-pictures do not seem to have any religious import like the famous LADY AT HER TOILET of Cave 17.

The paintings in the last category are decorative in character. Here the Ajanta artist has chosen themes from all facets of life; real and imaginary embellished them with pure geometrical patterns in myriad forms. Floral decorations, geometrical designs, birds, animals, mythical nymphs and monsters fill up every square inch of available space - on pillars, triforia, pilasters and the ceilings. It is worth mentioning that the ceilings contain paintings only of secular themes.

In the early phase of Indian wall decoration, that is, during the 2nd-1st centuries BC, the narrative paintings are arranged in the form of a long canvas, with the action progressing from episode to episode. This can be seen in the surviving paintings of this period in Caves 9 and 10. The scenes unfold on a ribbon-like space on the walls, but are not subdivided as in the later period except for an occasional tree, or a building or a group of rocks, which vaguely defines the end of one scene and the beginning of another.

An interesting feature of the Ajanta narration, both during this period as well as the later period, is that a strict chronology of events was not followed.

The giant canvas-like mural depicting SHAD-DANTA JAATAKA of Cave 10 is an example these two features.

In later centuries the paintings overspread the entire surface of the wall. In these paintings narratives proceed from scene to scene and from act to act harmoniously. The scenes are not separated into frames that might disturb the concentration of the viewing devotees.

The Ajanta artists used specific conventions for separating scenes and acts from each other, using suggestive punctuation marks. Thus in a palace scene pillars may separate the scenes and gateways, rocks, etc. may mark the end of one act and the beginning of the next one. A detailed study of Mahaa-janaka Jaataka in Cave 1 would explain this.

Rothenstein.... has justly made the point that in China and Japan, Buddhism was a foreign religion, and Buddhist art came to these countries with a heretic tradition already fixed and formulated. (But) to these Ajanta artists the Buddha and his disciples were Indians, men of their own race; they did not need to make translation from foreign to familiar terms, as did the medieval European painters in depicting the Gospel stories, in order to make the scenes intelligible ..... If you come to think of it, nearly all the religious art .... deals with far-off events, circumstanced in surrounding neither known nor understood except through books.

-Lawrence Binyon in the Introduction to the Mukul Dey's My Pilgrimage to Ajanta and Bagh, 1973.

INDIAN RELIGIOUS ART, BUDDHIST, JAINA OR HINDU, ARE THE ONLY TRULY INDIGENOUS ARTs justly made the point thaA

The technique of Indian wall painting, which was followed with little change in all later examples of mural decoration in Asia, is completely different from that of the Western fresco. Fresco, more correctly fresco buono, in its true sense implies the application of colours to a layer of moist lime plaster. Indian and all Asian murals were painted on an almost dry wall, called fresco secco.

It might have taken centuries for the Indian artist to develop the technique of preparing the wall for painting, and also to select suitable pigments with an appropriate binder, though no trace of such experimentation has been found. The importance of these may be seen from the fact that the Ajanta paintings have withstood the ravages of time with remarkable resilience.

However, from the outlines of some incomplete paintings in certain caves, like Cave 26, we get to know the painting sequence followed by the Ajanta artist.

"A buffalo-skin was boiled in water until it became soft. Sticks were then made of the paste and dried in the sunshine. It is stated that if colour is mixed with this hard plaster, called vajralepa, it makes it fast, and if white mud is mixed with it, it serves as perfect medium for coating walls. Vajra-lepa coating was usually done in three layers over a plaster which consisted of powdered brick, burnt conches and sand, mixed with a liquid preparation of molasses and drops of a decoction of mudga (Phaseolus munga). To this a quantity of mashed ripe bananas or tree resins and the pulp of bilva fruit (Aegle marmelos) was also added. After the mixture had dried it was again ground down and mixed with molasses and water until it became soft. The wall surface was washed down with water until it was thoroughly clean and then the plaster was applied with a spoon. This was the two-fold process by which the wall was prepared for painting."

The caves of Ajanta offer an instructive field for the study of the evolution of rock-cut architecture or cave architecture. Their structural qualities, like their sculpture, seldom receive due attention, which is focused more on the world-famous paintings that they bear.

It was in northern India that the great Buddhist emperor Ashoka initiated the cave architecture. But, it was in the middle part of India, called the Deccan, that it reached a level of sophistication unattained in the north.

Cave architecture is unique in the sense that it can be viewed as an enterprise of a sculptor. The Ajanta architect-sculptor showed his capability not only in carving a human or animal figure but something far grander and more monumental.

The cave architecture, at Ajanta and elsewhere, betrays the strong influence of wooden construction so as to create an illusion of building in wood. This, and other aspects, lead us to believe that the architect-sculptors were probably drawn from the profession of carpenters, with goldsmiths and ivory-carvers joining hands with the sculptors.

The evolution of rock-architecture, both chaitya-s and vihaara-s, follows the same pattern in this region and can be made to fall into two periods: the Hinaayana period of the pre-Christian era and the later Mahaayana period.

During the first phase, that is, the Hinaayana period of 2nd century BC to 2nd Century AD the sculptural activity was limited. Sculptures of this period can also be found in Pithalkhora caves in Aurangabad district and in the more famous Sanchi stoopa-s situated near Bhopal, capital of Madhya Pradesh. A continuity of this tradition can be seen in the caves of Bhaja (Pune district) and those at Kanheri, very close to Borivli, a suburb of Mumbai, and later at Amaravathi, near Vijayawada and Nagarjunakonda, on the banks of the river Krishna in Andhra Pradesh.

The second phase began in the late 5th century AD. Once a beginning had been made, side by side with the excavation of new caves the existing Hinaayana ones were suitably modified. The ornamental facade of Cave 10 belongs to this period.

A VIEW OF THE CAVES, THE FACADE of Cave 19 and the CHAITYA INTERIOR of Cave 19, INTERIOR VIEW of Cave 1 and A VIEW OF THE MAIN HALL of Cave 17 are examples of just such cave architecture.

Ajanta paintings were done over a period of eight centuries, from the 2nd century BC to the 6th century AD. During this period there have been many changes - changes in taste, manner, and style. The history of these changes in style and manner is, in fact, the history of art. Indeed it is interesting asking what a particular painting represents. For instance, in sculpture there have been images of the Buddha or the goddess Parvati depicted century after century; and once you know that an image represents the Buddha or the goddess Parvati, you have learned or understood very little. They are made so vastly different in every century, that you will be missing all the beauty and fascination if you are only interested in the theme, in the subject matter of a work of art. It is fascinating to follow the change in taste and, thus, the development of style. For each generation expresses in works of art its own concept of beauty, and rejects the ideals of the past. Sometimes simplicity is emphasised; at other times profuse and lavish ornamentation is in fashion. During one period painters prefer studied dignity and what they call 'good taste'; during others they like rich decoration so much that every inch of a given wall is filled with endless and delicate ornamentation. This is style - style that is different in every age and always changes.

By sheer chance, the development of style from the beginning to its final decline can be witnessed within the physical limits of Ajanta. In this respect Ajanta has no parallel.

Probably belonging to the same period, the painting of SOME FOREIGNERS from Cave 1 is a glaring example of the change of style and execution. Both the drawing and finish are degenerate and when compared with the other paintings of the same cave it appears clumsy and bare. Viewed critically, we notice that the refinements that are present in the others are non-existent here; gone are the shapely palms and sensitive fingers. The entire portrayal is rather stiff and simplified, having nothing in common with the linear expression of soft curves typical of Ajanta.

In the development of any art form, the initial stage is generally called archaic or in simple terms, primitive. This phase is characterised by rigid and lifeless portrayals. The artists are yet to understand true representation of movement.

It seems that the earliest existing examples of Ajanta painting, of the 2nd-1st century BC in the Caves 9 and 10 cannot be classified as archaic in the strictest sense of the word. They have already entered the transitional period that was to carry them on to the classical phase. This is evident from the subtle, beautifully drawn sketches that effectively portray the poses and gestures of the human figure and the grandeur of trees and flowers.

An expression of this period can be seen in Caves 9 and 10. The narration of Shyaama Jaataka in Cave 9, RAJA WITH HIS RETINUE in Cave 10 and what remains of the narration of SHAD-DANTA JAATAKA of Cave 10 are the few survivors of time, neglect and vandalism.

By the 4th and 5th centuries, AD, art in India reached a stage that must be called classical. The classic style means not only perfect mastery of the subject, but turning all this skill and knowledge to the noble purpose of creating beauty: everything is idealised, realism is only known and used in order to create things of beauty and perfection. There is a dignity and nobility in classical art that allows no exaggeration, no excess, no overstatement, no dramatisation.

Simhala Avadana in Cave 17, DYING PRINCE of Cave 16, DESCENT OF INDRA of Cave 17 and VOTARIES WITH OFFERINGS, certainly represent the classical peak of Ajanta painting, breathing peace, harmony, and balance. Agitation, overcrowding, too much movement and passion are all aliens to the classical art of this period.

The word `classic' is advisedly used with reference to the Ajanta murals because they occupy the same position in relation to all later developments in the Buddhist art of Asia, as do the marbles of the Parthenon to the history of Western art. The styles of Buddhist painting in Tibet and Nepal, Central Asia, China, and ultimately Japan all have their beginnings in the classical mode represented by this series of murals.

By the middle of the 6th century, the style turned baroque, a style of over-ornamentation and exaggeration.

The broad vision is progressively dissected into compartments so that the narrative is painted, not in an idyllic landscape, but in a maze of pillars in royal pavilions, palace windows, porches, balconies, city walls, shops and monasteries.

The eye-slits are stretched out of all proportion to the face, men look effeminate and women exaggeratedly feminine, with bulging curves and rounded breasts. Both men and women wear excessive ornament.

The affected, grossly exaggerated attitudes of the characters who partake in the Mahaa-janaka Jaataka (Cave 1), for example, one of its scene QUEEN CAPTIVATES MAHAA-JANAKA, and in certain scenes of Vidhurapandita Jaataka (Cave 2) are examples of this style.

Artistic standards were in the decline from the end of the 6th century; mercifully this phase did not last long, for the Ajanta caves were abandoned forever, for reasons unknown.

Take the case of QUEEN IN A PALACE SCENE in Cave 1. The classical poses and decorative elements are exaggerated with heavy heads, elongated eyes, excessively large mouths, thin legs, and superfluous hand gestures. In addition the composition is too crowded. The entire execution becomes careless, perhaps because of mass production, and the finish of the paintings was summary.

The painting presenting TWO FIGURES OF THE BUDDHA in teaching attitude in Cave 17 with disciples kneeling with folded hands is a specimen of this period of decadence. These are only two of many similar Buddhas that we find enclosed in separate cubicles, horizontally covering the whole of the upper panels of Cave 10. Compared with the other Buddhas on the walls of Ajanta painted earlier, they lack refinement and finish. The colours are naive, flat and unnuanced and the lines wanting in feeling.

THOUSAND BUDDHAS a scene from the Miracle of Shraavasti is obviously a product of this period.

By the end of the 5th century, there are signs that the Indian artist has made a change of style. These signs are at first minute departures from the purely classical sentiment, and a very hesitant deviation from the classical concepts of beauty and idealism. This is the period of mannerism.

During this initial period of mannerism, Ajanta produced some of the finest examples of the sacred image; BUDDHA PREACHES TO THE CONGREGATION of Cave 17, and the incomparable BODHI-SATTVA PADMA-PAANI and BODHI-SATTVA AVALOKITESHVARA both painted in Cave 1. The narration of Vidhura-pandita Jaataka in Cave 2 belongs to the period of transition from classicism to mannerism, for in this composition we find elements of both.

During this period one can notice that the differences have been narrowed in the portrayal of the sacred and the secular as compared to the earlier classical phase.

In all of these Afghan examples, the Indian style of drawing has become somewhat hard and wiry, and the soft shading of Ajanta has been conventionalised into a hard reinforcement of the contours in bands of darker pigments.

The eastward diffusion of the Ajanta style at sites like Kyzil and Turfan is marked by a gradual reduction of the Indian mode to a hard and wiry linear manner. Properly speaking the Central Asian style of these sites is probably to be explained as a fusion of Indian and Iranian elements. A shadow of the classic Indian tradition persists in the 6th and 7th century caves as the famous site Tun Huang in westernmost China, where in many instances Indian types and iconography are combined with the indigenous Chinese technique of definition in essentially linear terms.

The establishment of close political and religious ties between the T'ang Empire and India in the 7th and 8th centuries led to a much closer imitation of Indian models in the Far East. The annals of Chinese painting are filled with mentions of Chinese artists who were reputed to have introduced a style of painting in the Indian manner, which gave an illusion of relief through shading. A few fragments of painting from this period survive in Japan to indicate the spread of the Indian style to the Far East. For example, the paintings at the base of the Tachibana shrine at Horyu-ji in Nara are done in an Indo-Central Asian manner, seemingly transported to Japan untouched by Chinese influence.

The figures of new-born souls seated on lotus flowers, both in pose and the use of heavy shading, are so like some of the figures in the Ajanta wall-paintings that they might almost have been painted by the same hand.

Similar parallels between Ajanta and the Japanese art of the 8th century are to be found in the famous wallpaintings in the Horyu-ji Kondo which were destroyed in 1948. These scattered fragments are all that survive to illustrate what must have been a near universal influence of Indian ideals in the Far East in the 7th and 8th centuries. The persistence of these canons in even later periods, however modified by native traditions, serve to indicate how the classical style of the Ajanta wall paintings moulded the destiny of all later styles of religious art in Asia. This is similar to how the classical styles of Greece and Rome affected the entire later development of art in the Western world.

## G::"@+&::":?>:>5++E&9@EEE:D@_D>:GEECECEK:\EDF]BDA<FFBGKGB7@GKCK\KGFG]Kh<ac]KKch]GKFhC]cnKKKaK\ccKaK

,+>:+::+9?-:1?:+E?:v]?.:E:>]E:<eEDGGGG]CYchddcGfbn\c:@FC:<KaK\aGBaF-GaBdanhcacGhcGKnGcB]EcChGKchahkh_aK@K

W:4(/I7I!9I33:774OUb(()33:/)744937F3>48<4I7FII3W7<\OOtOWIF6K=9FILFWWbmFIXXFtFWmLUe^OVIBIFRXKFIWOObWL4F>7FFOdlRWKOXROOFFV=F=I<I7<4V4LrbuXm

Principles of Painting - Shading Techniques producing Depth and Relief - Dance and

Painting - Rendering Parts of the Body - Religious Motifs in the later Period - Portra yal of

Archaic or Pre-Classical Style (2nd-1st Centuries, BC) - Classical Period (4th-5th Century, AD) -

During the first phase, that is, the Hinaayana phase, the Buddha was not shown in the human form, but only in symbols, such as the Wheel, the Bodhi Tree and the Footprints of the Buddha. But during the Mahaayana period sculptures and paintings of the Buddha and the Bodhi-sattva-s were added.

The figure-sculpture of Ajanta belongs to the great art-tradition of contemporary India. Sculpture, mostly dating from the 5th and 6th centuries AD, is remarkable for its classicism marked by qualities like grace, elegance, restraint and serenity.

The NAAGA COUPLE in Cave 19 and the vidyadhara-s (flying couple) in Cave 16 illustrate this. The giant reclining figure depicting the Final Extinction of the Master called MAHAA-PARI-NIRVAANA in Cave 26 is a masterpiece.

The spiritual luminosity of the contemporary images of the Buddha is, however, wanting as the general character of the sculpture tends towards a certain heaviness of form.

It may be mentioned here that every one of the sculpture was plastered and painted, but most of this protective layer is now lost.
