And finally, when he announces his decision to renounce, he has cast off every-thing including the sacred-thread, excepting the close fitting beads. Is the removal of the sacred thread suggestive of the impending sanyaasa?

In the first scene the king has a vacant look reflecting his mental conflict and his hands showing his inner pain. While sitting before the sage his whole deportment is one of humility and utter surrender, his hands folded in prayer. But in the last scene his visage is serene and full of compassion and love, but of firm determination. His hands compose dharma chakra mudra (preaching-attitude) to show that he has now found the path.

The change in the attitude of Shivaali is even more pronounced in the two scenes that she depicted in, when pleading with the king in the first scene and when being informed of the king's renunciation. From a conceited queen, 'dressed nakedness' determined to win over her husband in the earlier scene, she is now a well-draped lady sitting before the king "erect like a candle undisturbed by air" ready to receive the heart-breaking news.

It is interesting also to note that the continuity in dress and physical features of the characters is maintained, while bringing out the contrast in the expressions of the characters.

Musicians playing musical instruments have been shown many scenes in Ajanta. A variety of musical instruments have been depicted, both in paintings and in sculpture. These cover the period from the 2nd century, BC to the about 6th century, AD. So Ajanta offers scope for study of development of the musical heritage of India. We can see both the continuity and change over the period as presented in Ajanta itself.

First let us compare the musicians present in the two scenes in this jaataka. The accompanying musicians of the dancer in the earlier scene were cymbal-players, flautists, drummers and a musician playing a string-instrument. We find from this painting and also elsewhere in Ajanta that side-flutes were in vogue even during this period. It may be mentioned that side-flutes came into use in the West only in the 14th century. In the same way cymbals continue to keep time measure in dance performances even today. The bell-shaped drum with a narrow ring for a grip looks more like some of the drums used in folk music. The standing drums are no more found in India. We are not able to make a judgement on the string-instrument as this part of the painting is somewhat mutilated.

A slightly different group of musicians accompany the king when he abdicates. The musician blowing a conch-shell is shown with puffed cheeks and popping eyes. Conch is still being used for heralding the arrival of important people like the king or during the ceremonial procession of deities and pooja-s in temples and houses. The mridangam (or pakhawaj) player is shown very realistically in the midst of executing a complex rhythmic pattern with both the hands. His instrument and playing technique are the same as those used today. The playing posture as well as fingering of the flute player look natural, and we now realise an unbroken musical tradition of not less than 15 centuries.

We can witness another set of musicians in the composition DESCENT OF INDRA in Cave 17. In the BODHI-SATTVA PADMA-PAANI PANEL in this cave itself we come across the kinnara-s playing musical instruments, one of the instruments resembling the modern mandolin.

The dancer in the first act striking a graceful pose of tribhanga was already mentioned; her dress and ornaments were described in detail. Dancers have been portrayed also in other scenes in Ajanta. For example, DANCER IN THE NAGA-COURT in this cave is one, where the principal dancer is drawn matching the expertness of the dancer in this story, though not as elaborately. Fortunately we have a composition depicting dancers accompanied by musicians belonging to the earlier period, namely of the 2nd-1st century, BC in the DANCERS AND MUSICIANS painted in Cave 10, though badly damaged. One can feel the unaffected gay abandon in the Hinaayana masterpiece, as against the Mahaayana painting which is more stylised. The scanty clothing and the few ornaments, such as bangles of conch-shell modelled on traditional folk ornaments of the pre-Christian era painting is in contrast to the beautifully designed garments, the variety of glittering trinkets, and bewildering coiffure of the dancer in the Mahaa-janaka court. The costumes, the hair styles, the shape of the eyes and the general atmosphere all epitomise the final phase in the development of the Ajanta style of painting.

We shall consider the various techniques used in Ajanta to produce an illusion of the third dimension. Perspective is a technique of drawing objects on a surface giving an illusion of distance and solidity. In this the farther objects look small. This may be called true perspective or normal perspective. Another way is the reverse perspective. The building in front of which the dancer is performing with her orchestra is not shown in the true perspective, but is drawn in the reverse perspective. This cannot be ascribed to the Ajanta artist's carelessness or to his lack of knowledge of drawing in perspective, for he has executed some remarkable paintings in true perspective. The paintings showing GATEWAY TO BRAHMA-DATTA'S PALACE in Cave 2 and the MONASTERY, a scene from Shibi Jaataka of Cave 17 are excellent examples for true perspective. We should imagine, then, the artist composed the scene to suite the theme rather than exhibiting realism. We come across with reverse perspective in the depiction of Vidhura-pandita Jaataka of Cave 2. We will have some more to say on the technique of showing distances while explaining LUSTRATION AND RENUNCIATION scene in the same cave.

Another method of creating an illusion of the third dimension is producing depth and relief while portraying characters. One of the methods used for this is called ujjotana (adding-highlights).

In the execution of the figure of Mahaa-janaka and other characters, the artist has made use of the ujjotana technique, that is, by adding white patches appearing on the chin, breasts, arms, legs or wherever an elevation of form was desired. This method is different from vartana (shading). Adding to this was the use of blue colour, especially for the background to create an illusion of depth in contrast to the warm red and brown colours.

A noteworthy feature of Ajanta is the expert draughtsmanship. The perfection of painting technique is displayed in the extraordinary breadth and confidence of draughtsmanship - the varying thickness of line drawn with a free flowing sweep of the brush to depict the oval faces, arched eyebrows, aquiline noses, and fine sensitive lips. The lines have become darker to serve as a suitable accessory to the vartana (shading) and ujjotana (adding-highlights) techniques for creating an illusion of depth. The visual depth is enhanced by the judicious use of blue - of lapis lazuli - by contrasting it with the warmth of the red and brown tones. The illusion of spaciousness is also evident where the reddish ochre, yellow ochre and dark brown tints applied to the dancing girl and her imposing costume stand out against a plain bluish-green background. white patches appeari&

This draughtsmanship is evident in the variety of body positions of the characters portrayed. An important feature of the Ajanta style is the consummate skill in the use of variety of sthaana-s (body positions) while portraying characters in Ajanta. In all the scenes depicted in this narration, every one of the women is shown in different positions - from the rijvagata (frontal) to parshvagata (strict profile) to anriju (back view).

The Ajanta artists followed certain conventions in separating scenes. A gate is often placed to herald the beginning of an act. In this panel, we find the king on an elephant starts a new act. One will not fail to notice that the king passes through the gate once more in this narration, that is, when finally MAHAA-JANAKA LEAVES THE PALACE after abdication. Within the first Act the pillars of the pavilion separates the first scene from the following one of dance performance. So is the case in the last act.

Ajanta, as said before, is a treasure house to study the contemporary fashion in textiles, jewellery, etc. As an example, the chamara-bearing girl sports an upper-garment with rows of geese printed on it - a fashion referred to in the contemporary literature. Note the architectural details of the palace in this as well as in a later scene. This is characteristic of the baroque period - too many pillars and rather excessive decoration on the pavilion.

In the central pavilion is shown the PREPARATION FOR THE CEREMONY. A lady with a tray displays movement so free and poise so graceful that its equal is hard to find. This lady, as well as the other one in the scene, is dressed in garments that are almost transparent or just nude. Also found in this central panel are two more persons: an old servant leaning on his staff and another carrying a pitcher on his head. The dark complexion of the lady with a tray is beautifully balanced by the red column towards her left and with the fair complexion of the other woman.

BODHI-SATTVA PADMA-PAANI, the central figure of this panel, is the most popular painting of Ajanta and no collection of Ajanta painting can be complete without this masterpiece. This panel is painted on the back wall, to the left of the antechamber, symmetrical with the BODHI-SATTVA AVALOKITESHVARA panel on the other side of the antechamber.

The painting belongs to the post-classical period of mannerism, to be followed by baroque ornamentation deviating from the classical idealism, as may be seen in the narration of Mahaa-janaka Jaataka in this cave. Known for its brilliant composition, this panel compels the beholder's eye towards it and one is struck by the general ensemble and unity of the theme. The remarkable fact is that the massive proportions of the figure of Bodhi-sattva Padma-paani - it measures 1.8 metres across the shoulders - in no way detracts from the harmonious composition. The panel may be studied making use of the line sketch BODHI-SATTVA PADMA-PAANI PANEL. BODHI-SATTVA PADMA-PAANI, PADMA-PAANI'S CONSORT shown on his left, the MITHUNA FIGURES depicted on the left above the main figure, and, finally, the background itself are the details in colour.t

BODHI-SATTVA PADMA-PAANI is a personification of apparently contradictory qualities of perfect compassion and perfect indifference, both existing simultaneously. Out of compassion for the countless creatures in the cosmos, he renounced the pursuit of Buddha-hood and nirvaana, so that he might continue preaching the Buddhist doctrine until the last being in the cosmos received enlightenment and thus was released from the chain of rebirths.

On the other hand, this dispassionate Bodhi-sattva has, paradoxically enough, all the qualities that youth, high birth, noble lineage and religious ardour can produce: strong masculine limbs, a broad chest, a well-set neck, a high philosopher's brow, large meditative eyes and firm lips. The supple body is full of vigour, but the plastic grace is almost feminine and at the same time detached from the distracting environment. The Bodhi-sattva, lambent with the glow of wisdom, and marked out for the spirituality of Buddhahood wears a rich diadem on his forehead and rows of necklaces adorning his neck. Through the extraordinary confidence of the line and the softness of the shading the countenance reflects a lyrical and pensive abstraction. The flawless, opalescent smoothness of the skin, the beautiful proportion of body to face, and the eyes half-closed in meditation radiate a celestial beauty and purity. The textures and muscular structure are scarcely indicated so that the figures symbolize the divine anatomy of supernatural beings.

Thus, the Padma-paani figure is the finest example of the realisation of the classical ideal through symbolism based on animals and plants, as laid down in the shastra-s. Usually the limbs of the cult image are related to the shapes and forms of the vegetal and animal worlds. For example, the eyes of the Bodhi-sattva are likened to lotus petals, the curving brow-line to that of the arching bow and the ovoidal face is supposed to resemble an egg. Further, his shoulders and torso are compared with the domed head of an elephant and the body of a lion respectively.

During this period a new technique of luminosity called chaaya-tapa (shade-and- shine) was introduced. A soft glow that can be seen on the outline of the body against the slightly darker background. This technique has succeeded in casting that unreal, mystical light which makes the sacred countenances of the Buddhas and Bodhi-sattvas radiate with a mysterious glow.

The illusion of ethereal light gently thrown on the Bodhi-sattva from his left produces a soft shadow on the right side of the face and neck. But unlike normal chiaroscuro, the left side of the face is also treated with a deeper brown tone than the softly highlighted right cheek and nose. In conformity with the Ajanta tradition, there are no cast shadows. These are scrupulously shunned by the artists who realised, as did Leonardo da Vinci in his time, that direct light and cast shadows destroy the representation of true relief or volume.Menu/Sampler2/ActualCoColor

The luminosity is further enhanced by the masterly handling of blue, lapis lazuli, sparingly employed on the necklace beads and delicately applied on the fringes of the fresh lotus. This is reflected in the warmth of the golden brown and pearl grey of the Bodhi-sattva's complexion. At the same time, perspective is created in the background by the relative depth in space of the blue jacket of the woman behind the Padma-paani's left arm, and of the blue birds in the conventional bands of hills in the top left-hand corner of the picture.

To the left of the Bodhi-sattvaa is painted PADMA-PAANI'S CONSORT. She is holding a lotus to indicate that she is his consort. She is one of the most graceful women of Ajanta, exuding serenity and love. Also another instance of 'black is beautiful'!

On the left part of the panel are the MITHUNA FIGURES watching the Great Being from their hill resort as they sit in an amorous mood, exhibiting an incredible bhaava-madhura (tender feeling) of reciprocal love. The elegance of the contentment and modesty of the figures is stressed more by the almost symbolic tenderness of the soft caress, than from the facial expressions.

A number of mithuna figures have been painted below the eight Buddhas in Cave 17. This painting may be compared with PRINCE AND HIS CONSORT a scene from Shibi Jaataka which depicts the same subject in the same cave, but provides a complete contrast in the manner of drawing and colouring.

PRINCE AND HIS CONSORT is a good example of the animnonnata (flat) style of shading consisting of flat irregular planes of primary colours. But MITHUNA FIGURES is done using the nimnonnata (high relief) treatment of painting, retaining the softness and roundness of the limbs, demarcated only by a slight deepening of the colours along the edges.

The restraint of the sacred style of painting in the Bodhi-sattvas is beautifully balanced by the lively background and the warm atmosphere of the secular scene. The background is infused with the colours of the luxuriant foliage of the areca palms and the ashoka trees, the strong red squares like pillars and cross-pieces which are a convention for the ridges of hills, the clear blue of the bird's feathers, and a variety of other colours which enliven the drapery and jewellery and even the skin-tones of the men and women.

There is a female chaawri-bearer wearing a long blue coat, while on the right is a dark mace-bearer in a long white coat. In the background monkeys are seen frolicking about and a pair of peacocks shrilling in joy. In the left corner is a KINNARA PLAYING THE LUTE quite similar to the present day mandolin, and cymbals, giving us an idea of the kind of musical instruments used at that time. We come across musicians and contemporary musical instruments in the scenes of DANCING GIRL WITH MUSICIANS and MAHAA-JANAKA LEAVES THE PALACE the narration of Mahaa-janaka Jaataka of Cave 1 and in the painting DESCENT OF INDRA in Cave 17.

Painted on the left wall of the ante-chamber, this panel, called the TEMPTATIONS OF THE BUDDHA, shows the assault on Gautama by Maara's army and the amorous attempts by Maara's daughters to deflect him from the path of Enlightenment.

The beauty of this painting and its artistic quality compare with the classic composition Simhala Avadaana depicted in Cave 17. Here too the artist's vision is projected over an entire wall, and the activities of Maara, the Buddhist devil, are interpreted with a vigour and liveliness which has few parallels.

The Episode: Gautama sat under the Bodhi tree in deep meditation in order to attain enlightenment when Maara, the Evil Spirit, approached him. Maara made many attempts to dislodge the Master from his resolve lest he should achieve the supreme moment and open up the path of salvation for humankind. Having scared away hosts of gods who were waiting for the great moment, the spirit of sensual pleasure and worldly passion at first tried to persuade the Bodhi-sattva to abandon his quest by tempting him with the desire for power and glory. For days Gautama withstood temptations of all kinds. Having failed, Maara, disguised as a messenger falsely told the would-be Buddha that his wicked cousin Devadatta had rebelled, thrown his father into prison and seized his wife Yashodara. It was in vain.

He then sent his three most beautiful daughters called Lust, Thirst and Delight to disport themselves before the sage. They danced and sang and contrived every means of seduction; but Gautama rose above their wiles. Thereupon Maara summoned his demons and made a furious and appalling assault with whirlwind, tempest, flood and earthquake to dislodge Gautama. But Siddhartha, calm and unmoved, sat under the Bodhi tree. The frustrated Maara then challenged His right to sit on the spot and even demanded evidence of his goodness and benevolence. The Master called upon the Earth to bear witness to his right by virtue of his acts in previous existences. This he did by touching the ground with his hand, and the Earth spoke with a voice of thunder: "I am his witness." Maara, defeated, fled in dismay; and it was in the course of the succeeding night that Siddhartha passed from the state of being a Bodhi-sattva to complete and perfect Buddhahood.

In the following discussion you may refer to the line sketch MAARA'S EPISODE, and the pictures in colours TEMPTATIONS OF THE BUDDHA, DAUGHTER OF MAARA and MAARA'S ATTENDANT which are details of the composition.

The Buddha seated in the vajraasana (adamantine-posture) in bhoomi sparsha mudra (earth-touching-attitude), serene and calm, is painted in the centre of the wall, and He is surrounded by a fantastic variety of monsters and witches.

Around Him there is a group of seven young damsels whose pretty features, graceful poses and soft facial expressions have been justly compared to the women in Raphael's paintings. Especially beautiful are the two women one on either side of the Buddha. They are apparently two of the three enchanting daughters Of Maara, Rati (lust), Trishna (thirst) and Arati (delight) who unsuccessfully tried every means of seduction. A DAUGHTER OF MAARA is shown as an example. They have been painted in the mould of the classical beauty of Ajanta, well qualified to do their appointed task of seduction - full breasts, pouting lips, round eye-balls and ear-rings.

If Maara's daughters are personifications of seductive beauty, his army is a caricature of the horrible and detestable. MAARA'S ATTENDANT, a part of Maara's army is painted to the right of the Supreme One beyond Maara's daughter and is an interesting study. The artist has employed deep contrasts in tone in the face to bring out the grotesque and banal facet of his mission. The serio-comic effect is principally created by placing the clumsy face in the shadow, accentuated by a few clownish spots of highlight on the chin, lower and upper lip, the nose and the two ungainly earrings. At the same time, the lighter areas balance the paleness of the skull and the band of the attendant's headgear. The result is one of ridicule.

In the execution of the characters of this drama, the artist has exhibited his skill in the use of highlighting, called ujjotana (adding-highlights), appearing on the chin, breasts, arms, legs or wherever an elevation of form was desired.

The theme of this unsuccessful venture of Maara and the Master's conquest of temptations is a favourite subject-matter of Buddhist artists. A master-piece depicting the same scene but in relief in stone, TEMPTATIONS OF THE BUDDHA - SCULPTURE, can be found in Cave 26. One is at a loss to decide who is superior - the master-artist with the brush or the one with the chisel and hammer. May be the sculptor has a slight edge over his brother-artist !

As in the composition of the BODHI-SATTVA PADMA-PAANI PANEL on the left side of the wall leading to the ante-chamber, is the painting BODHI-SATTVA AVALOKITESHVARA PANEL. In it we find, overwhelming his rich surroundings by his personal splendour, stands the BODHI-SATTVA AVALOKITESHVARA.

On the top left near a gate is a standing couple absorbed in religious conversation, as is apparent from the mudra of the right hand of the male figure. A dark lady of singular charm, commonly known as SHAKTI PANDARA or Dark Princess in the left lower corner, accepts lilies offered by another lady. Above this is a royal figure making an offering of lilies to the Bodhi-sattva. Though richly attired, his glory fades into insignificance in the presence of the divine Bodhi-sattva.

BODHI-SATTVA AVALOKITESHVARA, earlier identified as Bodhi-sattva Vajrapani, is regarded as having emanated from Amitabha and his Shakti Pandara. He is the most esteemed Bodhi-sattva of the Mahaayana pantheon, being styled sangha-ratna (Jewel of the Order).

He poses with a Divine Buddha on the crest of his immense bejewelled diadem. Bodhi-sattvas are identified by just such a Buddha-sign on the crest. His dark complexion points to his southern origin.

The elaborate jewellery of the Bodhi-sattva reflects southern customs and fashions. On the other hand the northern influence is still predominant in BODHI-SATTVA PADMA-PAANI, which has Aryan features and a fair complexion. The dark skin, round face and fuller lips that were prominent in the Ajanta paintings of the pre-Christian era reappear in the Deccani-type ethnic structure of the BODHI-SATTVA AVALOKITESHVARA.

In the drawing and colouring the artists seem to have used similar techniques as were used in the Padma-paani panel. There is, however, a greater relief effect in the drawing of the Avalokiteshvara, chiefly because of the larger area of greenish background to the figure.

SHAKTI PANDARA, the lady on the right of the pair shown in the bottom-left corner in the panel, accepting lilies from the other lady, is one of the most famous of Ajanta paintings at Ajanta. In the opinion of Yazdani it is one of the finest works of art in the world. Popularly known as the Dark Princess, it is probably a representation of Amitabha's Shakti Pandara.

Its fine modelling, exquisite ornamentation, tranquillity and thoughtfulness are beautifully brought out by the dark complexion against a lighter background. Certainly there is no other portrait in Ajanta with such perfect eyes; the hazel-brown of the pupils and the red spots in the corners of the eyes radiate an astonishing natural friendliness. It is truly an unusual painting, considering the late period to which it belongs, when the elongated and exaggerated eyes of a declining art were already in vogue.

It may be recalled that in an earlier discussion it was pointed out that most of the beautiful women of Ajanta are depicted as black beauties and this detail was taken as an example. Note that BODHI-SATTVA AVALOKITESHVARA depicted in this panel is also of dark complexion and this was attributed to the southern influence.

The story of Champeyya is painted on the wall of the rear verandah, left of Bodhi-sattva Avalokiteshvara above the doors of the two cells.

The Jaataka: The Bodhi-sattva was once born as Champeyya, the naaga king. He felt unhappy with his wasteful life, and attempted to take his life. His wife Sumana helped to revive his interest in life and was his constant companion. Once he was caught by a cruel Brahmin, and taught him dancing. The Brahmin made the serpent-king dance before King Uggasena. Meanwhile, Sumana, who was searching for her lost husband, finally found her husband in the court of King Uggasena entertaining the king with his dance. On her plea the king released the serpent from captivity. It is then that King Uggasena came to know that the snake was none other than Bodhi-sattva born as the naaga king. Bodhi-sattva took King Uggasena to the naaga-world, where he gave him a sermon. Later he helped the king to vanquish his enemies.

The artist has delineated the story in five scenes, arranged in two rows. The top row depicts three scenes and on the bottom row the remaining two scenes. The entire jaataka is given in the sketch STORY OF CHAMPEYYA, which may be referred to while following the individual scenes.

In the first scene, CHAMPEYYA REPENTS HIS LIFE OF PLEASURES shown at the extreme left of the top row, we see the Bodhi-sattva, born as naaga-king, Champeyya sitting on a throne repenting of his life of pleasures. Sumana, his queen is sitting by his side and is looking up eagerly towards her husband. As usual, the court dwarf is in attendance, this time holding a tray of fruits, flowers and cosmetics, but the way he looks up eagerly at the king shows his concern. There are two more maids-in-attendance in this scene.

To watch the next scene, KING UGGASENA WATCHES SNAKE-CHARMER'S TRICKS we must go to the extreme right. The snake-charmer, we find, is on the extreme right. His face is damaged. The serpent is in front of him, uncoiling out of the basket. King Uggasena, with his head completely missing, is seen seated on a throne. The folds of his upper garments are artistically drawn and as usual the bolster at his back has interesting motifs on it. With his right hand he has made a gesture, expressive of wonder. His minister is sitting on his left. The serpent-king, Champeyya, while showing his dances, suddenly sees something that makes him stop midway in his dance.

As a part of the second scene we find some courtiers also watching the snake show. Among the COURTIERS WATCH THE SNAKE-DANCE there are two very interesting figures, whose racial traits and dress are very characteristic. The features of one of them, the moustache, and the tuft of hair on the crown of his head, are so typical that even today he might be taken for a citizen of Varanasi. His upper-cloth is of silver brocade, with floral patterns of silk worked on it, is reminiscent of the dress and the famous brocade industry of the place.

The features of the other figure are like those of a Brahmin of Orissa. The style of his hair and the manner in which he has thrown a part of his dhoti across his shoulder are also characteristic of the Orissa people. The poses of the figures are extremely interesting, particularly the homely way in which the Oriya is sitting and looking at his Varanasi comrade.

Between the two panels described above, we have the APPEARANCE OF SUMANA AND HER CHILD. A mother with her child is seen appealing to a doorkeeper for admission to the court. This, in fact, was the reason why the naaga-king had halted his dance, for he suddenly recognises them as his own wife and son.

The part of the story of the king's recognition of the serpent as the naaga-king and the reason why he came to such a pitiable state has not been painted in this panel. The remaining story is painted in the bottom row.

Moving right, we come to a royal hall full of ladies and attendants, where CHAMPEYYA GIVES A SERMON initiating Uggasena into the mysteries of the faith. Of the female figures, the most charming is that of Queen Sumana, standing above the left shoulder of Champeyya having a serpent-hood over her head. Her features are exquisite and her face has a delicate lambent grace.

There are as many as seven maids-in-attendance in different poses and attitudes. The THREE MAIDS LISTENING TO THE SERMON are typical Ajanta beauties in graceful poses. One of the three male figures is our inexorable dwarf. The dwarf has raised his eyebrows to show his indifference to the serious sermons of the Bodhi-sattva.

The second male figure is that of an old servant standing on the right of Uggasena near the pillar. Here again we find the artist in a lighter vein, for the servant is on the point of helping himself to some fruit from a tray held by a girl who is too rapt in listening to the sermon to notice the theft. Another girl appears to be apprehensive of the mischief and without turning her head is glaring at the thief out of the corner of her eyes. The third male figure is that of the naaga-King's bodyguard. Dressed in a long silk-coat with flowers interwoven in it and with tight sleeves, he is armed with a sword having a jewelled handle suggesting that he is the king's bodyguard. He has a cap on his head with a round brim and peaked top. Most likely, he is a foreigner. The scene also illustrates the court-architecture.es

The Ajanta artist has a typical sense of humour. He portrays incidences in a lighter vein in rather serious situations. One instance was referred to in this narration. There are a few more of examples. In Cave 2, a humorous scene is sculpted under the figure of Hariti. On a pedestal we find a group of five boys goading two rams to fight, a common mischief of the ubiquitous backbenchers and truants even today!

The mural commonly known as QUEEN IN A PALACE SCENE is painted on the right hand wall of the main hall. The painting has not yet been identified. A line sketch QUEEN IN A PALACE in addition the colour reproduction QUEEN IN A PALACE SCENE may referred to while following the discussion.A queen rests on a cushion surrounded by her retinue and converses with the king. These figures are somewhat disproportionate and are disposed in a mechanical way. They represent a decline in artistic standards, marked by anatomical distortions. Compared with the fifth century paintings, the classical poses and decorative elements are exaggerated. The peculiarities of the style are the heavy heads, elongated eyes, excessively large mouths, thin legs, and the superfluous hand gestures. In addition the composition becomes careless, perhaps because of mass production, and the finish of the paintings was summary. There is an unmistakable overcrowding in the scene, which is against the spirit of classicism.The Ajanta artist has adopted simple as well as sophisticated techniques to bring out depth and relief. One among them is vartana. In this, relief is brought about by various methods of shading. A different technique was ujjotana. In this technique, the addition of highlights appropriately gives a three dimensional effect. The use of this technique may be seen in this scene and in a detail of this scene, WOMAN HOLDING A LOTUS. Highlights in the form of white patches may be found on the cheeks, the chin, nose, etc of the characters.

On the right-hand wall is an unidentified scene depicting SOME FOREIGNERS, which is significant for perhaps this panel is one of the last to be painted by the Ajanta artist before he abandoned the caves. This conclusion is based on the style of the painting.

The detail shows three fair skinned persons and a dark complexioned person who has an expressive face. These fair skinned people are most likely foreigners judging from their features and from the ornaments of the person in the foreground. This is understandable, for we have seen foreigners painted elsewhere in Ajanta, for example, a scene from a ROYAL HAREM on the ceiling of this cave and also on the ceilings of Cave 2, a scene called, FOREIGNERS DRINKING. Further Ajanta lay close to the trade route and among the benefactors and pilgrims would have been many from foreign countries.Considering the style of execution, this painting definitely belongs to the period of decline. The drawing, colouring and the finish look clumsy. The hands and palms are no not shapely. The magic wrought by the Ajanta artist by the competent use of thick and thin lines producing soft curves is totally missing here. The lines are stiff and lifeless.

But Ajanta artist is enigmatic. The artistic quality of the man in the foreground commented on above is really not quite true for the two persons painted above him, nor to the expressive dark complexioned man to his left. Scholars consider the TWO FIGURES OF THE BUDDHAS in Cave 10 to belong to the same decadent period.

That the Ajanta artist has not painted religious themes on the ceilings was mentioned elsewhere.. This perhaps gave him ample opportunity for his creative abilities. He gave expressions to his fantasies and rich imagery. In this cave, the ceiling, and some of the pillars, contain some exquisite animal and geometric motifs. The ceiling of the ante-chamber is embellished with a large a circular lotus-motif. The mail hall and elsewhere the decorations consist mostly of a number of rectangular panels filled with decorative motifs framed by smaller squares or rectangles with various decorative patterns. These decorative elements are of fruit and floral forms, animals, mythical forms, cherubs and human beings. Some of these motifs are repeated a number of times so that the effect is that of an enormous printed textile spread over the sanctuary. A selection from these individual motifs is given below.

FIGHTING BULLS, painted on the capital of the second pillar in the left-hand corner of the hall, is one of the most forceful and vigorous drawings in Ajanta. The two bulls convey a tense fury, and this effect is enhanced by the delineation of their bodies in identical postures; their heads are lowered, muscles contracted, tails raised, and the curves of their necks and humps clearly delineated. 0

We know of the same subject depicted in the Bhaja Cave, and one thousand years later a representation of two bulls engaged in combat painted in Mahal-i-Khas in Fatehpur Sikri, but neither of these paintings reaches the power of pictorial expression attained at Ajanta. To appreciate the artist's confident draftsmanship a line sketch of this painting, FIGHTING BULLS is included. The placid RESTING BULLS painted on the ceiling of Cave 17 is a study in contrast to these furious bulls.

A similar expression can be seen in the CHARGING BULL. Ajanta artists have employed a number of techniques to give a feeling of depth. Showing farther objects in diminished size called foreshortening, is one such technique. This painting is a splendid example of how conversant the artist was with this technique. A totally different approach is the use of vartana (shading) techniques. One of them is called patraja wherein the depth is brought out by drawing lines of suitable thickness to follow the contours of the object. We can see this technique used in this painting, and in the painting of an ELEPHANT on the ceiling of this cave. Both these paintings illustrate the skill of the artist in composing animals spaced in flowers and plants.

Another repetitive motif was painting mythological creatures like the MYTHICAL BIRDS. The colour scheme is most pleasing.

The artist had ample scope for mixing what is real with what is totally imaginary. He found uninhibited space to record his sense of humour on the ceilings. For example the CHERUBS IN PLAYFUL MOOD is one of the many such cherub pairs we find on the ceiling. CLOWN is an interesting study. The dwarf, cradled amongst flowers, uses the creeper as a swing, but the artist did not consider it inappropriate for a slender vine to support a man.

But it is the bacchanalian scene ROYAL HAREM which takes the cake for its incongruity in a religious shrine. The characters are obviously from a foreign country. From their attire it may be deduced that they are from the Northwest of India. Some identify the main characters with the Persian emperor Khausrau and his queen Sirin.

The effect of such an aesthetically pleasing way of filling up the space with idyllic colour designs can be seen in SQUARES AND RECTANGLES - 1. On the top two corners we have lotuses. The central square of the top row and the left corner of the bottom row are fruits that are all still life portraitures. The charging bull at the lower right corner is well animated. The central rectangle is a masterly composition. The motifs filling the space are a goose-like mythical bird, a parrot with a happy cherub at the centre, all in great harmony with the surrounding flowers.`BC^]q_w

Some of the painted scenes are damaged by fire. The dream of Queen Mahaa-maaya is faintly visible. The scenes of a Brahmin interpreting the dream, the Queen musing about the interpretation and finally, the birth itself are more clearly discernible.

The first scene, FUTURE BUDDHA IN TUSHITA HEAVEN, is enacted in the Tushita heaven, and painted above the second cell-door. Seated in pralamba (sitting-with-legs-hanging) pose hands striking dharma chakra pravartana mudra (teaching-attitude), He is surrounded by other deities and is making the announcement that He will be descending to the earth to be born to Queen Mahaa-maaya. The portrayal of the Master is not one of the best in Ajanta. The direct frontal pose without any inclination of the head is a rare feature in Ajanta art. Further the treatment of the whole composition lacks imagination and creativity. On the other hand the other scenes in the narrative are nor marred by this deficiency.

The next scene in which Queen Mahaa-maaya is dreaming is badly damaged, and is barely discernable. Hence we are constrained to move on to the next scene.

This scene in which the KING LISTENS TO THE INTERPRETATION OF THE DREAM by a Brahmin called Asita, is painted between the front pilaster and the third cell door. (Refer also to the sketch KING LISTENS TO THE ASTROLOGER).

The royal couple, Suddhodana and Mahaa-maaya are seated on two thrones and Asita, the astrologer, squats in front of them having made a loop with his fingers. His long twisted moustache is prominent. He is telling them that the queen has conceived a son destined to become either a universal monarch or a universal teacher. There are three female attendants at the back of the throne, the one on the extreme left being a chaawri-bearer. She looks at the queen. The other two are looking in different directions. There are three more female attendants further down. All these nine figures are enclosed in a framework formed by the vertical pillars, the foreground and the architrave on the top.

Moving further right, beyond a pillar used by the artist to start a new scene, we find the figure of QUEEN MAHAA-MAAYA MUSING over the prediction. (This scene is contained in the line sketch.) Here she stands, leaning against a pillar with her left leg touching it. We now see her musing over the exciting augury. That she is baffled is evident from her expression and the loop made by her fingers. Two more attendants look at her with anxiety written on their faces, but the queen appears to be unaware of their presence. In drawing the figure of the queen the artist is chiefly concerned with giving expression to her serenity mingled with a bashful smile expected of a would-be mother, and her anxiety for the safety of the life taking shape within her. Nevertheless, he drew in details the rich jewellery, elaborate coiffure and floral decoration over the chignon.

The lady is very scantily dressed, particularly in contrast to the two attendants in this scene. This feature of being 'dressed in nakedness' is a common, but intriguing feature at Ajanta. Her legs resemble the trunk of banana-tree, following the established canons of the time.

The BIRTH OF THE BUDDHA, is the next scene, painted on the space above the third and fourth cell-doors. The holy birth takes place in the Lumbini Grove. We see Queen Mahaa-maaya standing holding on to a branch of a shaala tree. The pose of the queen is very similar to that in the previous scene. In front of the queen are Mahaa-Brahma who holds a white umbrella. To his right is Indra respectfully holding the baby. By the side stands a chaawri-bearer showing great excitement. There are a few more figures in the group. To heighten the effect, the artist has taken liberties to incorporate gateways, buildings, compounds, etc, though these things are hard to imagine in a grove situated along a road.

The next scene in which the Holy Baby strides forward seven paces is badly damaged.

The classical style of Ajanta painting at its finest is undoubtedly represented by two superb panel, VOTARIES WITH OFFERINGS, on the left hand wall of the shrine of Hariti, the angel who protects children. The admirable composition of these two panels of identical size is constructed around the five main figures in such a way that they occupy a major portion of the wall; the other figures are about half the size and occupy the four corners.

The scene set is a landscape of rocks and gardens with their swaying creepers and banana trees. This creates an atmosphere of carefree tranquillity, as the lovely women proceed with gentle swaying movements towards the shrine. Unobtrusive modelling and calm linear harmony, combined with a restrained use of red tints for the women, is balanced against the greens of the banana leaves.

The figures are drawn halfway between strict profile and complete frontality. The faces are similarly sketched, and in one or two cases the woman's head is elegantly turned towards her companion. There is a remarkable ease in the drawing of arms and hands; the arms bend at the elbows, mostly at right angles, or hang softly caressing the slightly curved hips. The exquisite, gently bending attitudes of the votaries as they walk along in the open air with uncovered breasts wearing simple attire and very few ornaments bear some similarity with the Hinaayana painting RAJA WITH HIS RETINUE in Cave 10.

But this painting represents an entirely new concept of Ajanta colour and design. The elongation of the suave and elegant figures, which began at Amaravati, situated near Vijayawada in Andhra Pradesh, where there are excellent Buddhist sculptures belonging to the 2nd century, AD, is completed in Ajanta with unmatched perfection. The curvature of the figures is supple, flexible and simple, yet not exaggerated, and there are none of those excessive gesticulations, that are typical of the later baroque period of the 6th century.

Another point to be noticed in the evolution of style is the shading technique, called vartana, to bring out depth against the uniform tonal level maintained in the earlier Hinaayana painting.

Two other compositions that arrest our attention painted in the same shrine, and that belong to the same period, are CHILDREN PLAYING WITH HEN and CHILD TRYING TO SNATCH A TOY. Both these scenes are examples of the way the Ajanta artist handled with great interest, incidents from day to day life of common people..

These characters painted on the two walls in the Hariti Shrine together with the Simhala Avadaana painting in Cave 17 and the beautiful painting DYING PRINCESS in Cave 16, certainly represent the classical peak of Ajanta painting.

There are eight places that are considered the most sacred places in the Buddhist faith. They are referred to as attha-mahaa-thaanaani. These places derive their holiness because of their intimate connection with major events in the life of the Master. Of these four are related to the important events in the life of the Buddha: Lumbini, where Siddhartha was born, Gaya, where He attained Enlightenment; Sarnath, where he preached the First Sermon, and Kushinagara, where he obtained nirvaana.

The remaining four are the locations where He is believed to have performed miracles. One of these is the theme of this mural. In this Lord Buddha displays His miraculous powers at Shraavasti, by multiplying himself a thousand-fold to the bewilderment of His critics. This miracle is painted elsewhere in Ajanta as well.

The other miracles are: His ascending to the Heaven of Thirty-three Gods and preaching the Dharma is the theme of a mural called BUDDHA PREACHING TO THE CONGREGATION and the Miracle at Raja-griha of the subjugation of a mad elephant, Nalagiri, both painted in Cave 17. The miracle of a number of monkeys offering Him honey at Vaishaali is not to be found in Ajanta.

Shraavasti was the capital of the ancient kingdom of Koshala and is identified with the present day villages Saheth and Maheth the former in the district of Gonda and the later in the district of Bahraich, both in the state of Uttar Pradesh.

There are excellent portrayals of the compassionate Buddha in Ajanta. But, none of the painters reached the mark of high artistry to be seen in this painting. Conventional norms and formulae, in certain instances, have had preferences over artistic empathy. This painting demonstrates that conventionalism and mechanical representation rather than artistry inspired the scene.The scene on the right-hand wall of the ante-chamber, consisting of monotonously-reproduced figures of the Buddha in various mudra-s (hand-gestures), represents the Miracle of Shraavasti. Rows of seated Buddhas are also painted on the right and left walls of the shrine. These two compositions belong to different styles.

This miracle performed at Shraavasti became popular and has been painted in a number of caves in Ajanta. In this rows of Buddha-figures, one above the other, came to be painted. The repetitive theme in this Miracle of Shraavasti of Cave 2 reflects the artistic tendency of the age. The paintings lack the vigour, imagination and delicacy of the earlier ones. A fragmentary painted record on the back wall of the antechamber to the right of the shrine-door in Cave 2 may record the donation as that of the THOUSAND BUDDHAS. This record is attributed to the 6th century, AD.

Trying to impart some artistic interest to the panel they took care to vary the Buddha's postures and mudra-s. The painters also introduced an element of colourfulness by painting the robes of the Buddha in different colours ranging from white, grey, brown and green to red.

This painting may be considered a part the continuous decline in standards at Ajanta. Also belonging to this period is the painting of TWO FIGURES OF THE BUDDHA in Cave 17. These are painted one above the other, each enclosed in a cubicle.

On the other hand, the IMAGES OF THE BUDDHA, painted on the right and left walls of the shrine, is of considerable sophistication. This composition avoids the monotony of repetitive images of the Buddha witnessed in the previous depiction. This has been done by varying slightly the postures, particularly by turning some of the faces slightly, alternating the mudra-s and finally by the expert use of ujjotana (adding-highlights) technique and succeeded in creating a mysterious glow on the face the Master. To add to the effect the artist has provided the bolsters with a variety of designs on them.

The same theme, BUDDHA BAFFLES CRITICS, of the Miracle of Shraavasti of Cave 6 is a superior composition, particularly the variation in both the size and the poses of the Master and also the refined portraiture of the worshippers shown below.

This jaataka tale is the story of Bodhi-sattva born as Vidhura-pandita who demonstrated his supreme wisdom as a minister in the court of King Dhananjaya. Several episodes from Vidhura-pandita Jaataka occupy a large portion of the right wall of the hall, above and between the second and third cell doors.By the end of the 5th century AD, the classical upsurge of the Ajanta genius represented by the masterpieces in Caves 16 and 17 and the Hariti Shrine of Cave 2, began to give way to what has been called mannerism which later deteriorated into the baroque style. The latter style may be seen in the Mahaa-janaka Jaataka of Cave 1.

Passing from the classical to the manneristic mode, there were alterations in composition and in figure-drawing and decoration. The broad vision is progressively dissected into compartments so that the narrative is painted, not in an idyllic landscape, but in a maze of pillars of royal pavilions, palace windows, porches and balconies, city walls, shops and monasteries. Scenes became crowded with people, who are sophisticated, though they are not yet affected or effeminate. The men are still manly and the women feminine and they wear simple clothes and few ornaments.

For postures, expressions and movements characteristic of this period, it is difficult to match the episodes of the Vidhura-pandita Jaataka. Mention was made about the Ajanta artist's knowledge of perspectives, and his employing both the normal perspective and the reverse perspective. Certain scenes in the narration are depicted in the reverse perspective.

Since this is one of the surviving paintings where sufficient portions are discernible, let us first narrate the story before attempting to explain the painting.

The Jaataka: Bodhi-sattva was born in Indra-prastha and became a minister under King Dhananjaya belonging to the Kuru dynasty. He was universally admired for his wisdom. Once he delivered a sermon in the court of King Varuna, king of the naaga-s. Impressed with his discourse, the king presented him with a costly piece of jewellery. This was not to the liking of his queen, Vimala. She feigned illness and said she would die unless his heart was brought to her as a proof of his demise. The distraught king proclaimed that he would give his beautiful daughter Irandati in marriage to anyone who would perform this task. The king entreated his daughter to seek a brave husband. Once a yaksha general called Punyaka, while riding past in heaven on his magic horse, heard the sweet song of Irandati, and fell in love with her. He promised to get the heart of Vidhura-pandita. Punyaka was aware of King Dhananjaya's weakness for the game of dice and enticed him to a game. In the ensuing game Punyaka won from the king Vidhura-pandita as a wager. On the way to the naaga world Punyaka made a number of unsuccessful attempts to kill Vidhura-pandita to get his heart. Finally, Vidhura-pandita volunteered to tell him the way to get his heart. Punyaka, taken aback at his generosity, brought Vidhura-pandita alive to the court of King Varuna.

Both Varuna and his queen were enchanted by Vidhura-pandita's wisdom and made him to stay as their guest for a few days before returning to the Kuru kingdom.

Now let us try to follow the story. We must remember that the painting sequence in Ajanta does not follow a chronological order. This we have seen in other panels as well.

The first three scenes are painted in the extreme right. The first two scenes are shown in IRANDATI MEETS PUNYAKA and the third scene is KING VARUNA'S PALACE SCENE. The following explanation is supported by a line sketch PUNYAKA'S ARRIVAL and the above two colour pictures.

In the centre of the line drawing, and on to the right of IRANDATI MEETS PUNYAKA, we find Princess Irandati on a Swing. This is one of the most beautiful compositions of Ajanta. The background is embellished with floral designs. The movement of the swing is aptly conveyed by the curves of the ropes and the streaming flags attached to the swing. The outstretched leg of the girl with toes brought together is a realistic rendering of a pendulum-like movement. According to the jaataka, while swinging carefree, Irandati was also singing in a sweet voice. The swing plays an important role in Indian cultural and social life, and has been extolled as romantic in Indian literature.

After the marriage Punyaka sets out to kill Vidhura-pandita and to bring back his heart. Punyaka knows King Dhananjaya's weakness for gambling. Left to the second cell door and above it we see four main scenes. each enclosed in a pavilion, depicting Some Episodes from VIDHURA-PANDITA JAATAKA. Details of each of the scenes are also given and we shall discuss these scenes in a chronological order.

On the left side of the top left panel we have the scene called GAME OF DICE. The scenes are shown as line drawings in GAME OF GAMBLING. In this we see Punyaka enticing Dhananjaya to a game of dice and the game being played. On the left we see Punyaka showing a magnificent jewel as a wager to tempt King Dhananjaya to a game. The king is occupying a low seat; the cloth covering the seat is striped. He has made some gesture with his right hand whereas his left hand is resting on the left knee. Vidhura-pandita, sitting to the left of the king, is looking at Punyaka with concern. Below the king on his right is a door-keeper with a moustache and a long coat with tight sleeves. This was possibly the usual livery of doorkeepers, for we find the doorkeepers of Mahaa-hamsa Jaataka in the same cave, dressed similarly as also the one in Conversion of Nanda in Cave 1. In front of the doorkeeper, below the throne of the king there are two more figures, one male and the other female, the latter with the back to us. They are possibly personal attendants to the king. Both of them are in great animation.

To the right, King Dhananjaya and Punyaka are seen engaged in a game of dice. The king is seen on the left and Punyaka on the right with the game-board between them. The king is at the point of throwing the dice. Punyaka is serious and anxiously waiting for the dice to show up. The queen, painted below the king, is appealing to Punyaka, perhaps to stop the dangerous game.The artist has adopted the technique of reverse perspective to draw the board of dice in minute detail showing clearly all the twenty-four divisions in two rows. The three dice are lying at the end of the king's outstretched hand. Such detail would not have been possible if normal perspective was followed. We have seen another example of this technique of reverse perspective in the building behind the dancer in Mahaa-janaka Jaataka.

The king loses the game, and, thus, his minister. In the next scene, VIDHURA-PANDITA COUNSELS DHANANJAYA, painted below the GAME OF DICE, we see King Dhananjaya and his minister in conversation. The king rather hopes that the rules of the game will not allow him to wager his minister. But the truthful minister Vidhura-pandita counsels Dhananjaya that a minister is a legal wager, and thus he had now become the property of Punyaka. The scene has been somewhat damaged and hence the rest of the figures in this scene are not clearly seen.

Before leaving for the naaga-kingdom with his new master, VIDHURA-PANDITA GIVES A FAREWELL SERMON in the court of King Dhananjaya. This scene is depicted to the right of the scene of Vidhura-pandita counselling his king. Between the two scenes is shown a balcony in which the queen is talking to a maid, perhaps telling her to request Vidhura-pandita to give a sermon before leaving. While giving the farewell sermon the minister is holding a lotus flower on his right hand symbolising his divinity. In this masterly composition, we see Vidhura-pandita as a personification of love and compassion. We have the king before him listening to the sermon in rapt attention. Behind Vidhura-pandita there are three graceful maidens with sorrowful faces at the imminent loss of such a saintly person.

Below this is a long panel depicting the DEPARTURE OF VIDHURA-PANDITA. Similar in composition to the marching of Simhala in the Simhala Avadaana panel, the majestic movement of the horses in various hues, the stately gait of the caparisoned elephants, the variety of sthaana-s (body-postures) of the solders, their weapons etc are drawn with great skill. According to the story, Punyaka takes the minister on his flying horse and flies towards the naaga-loka (serpent-kingdom). On the way he attempts to kill Vidhura-pandita to take out his heart, but fails. It is then that the compassionate Vidhura-pandita explains to him how he may be killed and his heart taken out. Ashamed of his proposed evil act Punyaka brings Vidhura-pandita alive to the naaga court.

The last scene is enacted in the kingdom of the naaga-s. This episode, painted to the right of the scene GAME OF DICE, shows VIDHURA-PANDITA IN THE NAGA KINGDOM preaching before an assemblage of naaga-s, headed by King Varuna himself. The major portion of the scene is given in the sketch, VIDHURA-PANDITA IN NAAGA-LOKA. The teacher has, as usual, taken the central position and is practically the pivot of the composition. He is seen seated on a low stool, interlaced with tape. He has a tilak (decorative mark) on the forehead.

In front of the revered guest is the naaga-king with folded hands bowing his head in supplication. The halo of five serpents makes his identification easier. Besides the king we see two ladies, evidently Queen Vimala and Princess Irandati, listening to the sermon. They do not have their hoods now. Punyaka's attention is not on the discourse; he seems to be eyeing his sweetheart, Irandati.The balcony scene to the right of this has already been described as QUEEN VIMALA AND PRINCESS IRANDATI. Below this balcony is a window through which we see a couple. Some identify them as Vidhura-pandita, holding a lotus in his hand, and Queen Vimala.After such a long description we may go back to the discussion on the art style of this narration. The reason for this extended discussion is that the mural exhibits characteristics of both the classical and the later baroque periods. Interestingly certain aspects of the composition resemble the pre-classical style. Thus in terms of art style this is really unique.

The facial features are similar to those in the pre-Christian era painting of Cave 10, like the RAJA WITH HIS RETINUE. The golden-brown complexion, round faces, full red lips, short, but pointed noses are considered to be Southern and non-Aryan. So also the veil over the women's head and the elaborate turbans on the heads of the men-folk. These are found in some measure in this painting. There are no exaggerations like bulging body-curves and elongated eyes. Men and women wear simple dresses and are bare above the waist.

On the other hand the spatial arrangement is not classical, at least in some scenes like the scene in which Punyaka entices Dhananjaya.

That the narration takes place 'in a maze of pillars of royal pavilions, palace windows, porches and balconies' betrays its deviation from the classical idyllic landscape. Though a variety of body-positions are shown there is certain amount of monotony. This is seen in the scene, Punyaka enticing Dhananjaya in the panel, GAME OF DICE and in the panel VIDHURA-PANDITA GIVES A FAREWELL SERMON, the two adjacent panels.

Every available space on the ceilings and on the pillars has been painted. This cave contains the best decorations on the ceilings. The compositions of these ceiling paintings are, like in the other caves, of two major types. One is a square containing a number of concentric circular bands around a central lotus-design, with the four corners - called spandrels - embellished with mithuna-s (couples), birds or mythical beings. The circular bands, in turn, are filled with wreath of flowers. The best preserved of the ceiling paintings are in the sanctum. This kind is referred to as 'Squares and Circles' in the present discussion.

The other is filling up the space with squares and rectangles, in which are painted various motifs. These are being referred to as 'Squares and Rectangles' in the discussion.

Fruits, birds, animals and pure geometric patterns are the motifs used in the above. The fruits, which have been depicted, are fruits, mangoes, pineapples, pears, grapes, berries, lichies, etc. We have birds like, geese, ducks, cranes, peacocks, parrots, etc.

There are also mythical animals of a wide variety. In a lighter vein we have sportive figures, like, dwarfs, buffoons, jugglers, foreigners in exotic garments, etc. And, finally, there are pure geometric patterns.

Apart from the colour harmony and delicacy of the designs, even a casual observer can not miss the movement and plasticity of forms.

The painting on the ceiling of the main shrine, SQUARES AND CIRCLE - 3, is in a better state of preservation. The central circle contains a large, white and serene lotus, symbolising purity. The ring around it is a wreath. The one next is of some geometrical pattern. Filling up the spandrel are four flying gandharva-s, which arrest our attention. Bare-bodied and clad only in loincloths, these gandharva-s are in a playful mood. Their flight is unmistakable. The cumulous cloud behind and the fluttering ends of their garments point to this. Two of them are wearing socks. Binduja is a technique of vartana, a family of shading techniques adopted by the Ajanta artist to effect an illusion of depth and relief. This method, also known as the 'dot-and-stipple-method', uses a variation in the concentration of dots to produce the desired effect. This can be seen on the belly, chin and on the arms of the flying gandharva-s, bringing out the contours of their body.

On the ceiling of the ante-chamber we have in SQUARES AND CIRCLES - 6 a portraiture of a row of geese in a ring. It is a superb rendering. About this Yazdani comments: "Some (of these ducks) have their necks sharply curved, as if they were in an angry mood, some have stretched their necks forward to show their determination to attack, some have bent them low to preen their feathers in an attitude of languor, some have turned their heads gracefully to one side to look amorously at their mates, and some have turned them with a romantic curve to show their feeling of ecstasy.

The fine brush work of the artist in the delineation of the features of the birds also deserve a close study. The subject, both with regard to the ingenuity of design and perfection of technique plainly shows the skill of the Ajanta artist in decorative art." But Narayan Sanyal points out one feature not mentioned by the others. The number of geese in the composition is twenty-three, an inconvenient prime number, precluding the possibility of using a stencil!

Painted outside the antechamber is another beautiful SQUARES AND CIRCLES - 7. A line drawing of this is given as A CEILING PAINTING OF CAVE 2. Exquisite geometrical and floral patters fill the circular bands. Mithuna-s are painted in the spandrels. In spite of the restricted space the couples are not cramped as can be seen in the Detail of SQUARES AND CIRCLES - 7. As in Cave 1, the ceiling decoration consists of filling up the space with squares and rectangles. Belonging to this mode of decoration is SQUARES AND RECTANGLES - 2, a part of the ceiling painting in the rear aisle. The geese, the mythical birds and the floral patterns are remarkable. Skilful shading has given the pure geometrical patterns a three dimensional effect.The ceiling also contain some scenes in a lighter vein. Two FOREIGNERS DRINKING is a detail from the painting on the ceiling of the verandah. From their dress, they look like foreigners. But their dress is very interesting - head-dress, scarves, tunics and socks. The intriguing aspect of this detail as well as similar paintings in Ajanta is the inappropriateness of such drinking scenes in a religious shrine.

2. Shad-danta is seen again surrounded by six elephants: The Bodhi-sattva now playfully holds a huge lotus in his trunk that he perhaps offers to Mahaa-subhadda that in turn enrages Chulla-subhadda.

3. Subhadda, Queen of Varanasi, feigns Illness and directs Sonuttara about the whereabouts of Shad-danta: The young queen of Varanasi (who was Chulla-subhadda in her previous birth) is seated on a bamboo stool. She wants the tusks she has seen in a dream the night before. The king stands before her, promising to send a retinue of hunters to find out this six-tusked white elephant. There are four attendants in this scene, two seated and the other two standing. They are all facing the queen. There is a metal ewer beside the bed. Such ewers are still in use in India for carrying water for religious purposes. The sandals of the king also show a design available even now in the markets throughout the subcontinent. The king one feels, had been listening a moment before and has just stood up to say something in reply to the surly queen.

4. The King orders his Men to get the Tusks: The king, seated on a throne in the court, is ordering Sonuttara and his lieutenant to arrange for the hunting mission. The stooping hunters show their obsequious reverence.3

The queen, seated on a couch beside the king, has turned her head to look at him. She has also raised two fingers of her right hand, and rests her legs on a low cushion. By the side of the chaawri-bearer behind the raja, is an Amazon holding the royal umbrella. The woman wears a scarf over her head. None of the other girls in this panel has this sort of headgear. A stout bodyguard, holding a staff is standing on the other side of the queen. The mango tree is at the back. We see the same girl noticed in the earlier scene, at the feet of the queen.

5. Sonuttara spies the Elephant from the Rocks: Bodhi-sattva is seen surrounded by ten elephants and a calf. At the extreme right Sonuttara, with the same striped vest, stares at the colossal elephant with wonder, holding a bow in his left hand. The front-view of the elephant next to the hunter shows how the artist has tried to change the angle of vision to avoid monotony. Although the banana-grove is customarily associated with the elephant in Indian painting and literature, the artist has drawn a massive banyan tree in tune with the bulk and majestic figure of the Bodhi-sattva.

6. Sonuttara brings the sawn Tusks and Subhadda swoons at the Sight of the Tusks: Sonuttara has arrived at the court of the king of Varanasi carrying the treasure. The queen has fallen into a swoon as she remembers her beloved of the previous birth.

The king, seated by her on a chair that seems quite modern has turned round to support her with his left hand. There are four female attendants standing in a row at the back of the queen. The girl at the extreme right with a laurel on her head has covered her mouth with her right hand to stifle a shriek. The maid next to her has brought some water in a pot to revive the swooning queen. The damsel next to her, holding a fan in her right hand, looks at the former. The fourth, an umbrella-bearer, with a typical chignon on her head stares at Sonuttara. Besides these four girl-attendants, there are two more, one sitting at the feet of the queen, rubbing her feet, to help the blood circulation. The other damsel leans forward to balance the Queen as well as the composition. The realistic depiction of the scene, its dramatic treatment, together with the rhythmic expressions, all speak of the magnificent skill of the artist. The figures drawn in this scene reflect great plasticity of form mingled with artistic elegance and grace. The anxiety of the attendants, the expression of awe and wonder of Sonuttara at the sudden turn of events, the extreme perplexity of the king are all feelingly conveyed. But the melancholy and repentant look of the swooning queen surpasses them all and is comparable only to the monumental, the DYING PRINCESS, painted in Cave 16, some eight centuries later.

With the decline of artistic standards, the narrative scenes were replaced by repetitive representations of the Buddha. They came to be painted as a result of the gifts of Buddhist monks for earning religious merit for the attainment of supreme knowledge by all sentient beings beginning with the donors' parents. A painted record below the figures of eight Buddhas in Cave 22 tells us that the painting was the gift of a monk for the attainment of the supreme knowledge by all beings. The purpose of such gifts is further made clear by the statement that they endow the donor with good looks, good luck and good qualities.

In this painting TWO FIGURES OF THE BUDDHA are shown seated on a full-blown lotus against a bolster. The devotee who caused the figure to be painted also sits near the Master with his hands folded in anjali mudra (praying-attitude).

This painting is a specimen of the period of decadence. These are only two of many such Buddhas that we find enclosed in separate cubicles. Compared with other the Buddhas on the walls of Ajanta painted earlier, they lack refinement and finish. The colours are naive, flat and unnuanced - the lines wanting in feeling.

This painting may be considered a part of the continuous decline in standard at Ajanta. Also belonging to this period is the painting THOUSAND BUDDHAS, a scene from the Miracle of Shraavasti in Cave 2.

One wonders why out of the six female figures the princess is practically nude, although the artist has carefully arranged dresses for the other female figures. We come across with the feature of heroines of Ajanta being 'clothed in nakedness' a number of times.

The princess, it may be pointed out, is of dark complexion, this 'black is beautiful', is frequently met in Ajanta. The other black beauties are SHAKTI PANDARA painted in the Bodhi-sattva Avalokiteshvara Panel in cave 1, Princess Irandati of Vidhura-pandita Jaataka in Cave 2 and the famous BLACK APSARAS of Cave 17. While commenting on this aspect it was pointed out that both Rama and Krishna were dark complexioned. Hasn't Kalidasa extolled that 'black is beautiful' while describing classical beauty in his Megha-dootam?

John Griffths was Principal of J.J.School of Art, Mumbai, and he had supervised copying Ajanta paintings from 1872 to 1855. He has published a monumental two-volume book on Ajanta paintings in 1896. He wrote: "For pathos and sentiment and the unmistakable way of telling its story this picture, I consider, cannot be surpassed in the history of art. A Florentine could have put better drawing, and a Venetian better colour, but neither could have thrown better expression into it."

On to the right of the main-doorway is painted this story of a miracle performed by the Buddha, called the Miracle at Raja-griha or the Subjugation of Nalagiri.

The Story: Deva-datta was a cousin of the Buddha. He was envious of the Great Being and attempted to murder him. He failed miserably every time. Subjugation of Nalagiri is one such episode. It may be recollected that King Bimbi-saara was a faithfully follower of the Buddha. But his son, Ajaata-shatru, who came to power after dethroning his father took steps to remove Buddhist influence in his country. Deva-datta conspired with King Ajaata-shatru of Raja-griha, and released an intoxicated elephant, called Nalagiri, on the streets of Raja-griha just when the Master was passing through.

The people ran helter-skelter on seeing the charging elephant. But the Most Compassionate One was unperturbed. When Nalagiri came close he gently stroked him, to which the mad elephant responded by kneeling down in obeisance. Deva-datta's scheme was defeated by the love shown by the Buddha.

In the first act, we find the king and DEVA-DATTA HATCHES A CONSPIRACY. The location is a two-storied palace building. On the right side of the top floor we have King Ajaata-shatru in royal dress and a crown. To his left is Deva-datta who is in the garb of a Brahmin priest. The left part of the king is damaged and so is the major portion of the right part of Deva-datta. The king is wearing jewellery studded with pearls, diamonds and other precious stones. From the gestures of these characters we may reconstruct the plot. Deva-datta has raised two fingers. He has argued his case.

Behind the king we find four women, three directly behind the king and one below the other maids. If we understand the syntax of Ajanta properly, the women are all palace-maids, and the queen is not among them. The maid in the middle is somewhat short and is wearing a long sleeved jacket. She is looking at the maid to her right, who, from her looks is shocked at the conspiracy taking shape in the balcony.

There are five maids seen on the lower floor. They are animatedly discussing the shocking rumour they have heard.

The noteworthy feature is the hairstyles of these maids. There is no repetition. Particularly interesting is the hair-do of the woman who is seen second from the right on the lower floor. This bears a close resemblance to the head-dress of present-day Mohini Aattam dancers of Kerala. The architectural details of this scene are worthy of study. Also interesting are the sthaana-s (body-postures) of the women and their mudra-s (hand-gestures).

Separated by a conventional arch, the next act begins. We see a bhikshu hurrying, probably taking the information to the Buddha. Unfortunately, this part of the mural is badly damaged. In the next Act, which is better preserved the charging of the rogue elephant and its subjugation, are depicted.

A line diagramme SUBJUGATION OF NALAGIRI and a colour reproductions MAD ELEPHANT NALAGIRI and SUBJUGATION OF THE ELEPHANT support the following description of the scene.

The incident takes place in the market place with rows of shops on the ground floor and residential apartments on the upper floor. The former has awnings of canvas or bamboo-mat in the form of lean-to verandahs to protect the shops against rain and sun. It may be pointed out that a similar market-scene can be found painted in Vishvaantara Jaataka in the same cave.

The awnings of the first shop from the left are found to have been dislodged by MAD ELEPHANT NALAGIRI, which dominates the composition. There are terror-stricken people here and there running away in fright. The shopkeepers pull down the door-shutters and the ladies in the balconies look horrified. Near the hind part of the elephant a mounted guard is seen with his right hand raised to warn the crowd. The gigantic monster has a sinister look and a man is seen dangling between the tusks.

In the next scene SUBJUGATION OF THE ELEPHANT, to the right we find the elephant kneeling before the Buddha cringing in obeisance. The Master, standing on a lotus, with his disciple, Shari-putta, on his right, is seen caressing the subjugated giant, stroking him with affection. Shopkeepers and pedestrians are all impressed at the miracle. Some have folded their hands to show respect. The smartly dressed ladies on the upper storey, who were torn a few minutes ago in horror and anguish, are now relieved of the tension and are seen strewing flowers and garlands on the Master.

This particular scene has been a subject of criticism by Yazdani. He observed: "In drawing the figures of the ladies in the balconies, the artist has exhibited both imagination and artistic skill, but the composition of the entire subject would appeal more to a lover of the legend than to the art; for the two large representations of the elephant have covered almost the entire space, and the figures of the Buddha and his chief disciple show little spontaneity or freedom of expression qualities which are essential to art of a higher class."

A lover of art for art sake may not agree with the artist who is driven purely by religious fervour.

The Shad-danta Jaataka is painted in Cave 10 during the pre-Christian Hinaayana period. The same jaataka has been narrated in this cave, after a gap of about six hundred years. A comparative study of the two compositions would be rewarding, as this brings into focus both the continuity and the variation in the artistic styles over a period of these six hundred years. The painter has drawn Shad-danta Jaataka of Cave 17 on the outer wall of the front corridor to the left of the main entrance.

In the second scene, shown in the middle we find SPORTING SHAD-DANTA with his herd in the beautiful lake. The lotus flowers on the surface of the water, barely visible at the bottom of the left side, vividly suggest the animals' carefree life in the jungle.

In the lower portion of the right hand panel is depicted the third scene. First, we find the six-tusked Shad-danta and his followers. The hunter, Sonuttara, has been drawn thrice. First, he stoops low before the Bodhi-sattva, in great reverence. In the second, he looks back at the elephant with gratitude while balancing the tusks on a pole. In the third, SONUTTARA BRINGS THE TUSKS from Varanasi.

In the last episode, painted to the left, the QUEEN FAINTS at the sight of the six tusks. It appears as a framed picture in the mural.In terms of style this composition is vastly different from the great mural narrating the same jaataka, Shad-danta Jaataka of Cave 10. While the arrangement was horizontal in the earlier mural, it is executed in a zigzag fashion here. The composition of the scene in the two paintings is different. While she was seated in Cave 10, we find her fainting in the arms of the king here, like Maadri reclining on Vishvaantara in the scene VISHVAANTARA CONSOLES HIS WIFE in the narration of Vishvaantara Jaataka, painted on the outer verandah in the very same cave. Further, the drawing of the royal bedchamber within a frame is unusual even by the standards of the day. Continuity is also witnessed from the painting of elephants from the Hinaayana painting of the 2nd century BC of Cave 10 to this composition.

Though not connected with the narration directly MONKEY BENEATH A PALAS TREE is an interesting painting. The relaxed, dignified calm of the monkey, consisting basically of one masterly sweep of a long stroke of the brush starting beneath the chin and forming a curve outlining the head and spine and terminating beneath the knee-cap, is a fine example of the translation of closely observed scenes and moments of action or repose into simple yet profoundly explicit drawings.

An equally interesting feature of the scene (not shown in the colour reproduction) is row of ants on one of the branches painted by the artist. The Ajanta artist has paid minute attention to represent all kinds of living beings, including ants!

The Buddhist faith preaches compassion towards all creatures. The Bodhi-sattva incarnated as animals many times to propagate this virtue. Once He was born as a monkey and the story is known as Mahaa-kapi Jaataka. There are two episodes narrated in this cave. In one of them He helped His herd escape from the attack of the king by forming Himself into a bridge across the river. In the second, He helps an ungrateful Brahmin who attempted to kill Him.

The first episode is painted on the inside wall of the front verandah, on the left when you enter through main entrance.

The Jaataka: Once the Bodhi-sattva was as born as the king of monkeys, and lived in a forest far away from the city. In the forest there was a very huge mango tree, which produced extremely delicious and extra-ordinarily large fruits. One day one of the fruits fell into the river and was picked up by a fisherman. Never having seen such a fruit he took it to his king. The king after enjoying the fruit, ordered his army to trace the tree which produced such delicious fruit. When the tree was located the king found to his dismay that a large herd of monkeys were destroying the mangoes. He, then ordered his army to shoot the monkeys down.
