The king-monkey apprehending danger to His subjects caught hold of a branch of the tree to convert his body into a bridge and ordered the monkeys to escape.

The last monkeys, who were envious of the king-monkey, intentionally kicked the most benevolent monkey while jumping off his back. The tired monkey-king fell into the river below wounded. The astonished king , touched by what he had seen, rescued Him. But in spite of his nursing, the monkey-king died, but without any hatred towards the monkey who kicked Him.In this badly damaged mural, the artist has taken some liberty in the narration. Instead of a mango tree he has drawn a banyan tree. This is evident from the shape of the leaves and from the presence of aerial roots. He has also added a touch of humour which will be mentioned in the discussion that follows. The mural is almost obliterated and hence the discussion is supported by a line diagramme MAHAA-KAPI SAVES HIS HERD.

The narration of this jaataka is another illustration that the scenes are not painted in the chronological sequence. In most of the paintings we find that scenes are grouped together on the basis of location. To follow the sequence would mean abrupt changes in the settings, which, the artist might have thought, would disturb the composition.

The story starts at the top left with a monkey sitting comfortably on a branch and enjoying a mango fruit. For the next scene is half way down the mural where the king riding a horse is marching with his army towards the forest. The king may be identified by the royal umbrella over him. The commotion of the marching of the army is evident, with the solders in great animation, carrying swords and shields.

In the next scene, which is shown a little higher up, three archers are shooting at the monkeys on the tree. On the top of the mural is painted the monkey-king stretching his body to act as a bridge and monkeys passing over his back. Next we see the fallen monkey-king being rescued by two solders on a blanket. The scene in which the Bodhi-sattva is preaching the Law to the king is not included in the sketch.

Let us see the secular part of the composition, for which the Ajanta artist has always had a weakness. A low wall separates the river from the forest. The river is shown abounding in fishes and water birds. In the lower portion there are a number of bathers, males and females. The Ajanta artist who never misses an opportunity to add a touch of humour, has portrayed a short man, probably a court-jester, eves-dropping on the bathing beauties! Another episode from Mahaa-kapi Jaataka is painted on the left-hand wall of the back corridor and the MONKEY HELPS A MAN and MAN THROWS STONE ON THE MONKEY are two of the important scenes. Let me narrate this episode in brief. Once a man, while collecting some fruits in a forest, fell into a pit from which he could not come out. The Bodhi-sattva, born as a monkey, saw his plight and with great effort carried him on his back to safety.

When the tired monkey was taking rest, the ungrateful man picked up a big rock and threw it at the monkey so that he could eat it. The monkey was badly injured and admonished the man for his ungrateful act. Then the compassionate monkey led him out of the forest.

In the first scene MONKEY HELPS A MAN, we see on the top right the fallen man shouting for help. On the left is the compassionate monkey responding to his call. The forest scene is composed excellently, the trees and two deer are very natural.

In the scene MAN THROWS STONE ON THE MONKEY, at the centre the exhausted monkey is asleep and the ungrateful man is about to throw a huge stone. The effort of lifting a huge stone is unmistakable. On the left we see the monkey chiding him.

Several episodes from the Sutasoma Jaataka are painted on the wall of the back corridor with a great deal of imagination and skill. Next to Simhala Avadaana and Vishvaantara Jaataka this narration is the longest in Ajanta. Unfortunately, a major potion of the painting has perished.

The Jaataka: King Sudaasa of Varanasi went on a hunting expedition. Exhausted, he took rest on a stone. While he was thus sleeping, a lioness attracted by the king came to him and licked his heel. This made her pregnant. Her son Saudaasa became the king after his father's death. One day his cook by mistake served him human flesh. Thereafter the lioness's instinct was quickened and he became a cannibal. He would satisfy his cannibalistic appetite by having his subjects murdered. In course of time the citizens drove him out of the country. While living in the forest, Saudaasa preyed on travellers. One day King Sutasoma of Indra-prastha, a Bodhi-sattva incarnation, had gone to the forest to bathe, where he was attacked by Saudaasa, and was taken as a prisoner. Sutasoma requested permission for a day's freedom so that he could hear a sermon from an ascetic. When Sutasoma kept his promise his captor was impressed. Then Bodhi-sattva preached the Law of Dharma to Saudaasa, who gave up cannibalism and joined the Holy Order.

One of the surviving early scenes of this story is the scene of the DEER HUNT by King Sudaasa. It shows the king of Varanasi setting out on a hunt with his retinue, accompanied by two other princes and a pack of dogs. The King is seen pursuing the deer and, having become separated from his retinue, entering a forest abounding in wild animals. The nimble movement of the deer and the wild stare in its eyes are shown in this DEER HUNT. In this unsurpassed masterpiece, the horror of being hunted and killed is marvellously conveyed by the animal's abrupt and swift leap and the almost hypnotised look in its bulging eyes as it glances back at the approaching horse.

The forest scene continues above this panel, and the depicted scenes involve the lioness. We see the COURT OF SUDAASA where the lioness tells the king that she is carrying the king's child.

The CORONATION OF SAUDAASA is the next important panel, where we find Saudaasa ascending the throne on the death of his father. The ceremonial bath is realistically portrayed and the excitement is unmistakable.

Another realistic presentation is the ROYAL KITCHEN OF SAUDAASA where his cook inadvertently cooked human meat. The composition provides us information regarding contemporary social habits.

The next scene in which SAUDAASA TASTES HUMAN FLESH is another important panel. The king is seen sitting on a low stool and eating from a plate kept on another low stool. Two cooks are looking through a window, most likely, of the kitchen. The maids who are serving him are seen looking at the king with concern.

The detail of the battle scene, realistically depicting the confusion of war, in which cavalry and foot soldiers are participating, is shown in KING FIGHTS AGAINST HIS ARMY when the army rose in revolt against the cannibalistic king.

ARMY DRIVING SAUDAASA TO THE FOREST is depicted in the next scene. We see the king on the galloping horse. The movement of the horse and the commotion of the moving foot-solders are the result of close observation and draughtsmanship.

The theme of this painting is one of the four miracles performed by the Buddha. He ascended the Heaven of Thirty-three Gods and preached the Dharma to His Mother at Shaankashya, one of the eight holy places in the Buddhist faith. They are referred to as attha-mahaa-thaanaani.

Two more miracles have been depicted in Ajanta. His multiplying Himself a thousand-fold, called the Miracle of Shraavasti, is depicted in Cave 2 and is described in the scene THOUSAND BUDDHAS. The Miracle at Raaja-griha, the subjugation of a mad elephant at Raaja-griha is also painted in this cave. Shankaashya, where this miracle is believed to have taken place is known to day as Sankissa or Sankasia, in the district of Farukkhabad in the sate of Uttar Pradesh.

The Miracle: The Buddha went to the Heaven of Thirty-three Gods at the invitation of Indra (called Shakra in the Buddhist mythology). There he preached to the congregation of gods, which included His mother Mahaa-maaya. After this, He returned to the earth by the three-ladder made by Indra. On reaching the world He found a large assemblage of monks and noblemen waiting to hear His sermon. This theme is painted on the left wall of the ante-chamber.

The episode is composed in three rows. In the top row we see the Buddha preaching in heaven, in the middle we see the Buddha descending and in the bottom panel, the Master is giving a sermon before a congregation of people on earth. A major part is given in the sketch PREACHING OF THE BUDDHA. The top panel is rather damaged, but some figures are identifiable. Close to the Buddha we see a group of women, including the mother of the Buddha MAHAA-MAAYA LISTENS TO THE SERMON. Maaya-devi is 'dressed in nakedness', whereas the dresses of the other people look foreign. On the other side are the gods.

In the middle panel the Buddha can be seen descending to the earth. He is accompanied by His disciples and the gods. The Compassionate One is in His usual saffron robe.

The bottom row is better preserved. The characters comprising the audience are truly heterogeneous.

In this bottom panel of we see the BUDDHA PREACHES TO THE CONGREGATION. He is sitting in dharma chakra mudra (teaching-attitude) exuding warmth and compassion. His throne is ornamented. His chair-bearers are Bodhi-sattva Padma-paani and Bodhi-sattva Avalokiteshvara. There are also two flying cherubs behind him.

On his left are a few people squatting on the ground. All of them look like monks. The elderly monk in the centre of the front row is identified as Shari-putta, one of His important disciples. The one to his right looking at the Master is Ananda, His cousin and disciple. The one behind Shari-putta with a checked upper-cloth is another disciple, Moggallana. We notice at least three men in royal uniform riding horses.

Then we have a WOMAN ELEPHANT-RIDER with her entourage. She must be a queen as there is an umbrella held over head. Some identify her as Maaya-devi, the mother of the Buddha. She is sitting with a bolster of interesting design at her back. Careful readers might have noticed the endless variety of textile designs in Ajanta.

On the left part of the panel, there is another group of AUDIENCE. Among the people sitting on the floor, in the first row, starting from the left, Ajaata-shatru, his father King Bimbi-saara of Magadha and King Prasenjita of Koshala. Behind Bimbi-saara is a person of foreign origin. There are also a number of people with curved swords held high. Behind them are two men on horses. They look like foreigners again. Further above them are two caparisoned elephants with raised trunks. The riders may be foreign statesmen.

This grand mural, where over fifty people participate, is a subject of great admiration by art critics all over the world.

This mural, MOTHER AND CHILD BEFORE THE BUDDHA, is considered the best piece of art at Ajanta. It is most unfortunate that this fresco has been damaged to a great extent during the last one and a half centuries. Splendid reproductions of this picture have been made by some of the pioneer visitors. The scene is painted on the rear wall of the ante-chamber to the left of the door leading to the shrine.

The scene shows Yashodhara and Rahula, wife and son of the Buddha before he renounced the world, meeting the Lord, when the latter came begging to the palace-door during his sojourn at Nigro-dharama Vihaara on the outskirts of Kapila-vastu.

The sketch The BUDDHA IN KAPILA-VASTU and the MOTHER AND CHILD BEFORE THE BUDDHA and its detail the Mother and Child a reproduction in colour aid in following the description of the mural.To the left is the massive standing figure of the Buddha measuring about 3 metres in height. The features of his face are obliterated but his extended hand holding the begging bowl and the angle of his head towards Yashodhara, in fact his entire gesture shows his compassion mixed with love.

The orange colour of his long robe contrasts well with the dark tint of the background, which is again relieved here and there of its sombreness and monotony by the sprinkling of white flowers. The golden-brown head stands out almost in relief against the light green of the halo. The same effect of alto-relievo sculpture is noticeable between the jade-green begging bowl and the orange robe and the brown head.

In front of the Buddha stands Yashodhara with young Rahula. Rahula was only seven days old when his father renounced the world. Now he is a boy of seven years. He looks up at his father for the first time in his life. In his look we find awe and wonder mingled with love and respect. The figure of Yashodhara is superb. The artist has attributed to her all the charms of natural beauty. She has bedecked herself profusely with ornaments, perhaps with the faint hope of enticing Him to return to her.

It is true that we cannot say, from the faintness of the figure, whether the Blessed One notices in front of him his wife and child, or just a mother and a boy - just two entities worthy of receiving His universal love and compassion. But Yashodhara's look speaks more of love than of wonder. The poses of the heads of the mother and the child in the direction of the figure of the Buddha exhibits purity of form.

The artist has deified the Buddha by increasing the dimension of the figure, but he has added some mystic effect by not drawing the outlines with any firm and distinct brush-work. The Buddha thus appears more like an ethereal entity, more like a formless idea than a human being. In contrast, the figures of the mother and the child, though diminutive in size, are clearly drawn in detail. They belong to the earth, full of worldly emotions and feelings.

Laurence Binyon said: "I remember no picture anywhere more profoundly impressive in grandeur and in tenderness."

Maatri-poshaka Jaataka is an exquisite composition painted on the rear wall of the cave between the first and the second cell-doors right of the ante-chamber. The narration in this cave varies slightly from the jaataka story.

The Jaataka: The story as painted runs on the following lines. Bodhi-sattva was born as a magnificent white elephant in the Himalayas. His both the parents were blind and the white elephant took care of them with utmost devotion. Once he helped a man who lost his way in the forest. King Brahma-datta of Varanasi had lost his royal elephant and was prepared to pay a large amount of money to anyone who helped him to get a suitable elephant. The greedy person informed the king of the beautiful mighty elephant he saw in the Himalayas. A happy king sent his army to catch the animal. The elephant, in spite of his might, allowed himself to be bound and brought to Varanasi. But in the royal stable where he was housed he refused to eat the sumptuous food offered to him. The astonished kind wanted to know the reason. He let loose the elephant and followed him along with his solders. On reaching the forest the elephant met his old, blind parents and caressed them lovingly. The king was overjoyed with the devotion shown by the animal to his parents and freed the animal in the forest.

In the jaataka, the elephant was supporting only his blind mother, and hence the title. (maatri-poshaka means 'caring-for-the-mother'). But in the Ajanta narration the elephant was nursing both his mother and father.The story is narrated in two rows, as given in the line sketch PARENTS-CARING ELEPHANT. It starts in the left of the top row with the person who was rescued by the elephant giving out the whereabouts of the white elephant to the king. The scene of the captured elephant being led to the city is painted at the left bottom of the mural. The elephant refusing to eat is shown on the right of the top row. The released animal walking majestically towards the forest is pictured above the captured elephant and the happy reunion is found to the right of the bottom row.

The entire bottom row is shown as CAPTURE, FREEDOM AND REUNION, of which the HAPPY REUNION WITH THE BLIND PARENTS is a detail.In the first scene, the king is sitting on a low throne with his queen standing by his side. A person standing to his left is giving information regarding the white elephant that he saw in the forest. A person, perhaps one of the ministers, is listening to the conversation; so do the two women from a window.

The next scene is shown at the left bottom of the lower row of CAPTURE, FREEDOM AND REUNION, where the captured elephant is led to Varanasi. He is brought under control with strong ropes tied around his hind legs. The elephant is preceded by two horse-riders and followed by a prince with an umbrella over his head.

The refusal of the elephant to eat is shown in the right of the top row. In this scene the king, seen at the centre of the panel, is supervising the feeding the elephant. The king is identified by the customary royal umbrella. To his right half hidden by the wall, the queen is peeping, perhaps watching the strange spectacle. The person to the right of the king is obviously informing him that the elephant is refusing to eat. The maid further to the right is carrying some edibles on a tray. Before the brooding elephant is seen some food in a large vessel and also sugar-canes are seen lying around.

The king, after having failed in his attempt to feed the elephant, orders the release of the animal, fearing that it may die of hunger. He has also ordered his solders to find the reason for the strange behaviour of the magnificent animal.

His being let loose and passing through a gateway can be seen in the middle of the bottom row, CAPTURE, FREEDOM AND REUNION. The animal happy gait of the elephant is unmistakable. The animal is preceded by a few soldiers and followed by a horse rider and a few foot-soldiers.

Finally, the HAPPY REUNION WITH BLIND PARENTS is painted on the right of the bottom row. We see the white elephant sprinkling water over his parents, and announcing his return. Being blind the parents happily caress their son with their trunks.

The importance of the composition lies in the artist displaying what one could call human feelings to animals. We have the white elephant painted four times, each in a different emotional situation. When he is caught and brought to Varanasi, he is moving against his wish. When he refuses food his determination is evident. Next on being let loose, he trots happily to meet his parents. When he meets his parents his joy knows no bounds.

The story of a compassionate buffalo and a mischievous monkey is painted on the right hand wall of the cave of the rear corridor, to the left of the cell door. (mahisha = buffalo)

The Jaataka: In one of his previous births, the Bodhi-sattva was born as a buffalo and lived in the Himalayas. In the same forest lived a foolish monkey, whose normal pranks included teasing the venerable buffalo. The benevolent one tolerated all such mischief. One day a savage buffalo was standing in the place usually occupied by the benevolent buffalo. Without realising it the monkey was upto his usual tricks. The buffalo did not take kindly to the teasing of the monkey, threw him and trampled upon him, in spite of the pleadings by the unfortunate monkey.

The story MAHISHA JAATAKA is painted in two scenes. The first scene may be found at the bottom. The mischievous monkey is troubling the kind-hearted buffalo by sitting oh his back and closing his eyes. In the second scene, the monkey is in trouble for he has teased a wrong buffalo, which has reacted violently to the monkey's pranks. The painting shows the monkey, being thrown down by the angry buffalo, pleading with his tormentor to forgive him. The depiction of the scenes is characterised by vigour, spontaneity and energy.

The mural, Simhala Avadaana, one of the largest compositions, covers the entire wall of the right hand corridor and continues on the upper part of the pilaster between this corridor and the one behind it.

The end of the archaic and the blossoming of the classical period cannot be better represented than by this composition. It is undoubtedly the boldest and the most grandiose composition in the history of Indian painting, a single composition of unparalleled power. This along with the paintings in Cave 16 and those along the two walls of the Hariti Shrine of Cave 2 represent Ajanta's classical peak.

The Story: Simhala was the courageous son of a wealthy merchant in the kingdom of Simha-kalpa. Against the wishes of his father, Simhala undertook a sea-voyage and was shipwrecked. He and the five hundred merchants who were travelling with him, were cast upon the shores of an island called Taamra-dveepa. The island was inhabited by man-eating raakshasi-s. They lured the stranded merchants disguising themselves as beautiful women only to devour them after taking their original form in the night. Bodhi-sattva in the form of a horse, called Bahala, took most of them to safety, by letting them cling to his body when he flew away from the island.

In the meantime, one of the raakshasi-s managed to reach Simha-kalpa and went to King Simha-kesari in the form of a beautiful maiden. She had a child with her, and complained that Simhala had married her and that the child was his, and that he had now deserted her. This was denied by Simhala. But, enchanted by her beauty, the king took her into his harem, in spite of the advice against this. The raakshasi, having managed to enter the palace summoned the other raakshasi-s from Taamra-dveepa. They finally killed the king and other inhabitants of the palace and ate them up.

The terrified people of Simha-kalpa made Simhala their king. He raised a huge army and invaded Taamra-dveepa. In the ensuing battle Simhala defeated the raakshasi-s. For this reason the island was renamed as Simhala-dveepa (the present day Sri Lanka).

The story begins at the bottom right, between the third and fourth cell-doors, where the shipwrecked Simhala accompanied by five hundred other merchants is depicted.

Above the shipwreck scene are shown the merchants, cast ashore, on an island of raakshasi-s being entertained by the latter, who have assumed beautiful forms to entice them. RAAKSHASI-S AS BEAUTIFUL MAIDENS and a RAAKSHASI FEIGNS LOVE shows some of these seductive beauties. The raakshasi-s dally with the beguiled merchants inside well decorated pavilions. Six or seven such decorated pavilions are shown, inside which we see couples indulging in revelry. The scenes are depicted with zest.

One of them is MERRY COUPLES UNDER TENTS being a sample of the bhaava madhura (tender-feeling). The raakshasi-s are shown inside pavilions as beautiful women and outside in their fearsome form. One of the tents is shown in detail in DALLYING COUPLE UNDER A TENT.

When the merchants come under their spell, CANNIBALISTIC RAAKSHASI-S take their real form and kill them with their curved daggers and dreadful fangs, and devour their flesh. On the left a pale-coloured raakshasi is seen with a severed leg in her hand, about to eat it. The one on the right is about to attack a merchant.

In the meantime Bodhi-sattva arrives on the scene in the form of a horse called Bahala. Apprehending danger and accepting the offer of the Bodhi-sattva horse, Simhala with his merchants manage to cling to the to the horse. In this ESCAPE OF SIMHALA the flight of the horse is shown above the scenes of revelry over the fourth cell-door.

On reaching the kingdom of Simha-kalpa, alighting near a gateway, MERCHANTS SHOW THEIR GRATITUDE TO BODHI-SATTVA.

Having lost their prey, one of the raakshasi-s manages to come to Simha-kalpa. Taking the form of a ravishing beauty, she approaches the court of King Simha-kesari.

This is one of the most beautiful compositions in the history of Indian painting. The panel is unfortunately badly damaged. An excellent line diagramme of the panel COURT SCENE by Syed Ahmad and a colour reproduction the INFATUATED KING will be referred to in the following discussion. On the left (in the line sketch) we see the raakshasi arriving as a beauty of unsurpassable elegance. She is seen standing on a lotus with a large retinue of attendants carrying her toilet. The procession includes an elephant and other paraphernalia. We now move to the right. Within the pavilion the king is seen seated with the usual bolster at the back.

The king becomes enamoured with her beauty and takes her as his wife. But obviously the old minister had advised him against this act. The minister can be seen to the left of the king with his chin on a walking stick, suggesting that he failed in his attempt to dissuade the king from his hasty action. In the painting we find two court women at the rear window and two peeping from behind a partition, curiosity writ large on their faces. The next scene is enacted beyond the pillar that is directly behind the brooding minister. Since the king has admitted the raakshasi-in-disguise we see a procession of damsels carrying royal presents for the wedding. Lastly, beyond the last pillar on the right we see the king with her newly married queen, who stands by his left with a child. We see the old minister still sulking at the turn of events.

Having managed to enter the palace the raakshasi-in-disguise summons her friends from Taamra-dveepa. The result is a MASSACRE IN THE PALACE, painted on the extreme left of the wall. The harem presents a gruesome sight. On the left a red complexioned raakshasi is seen holding the hair of a hapless woman and is about to cut it off. Another to the right has thrown down a woman and is pressing her down with her foot. The one on the right has plunged a dagger into the abdomen of a victim. Just above this are found two women in panic, one covering her eyes and the other holding her hands to her breast.

The king is also devoured by the cunning raakshasi (not shown in the figure).Having found a place for sumptuous food we see VULTURES ON THE GATE and hovering over the bloody scene.The panic-stricken people of Simha-kalpa crown Simhala as their king and request him to destroy the raakshasi-s. Simhala, the king, prepares for war against the raakshasi-s and the next scene is of ARMY GETS READY.The last portion, painted between the second and third cell-doors, is important for its magnificent composition, and, fortunately, because it is in a reasonable state of preservation.

On the top-left, we find SIMHALA RIDES A WHITE ELEPHANT with two generals riding on elephants beside him. Over the head of Simhala is the royal umbrella, signifying that he is now the king. The slanting spears, the waving flags, the forward lean of the elephant riders and the curved heads and huddled trunks of the elephants, all express the emotion of movement.

Below this scene the army is seen landing and at the bottom we see the cavalry landing. To its right is the scene showing the landing of Simhala and his generals, all riding caparisoned elephants, reaching the shore, the riders shooting the raakshasi-s with bows and arrows. The royal umbrella, festoons and spearheads form a gorgeous pattern. Moving to the right we see the fierce fighting, leading to the surrender of the raakshasi-s. Some of these demons can be seen with folded arms and some others touching the ground, both denoting surrender.

The final scene, portrayed above the surrendering raakshasi-s, is the coronation of Simhala and the royal celebration.

In the midst of these scenes portraying battles and the turmoil of war, the panel at the same time marvellously recreates an atmosphere of pleasure in the beauties of nature by means of a very simple and natural style, characteristic of the classical mode.

The men are heroic and the women wear the simplest coiffures, with usually a plain ribbon and a few flowers tucked in the hair. Their ornaments generally consists of a single strand pearl necklace, plain round earrings and sometimes a ring. Wigs were not known at the time although corkscrew curls were occasionally beginning to drop over the temples. The costumes are not intricate, and for that reason freely reveal their slender bodies. Both the men and women are generally represented as definite ethnic types, with a fair or reddish-brown complexion, which obviously points to northern influences.%

There are three categories of murals in Ajanta: the first, picture-narratives the subject-matter of which is a jaataka or episodes from the life of the Buddha; the second, decorative designs, mostly found on the ceilings; and, the last, solo-pictures, self-contained with mythological or secular themes. It is possible that we may call some of these pictures secular, simply because we are not able to connect them with any religious theme.

LADY AT HER TOILET, a solo-picture has amazingly escaped the ravages of time. It is painted on the upper part of the pilaster between the front and right corridors. A line sketch TOILET SCENE is included for better appreciation.

It presents an aristocratic lady at her toilet with maids-in-attendance on both sides and a female dwarf further down. The central lady has an apparel of some gossamer-like fabric, almost transparent. The existence of this almost invisible fabric is deduced from the long folds of the lower garment. The lady is adorned with elaborate jewellery and she holds a mirror in her left hand and a champaka flower in her right. The beads of the pearl necklace have been drawn with thick pigment, as in an oil-painting, so that the gems are raised in relief.

The figure of the chair-bearer to the right, though tarnished and dark now, shows spontaneity, elegance and grace.

The artist has painted the lady with the mirror with an unusually slender waist and with the right foot placed somewhat awkwardly. Indian art prefers to depict feminine beauty not as the artist sees her in real life, but as she reveals her charm in his mental vision, endowed with all the attributes showered on her by the classical poets.

The Shilpa Shaastra enjoins "The beauty of a female figure, be that human or divine, should be enhanced by elongating the eyes, enlarging the breasts and reducing the girth at the waist". Thus, a beautiful woman shall have legs coming down like the trunks of a banana tree, and the palms and feet shall resemble the leaves of trees. This simile pada-pallava (feet-like-leaves) was what the artist attempted, and not the feet made of bones and muscles. Sadrishya (resemblance), one of the six limbs of painting, is to be in the realm of bhaava (emotional appeal) and not in the superficiality of roopa (form). Examples of such similitude are charana-kamala (lotus-like feet) and simha-kati (lion-waisted). See also the line sketch pada-pallava (feet-like-leaves).

In drawing the chawri-bearer, who is to the right of the lady, the artist seems to be swayed by the canonical injunctions of the shaastra-s. "She should have her body weight on one leg only, the other being softly touching the ground; she should have graceful curves, alluring contours and should look askance." The other maid also stands in the abhanga stance to balance the composition. The dwarf is certainly not beautiful; but she harmoniously mingles with the schematic composition and accentuates the charm of the three damsels by contrast.

The most notable feature of the painting is, however, the lovely chiaroscuro, shown in the splashes of light on the rose-coloured ranges of the conventional hills and the dark green shade of the foliage of the mango-tree forming the background.

The story of the Bodhi-sattva born as King Shibi who was hailed for his generosity is painted in this cave. We have seen in the Shibi Jaataka of Cave 1, the king cut off a part of his own flesh to a hawk to save a pigeon. In this episode, from the Shibi Jaataka of Cave 17, the king gives away his eyes to a blind Brahmin. The episode is painted on the pilaster between the front and the right corridors and continued on the on the wall of the right corridor.

The Jaataka: King Shibi, ruler of Arittha-pura, was famous all over the world for his observing the Buddhist virtues steadfastly. He distributed alms generously. He built six monasteries, one at each of the city's gates, from where alms were distributed every day. Lord Indra wanted to test his generosity. He came to the king in the guise of a blind Brahmin and sought the king's eyes as alms. Much to the protest of his subjects, the king had his eyes gouged out and presented them to the Brahmin gladly. The story of the generous King Shibi is popular in Hindu and Jaina lore.

The painting is greatly damaged. The earlier visitors have written about four or five scenes, but only a part is now extant. In one of them we can see the king after he has given away his eyes. This is shown as a line sketch KING SHIBI IN PAIN and two details of this scene WAILING WOMAN -1 and WAILING WOMAN - 2. Also reproduced is a view of a MONASTERY. MONASTERY is a view of one of the six halls of alms built by the king to distribute alms to the needy. On the right a person is bringing some stock of grain for distribution. At the centre a royal attendant is pouring grain onto a piece of cloth held by a woman, who is happy to receive it. The one behind her is waiting for his turn. On the left there is a long queue of persons waiting and watching. In the background is a lady in monastic dress holding a tray in her left hand. The whole scene is fully animated. The scene depicts contemporary wooden architecture, and is shown realistically in normal perspective. We have seen that the Ajanta artist has chosen reverse perspective in the Mahaa-janaka Jaataka in Cave 1 and in the Vidhura-pandita Jaataka in cave 2.

The scene KING SHIBI IN PAIN, is subsequent to his giving away his eyes to Indra who came in the guise of a blind Brahmin. King Shibi may be seen sitting on a low throne. From his visage it is unmistakable that he is in great pain. He is supporting his body with his right-hand and is covering his left eye with the other hand. Tears and blood can be seen dripping from the exposed right eye. His sitting posture and his diadem proclaim his royalty.

Detail of two women in sorrow are WAILING WOMAN -1 and WAILING WOMAN - 2. The former is seen beating her chest and pulling her hair, and the pain in the other woman is depicted in her holding her head in her hand. The sorrowful mood of the maidens as well of the of the people around pervades the whole atmosphere. The grief of the women portrayed in this scene is similar to what we observed in the women shown in Shibi Jaataka of Cave 1.

An episode from Mriga Jaataka covers the front wall to the left of the window. (Mriga = deer) The Jaataka: Once the Bodhi-sattva was born as a Golden Deer with a sweet voice. One day the deer found a man drowning. It swam across and saved him. When the man thanked it, the deer requested him to keep its existence secret. On reaching his place, Varanasi, he heard that the King of Varanasi had offered a reward for anyone who could tell him about the Golden Deer. The greedy person guided the king to the abode of the deer. Before the king could shoot it, the deer spoke, and the king was enchanted by its sweet voice. On hearing that the man whom it had saved has treacherously betrayed the Golden Deer, the king decided to punish the man with death. The deer intervened and asked for mercy. The king, then, took the Golden Deer to Varanasi and requested it to give a sermon to his wife.

The story starts on this panel after the greedy man returns to Varanasi. The complete mural is reproduced as a sketch in STORY OF A GOLDEN DEER and some of its scenes are reproduced in colour.

On the top, in the sketch, we can see the man giving out information about the Golden Deer to the king. The king is sitting cross-legged on an erected platform. The drummer on the right suggests that the proclamation of the reward has just been made. There is a group of people excitedly discussing the announcement.

For the next scene we need to go to the bottom of the panel. We see the KING ARRIVES IN THE FOREST with his retinue, the abode of the Golden Deer. The normal commotion associated with such an expedition is portrayed very effectively. The artist has succeeded in capturing the majestic trot of the royal horse.

To the right we see a HUNTER ATTEMPTS TO CAPTURE THE DEER. The hunter is shown of reddish complexion, obviously to highlight him from the others in the crowd. He, while attempting to capture the deer, has his hands chopped off. The chopped hands can be seen just in front of the deer. Here the Ajanta artist has added this dramatic event not found in the jaataka story. To signify the forest location, the artist has painted a lion and a lot of vegetation in the lower portion.

The king, seeing the miracle, comes to know the truth regarding the deer that it is none other than the Bodhi-sattva Himself.

In the middle scene we see The KING RETURNS ON A HORSEBACK along with the GOLDEN DEER CARRIED ON A CHARIOT with great honour. The humility in the visage is unmistakable. Even the horse has lowered its head in reverence. Since it is a ceremonial procession the umbrella is in attendance.

In the previous scene the umbrella was missing, perhaps because while hunting the umbrella-bearer cannot keep pace with the king riding a horse. The deer in the chariot is majestic and composed, as one would be who is about to who would preach the Law of Dharma to the queen on reaching the palace.

Mention may be made here of a common practice in Ajanta composition. Very often episodes of a story are grouped according to the place of their occurrence and not according to their sequence of occurrence as can be seen in this narration. For example, on the top row are grouped together those scenes which took place in the palace, and the forest scenes depicted in the bottom row. We can see similar groupings even in the pre-Christian era paintings like the Shad-danta Jaataka of Cave 10.

Mahaa-hamsa Jaataka, the story of the golden goose must have been very popular at Ajanta because it is also depicted in Cave 2. In this vihaara the story is painted in two episodes on the left wall and the adjoining pilaster of the front corridor, to the right of the cell door.

The Jaataka: On Mount Chitra-koota, there lived a flock of geese and their king was King Dhritarashtra, the Bodhi-sattva born as a Golden Goose. On day Queen Khema of Varanasi dreamt of listening to a beautiful sermon by a golden goose. She wished to hear the complete sermon in person. Having heard about the Golden Goose, the king constructed a beautiful lake, planted five kinds of lotus, scattered different varieties of grain to attract the geese, and laid a snare. He also kept a fowler as a watch. The geese having heard about the beautiful lake sought permission from their king through their minister, Sumukha. The Golden Goose warned them of the danger. But they insisted, and the entire flock went to the lake. While they were enjoying the bounty of grains, their king uttered a cry of warning on sighting the snare. All the geese flew away leaving Sumukha, the minister and King Dhritarashtra. The king asked his minister to escape, but he refused. Seeing the unusual sight the fowler caught both the birds and took them to the court. They were both received with great honour. Then Bodhi-sattva preached the Law to the royal couple.

In the first part the hunters are seen catching the sacred bird and his chief adviser, the sacred golden goose, in the lotus lake. This panel is greatly damaged.

A FOWLER BRINGS THE GEESE TO THE COURT is painted on the pilaster to the right to the court scene which is to follow. Refer also to the line diagramme GOLDEN GOOSE BROUGHT TO THE COURT. In this we see the fowler carrying two birds, one in each hand, and also a few birds flying away. In terms of style, the birds are painted in a similar vein to the hen in the scene CHILDREN PLAY WITH A HEN. Although the firm brush strokes have given the birds in this cave, an appearance of solidity and firmness, the composition and their movements in the air are most natural.

The final part depicts a court scene in which the GOLDEN GOOSE GIVES A SERMON to the Raja of Varanasi and his consort. The king's court where the Bodhi-sattva gives a sermon is reminiscent of the court scenes we have scene elsewhere in Ajanta - of King Mahaa-janaka of Mahaa-janaka Jaataka in Cave 1 and of King Sanjaya of Vishvaantara Jaataka in Cave 17 for example.

It may be noted that the torso of the King of Varanasi is also modelled after a lion like King Sanjaya of Vishvaantara Jaataka.

We have some excellent designs on the ceilings in this cave also. As we enter the cave we see SIX GANDHARVA-S on the ceiling of the verandah. Each pair of adjoining gandharva-s have one arm common to both. A part of this can be seen in on the top portion of the colour reproduction MAANUSHI BUDDHAS AND MITHUNA FIGURES.

The entire ceiling of the main hall, INTERIOR VIEW OF CAVE 17 looks like the canopy of a shamiana, the decorated wedding tent of India. An interesting geometrical pattern, a detail from this ceiling painting may be seen in GEOMETRICAL DESIGN. It may also be seen that the pillars and their capitals are also decorated.

A part of the painting on the ceiling of the aisle can be seen in A VIEW OF THE AISLE OF CAVE 17, and in the scene VISHVAANTARA CONSOLES HIS WIFE. It may be seen that decorations using flowers, geometrical forms, birds and animals are painted in SQUARES AND RECTANGLES - 3 similar to the designs in Cave 1 and Cave 2.

RESTING BULLS, a lovely study of two animals, particularly the recumbent one, of free-flowing graceful lines, painted in one such rectangle.

Though there are no surviving paintings in this cave, this magnificent chaitya belonging to the Mahaayana period is taken for discussion because it is a treasure-house of sculpture. The relief sculpture on the facade, the naaga couple on the flanking wall to the facade, and the chaitya interior, particularly, the stoopa are worth studying in detail.

Facade: The highly ornate facade belongs to the classical period (5th century AD). The facade was artistically conceived resulting in an aesthetic unity of architecture and sculpture.

Projecting from the main entrance and supported by two beautifully moulded pillars is the mukha-mandapa (the passage leading to the shrine).

RAHULA RECEIVING BEGGING BOWL FROM THE BUDDHA (to your right); the Buddha appears in his mendicant garb at the palace door at Kapila-vastu where his son Rahula, led by his mother Yashodhara, receives the begging bowl from him. His superhuman stature and spiritual expression indicate his peerless wisdom and compassion. The crown held over His hallowed head by angels further enhances this effect. A comparison with the famous painting, MOTHER AND CHILD BEFORE THE BUDDHA in Cave 17 would be rewarding.

To the right of this, beyond the pilaster, the Buddha is again portrayed. He is standing in various mudra-s, on a double lotus against the drum of the stoopa under a finely carved makara torana (portal-of-crocodile) motif. Above this rises the anda (signifying the universe, literally, egg, symbolising that the universe sprang from the 'egg' of Brahma, the creator), harmika and the triple chhatra-vali (umbrellas), flanked by celestials.

The corresponding figure on the left side depicts BUDDHA IN THE VARADA-MUDRA, his figure steeped in karuna (compassion). On his right is a standing woman bereft of all ornaments and near his left foot is a prostrate woman. It is believed that these two are portrayals of Aamra-paali, a famous courtesan of Vaishali. According to Ashva-ghosha, Amra-paali appeared in white garments and devoid of body-paint and ornaments before the Buddha like a woman of good family at the time of worshipping and 'prostrated her slender body like a blossoming creeper and stood up full of piety'. Both the Buddhas stand gracefully in slight tribhanga postures. Their being half-inclined inwards, and therefore, facing the devotee entering the shrine has to be intentional, with their benign expressions creating a feeling of assurance in the heart of the worshipper. Even in the absence of the painted plaster today, one can marvel at the plastic wonder - the soft mellifluous contours of the bodies, coupled with the charming expression exuding spirituality.

The panel on (your) left has two compartments: the upper one having a seated figure of the Buddha in dharma chakra pravartana mudra (setting-wheel-of-dharma-in-motion gesture) and the lower, a standing figure, in varada mudra (benediction-gesture).

The upper part of the FACADE is dominated by an extremely elegant chaitya-window. On either side of the window is found figures of richly-bedecked and majestically standing yaksha-s. One is shown as KUBERA. These two guardians stand in tribhanga posture with a udara bandha (tight-belt) around their bellies. The general contours of the body are soft and fleshy. The facial expression is calm and charming with a smile concealed under a thick upper lip. A dwarf attendant is emptying a bag of wealth. A Buddha figure on his crest is a unique feature.

Naaga Couple: On the flanking wall at a right angle to the facade, the naaga-raja (king-of-the-serpents) is carved in the niche. It is a marvellous piece of sculpture, and a lovely example of the classical art of the 5th century, AD. The king of the serpents sits on a rocky platform in mahaa-raaja-leela (royal-sitting-posture-of-ease) with a seven-hooded cobra behind his head, in royal splendour, a figure of majesty and dignity, with an impressive crown. His queen, a small and lovely creature, leans lovingly against his shoulder, both at ease, each leg in a different pose, comfortable and informal. , whic

From behind emerges a maid-in-waiting, undraped, waving a fly-whisk in the background, a symbol of royalty. The bodies are beautifully modelled; the king is powerful with a well-built masculine body; the two women are gentle, delicate and lovely.

There is no excess, no overstatement anywhere. The entire composition is excellently balanced with the king dominating the scene and the maid being the least important. Indeed, this is a typical example which shows that the Indian relief work is often much more pictorial than sculptural. This is very much like a painting, set in a frame. If you examine the two pilasters which frame this 'picture' so handsomely, you will observe that the ornamentation on these pilasters is also very moderate, most of the shaft of the pillar is undecorated, simple. This is most characteristic of the classical style. The same is true of personal jewellery: the king and queen have fine crowns, no doubt, but otherwise they have only a few ornaments on their bodies: a necklace, a few bracelets, armlets for the king, and an anklet for the queen.

The beauty, the dignity, the elegance of this tranquil scene of 'royal portraiture' is undisturbed by extraneous adornment. Here is classic idealisation, the classic love of beauty, simplicity and dignity.

Buddha in the Niche: Opposite the naaga raja image is the figure of the Buddha in pralamba-paada (leg-hanging) posture and in dharma chakra pravartana mudra. Absorbed in contemplation, he is seated on a raised platform with his feet resting on a double lotus pedestal.

CHAITYA INTERIOR: Chaitya interior is an architectural and sculptural treasure, with the lavish sculptural decoration on pillars, capital and triforium and a standing image of the Buddha against the stoopa within a highly ornamented niche. On the capitals and the triforium are niches containing Buddha figures in dhyana mudra (meditating-attitude) separated from each other by a variety of foliage designs.

The stoopa itself attracts the visitor and holds him spellbound by its artistic charm. In contrast to the stoopa-s in caves 9 and 10, this stoopa shows advanced characteristic with base-mouldings, a round anda and a tall harmika with tapering triple chhatravali-s supported by yaksha-s and surmounted by a poorna ghata (ceremonial-jar), reaching to the vaulted ceiling. The serenity and charm of the central Buddha dominate the warm spiritual atmosphere of the prayer-chamber.

This chaitya follows cave 19 in general arrangement and decoration, but is considerably larger. This cave is also taken for discussion for its sculptural details, and the variations in approach to sculptural decoration.

In this cave the departure from the earlier tradition of decorating the interior walls with painting has been replaced by a large number of small and large sculptures panels. However, the ornamentation, executed in meticulous detail, gives the feeling of excess and a lack of the graceful proportions and rhythmic correlation of Cave 19.

Mention may also be made on changes in the structure and the decoration of the stoopa from the earlier ones. The stoopa proper has been relegated to the background, the emphasis being more on the elongated and decorated plinth. In the front of it is carved with a figure of the Buddha seated in the pralamba paada (hanging-legs) attitude under a pavilion.

There are two sculptural compositions, which arrest our attention. Both of them are found on the left wall. One is a colossal reclining Buddha depicting His MAHAA-PARINIRVAANA.

The other is TEMPTATIONS OF THE BUDDHA - SCULPTURE, a composition in painting of the same theme, TEMPTATIONS OF THE BUDDHA has been painted in Cave 1, a comparison between that and this sculpture was made while discussing the painting.

MAHAA-PARINIRVAANA: Near the side door, is a colossal figure representing Mahaa-parinirvaana (Final-Extinction) of the Buddha at Kushi-nagara. The Buddha is reclining on the right side on a couch between two shaala trees. The right hand under his head is placed over a pillow. The folds of the robe and the fingernails are realistic. Apart from the softness of the pillow and other realistic details of the limbs and dress what appeals is the bhaava of the composition. BUDDHA'S NIRVANA exudes calm and total peace.

Below are the figures of His disciples mourning His pari-nirvaana and above are celestial beings rejoicing at the prospect of being with the Lord. At a niche beyond the feet of the Master, ANANDA, the most favoured disciple of the Buddha, is resting his on his right hand and weeping bitterly.

The longest figure of the Buddha in the entire series dominates the composition. The usual problem of frontality has been eliminated by intelligently placing the lower figures with their backs and side profiles towards the viewers. The panel is also important for it makes the beginning of a new method of stuccowork on the sculptures, which are further painted.

TEMPTATIONS OF THE BUDDHA: The second, on the same wall, depicts, TEMPTATIONS OF THE BUDDHA - SCULPTURE by Mara. The Buddha is seated, with his right palm in the bhumi-sparsha-mudra (earth-touching-gesture) under the Bodhi tree at the centre. On the left is Mara on an elephant accompanied by his host of demon-forces attacking the Buddha. On the right is the retreat of Mara. The lower panel shows the three DAUGHTERS OF MARA, Rati (lust), Trishna (thirst) and Arati (delight) tempting the Buddha with their bewitching charm. Their coquettish gestures, attractive dance and melodious music - all failed to tempt Gautama and swerve him from the path of Enlightenment. Here the Ajanta-sculptor excels in the modelling of the danseuse in her tribhanga (triflex) pose with intricately carved headgear, earrings, necklaces and her orchestra comprising the percussionists and a lady playing on cymbals. They are then shown seated on the right lower portion with their daughters, dejected at the failure of their mission.

The story-telling quality of the sculpture, the unity of the composition and the ingenious disposition of the figures attest to the mastery of the sculptor in his art equalling that of his brother artist in painting.

The Ajanta painter has narrated the same scene, TEMPTATIONS OF THE BUDDHA in Cave 1 and a comparison between the two would be rewarding. This panel sets the trend towards the bas-reliefs in the round as seen in Ellora and later at Elephanta.

BBBB>>N"BN--BBABFF--UUBB--d->-B>7W2722W-Fd771WW6;211W881d777---AAAAABCCB8=oFF7WF.Fd8@2BFedaz8bOdeAAeWO7B

oWffWooyoWAWt^WsffofW`W``W`fW`f`fWfff`W]]Wmf`]GG]f]^WGZ]Z^GZZdiGE^WWWEE^dW;<<)0<;E^^mf^`W_ffp^^GEWGWWEW7/G<;W/<A/WA5W5<;5W;;//595/(./5)5<4)W<d

tiffWtWottfttif}oot^W]W_^iZfWfWfff`oftfff``^^GWZ^fdWZ]^m]WmZWWWW`^dZ^G=W5/55/54;GZEGW]GG?WGEd?GW5?G?F5FWGW5<<5p0=5)=W5WG9G;95A<4=;)5.4;<<Z`;W

o[olGFFFW`^f`o`^dW]G^5Wd]W`GWGZE<E5Z^dZ]]Z]=mGGEG=GWW]=G<W=WG?WAGW5/</=G5WG=<=5;=<5/W5GZ?ZZ=Zm`qti

ng[rgvg}}tWW^`E]^?WWd^``ZEdW]ZZ]^ZZZ]^EEZGW5<FG?ZG=W?dA5W<WWA5<5=54W5W55WGGW5W^EZfofo

S. Swaminathan is something of a legend at IIT Delhi: over nearly three decades, hundreds of students and colleagues have partaken of the fruits of his avid, wide-ranging curiosity. Some, no doubt, will have benefited also from his professional, technical expertise - gears, to be precise. But for lesser mortals, it is his other, 'lay' interests that hold greater fascination. Because Swaminathan is, essentially, an enthusiast, and he has the true teacher's gift of being able to communicate enthusiasm. Whether it be the glories of Sanskrit - barely discernible behind the fog of religious obscurantism - or the technical intricacies of the musical system as it evolved from the Sama-veda to now - or, as in the present instance, the many-splendoured delights of Ajanta, Swaminathan is an excellent companion. Of course there are greater experts, whom one reads with respect. But if one is looking for a knowledgeable companion, someone who will hold one's hand and lead one gently into the wonders - the details, the quirks and oddities, the humour and the humanity - that still survive in those dark caves then, quite simply - he's the man!

Contents: Introduction (by A.Ghosh); Ajanta: an artistic appreciation (by Ingrid Aall); The caves: their historical perspective (by M.N. Deshpande); The caves: their sculpture (by M.N.Deshpande); The Murals: Their theme and content (by M.N.Deshpande); The Murals: their art (by Ingrid Aall); The Murals; their composition and technique (by BB Lal); the Murals: their preservation (by BB Lal); Bibliography; Index.

The book is prepared with care. In my opinion a layman is likely to benefit a lot, even though he would need a lot of rereading. The best part of it is the eighty-five colour reproductions. The paintings can further be enjoyed through twenty excellent line drawings. The sixteen black and white reproduction of sculpture and their explanations are extremely useful. An excellent book and extremely cheap. I would strongly recommend every one to procure a copy before it becomes 'out-of-print'. There is also a Hindi edition which, for unknown reason, is priced at Rs. 800/-.

Contents: Like all other ASI booklets, this is also packed with authoritative information. After a brief introduction, it describes every one of the 30 caves.

Another excellent book. When I bought a copy in 1988, it was on the ninth edition and its price was ridiculously low, a mere Rs.4/- for a booklet of 80 pages and 20 B/W plates. It is a pity one cannot buy copy in a normal bookshop. One does not get even at the ASI stall at Ajanta. Very strongly recommended.

A very well written book; but I am afraid, not many people can have access to a copy of this book. The 77 colour plates are a treasure. This is supplemented by very good black and white line sketches. The text, though continuous, describes the history, development of art form etc, very lucidly. Appended to the text is a very detailed description of the seven most important caves, listing all the paintings found in them and describing in some detail the painting reproduced in the book. All the jataka stories whose scenes are depicted are also given which is very useful.

Contents: Layout and plans of Ajanta caves; cave I to 30; Development of Rack Architecture; Essence of Indian painting; Grammar of Ajanta paintings; Ajanta and the Art of India-Asia.

This is a very interesting book. The treatment is rather unconventional. The author, a reputed story teller (and engineer!), penned this book with love and dedication, and of course, with scholarship. With the thoroughness of an engineer he explains the lay-out of the caves through the medium of engineering drawing. All the important caves are explained in terms of the paintings found there. The best part is that all the characters of each scene is described in some detail. In the later chapters, rock architecture, principles of Indian painting and Ajanta inspiration at home and abroad are discussed and are comprehensible to even laymen. The pity is that there are no colour reproduction, though the line sketches are reasonably good. An excellent book. I have not seen it at any bookshop and I am not certain whether it is available at all. But I understand from my Bengali friends the original in Bengali is very popular.

This is more of a booklet (of 18 pages of text). The 28 colour reproductions are excellent. The treatment of subject matter is rather sketchy. As far as the availability is concerned, you may have to be lucky like me to find one at some second-hand book shop on the foot-path.

## Author: T.V. Pathy, Marathwada University, Aurangabad; Published by Smt.T.V.Pathy,

Authored by an extremely knowledgeable academician, the book describes the three sites in and around Aurangabad in about 90 pages. The approach is fresh and the book is extremely readable. The authors intimate knowledge is revealed in the book. Both laymen and knowledgeable would find the book very rewarding.

7. The Ajanta Caves: Ancient Paintings of Buddhist India Text and Photographs by Benoy

Contents: Introduction; The compassionate world of Buddhism; The site of caves; The early Hinayana phase; The second creative phase: Working methods and scale; The nature and scope of mural paintings; On the preservation of paintings; The sculptures; Discovery and appreciation; The role of photography; The impact of Ajanta on Asian art Note on the Jataka stories by Sangitika Nigam The Principal Painted Caves; Colour plates; Description and plans Appendix: Early description of the Ajanta caves and their discovery Select Bibliography Index

This book, with 213 illustrations, 189 in colour, is the greatest boon to Ajanta lovers. The true-to-life colour hotographs are the best photographs of Ajanta. The author has used special techniques for photographing the paintings without any artificial light. A great treasure to be acquired by any Ajanta enthusiast.The book at the time of writing costs 42 pounds.

Contents: Ajanta and Buddhism by Andre Bareau; General and plan of site; The Buddist Paintings of Ajanta by Amina Okada Grotto - Monastery I: The Assault of Mara; Sibi Jataka; A Monk at the Door of a Palace; Champeya Jataka; A Court Scene; A Palace Scene; Sankhapala Jataka; Mahajanaka Jataka The Indian Ideal of Feminine Beauty: The 'Beautiful Bodhisattva' and The Bodhisattva Avalokitesvara; Buddha in Various Attitudes The Notion of Landscape in Ancient Indian Art Grotto - Monastery II: The Birth of Buddha; Devotes with Offerings; ; Buddha in Various Attitudes; Vidhurapandita Jataka; A Thousand Buddhas; Buddha in Tusita Heaven

Composition and Continuous Narrative in Indian Art Grotto - Monastery XVII: The Seven Maushi Buddhas and Maitreya; A Prince distributing Alms; The Divine Indra; Apsara; The Story of Nalagir, the furious Elephant; ahula presented to Buddha; Hamsa Jataka; Simhala Avadana; A Princess at her Toilette; Matrposhaka Jataka; Scenes of Buddha Preaching; Vishvantara Jataka; Scenes of Animal Life Returning to Ajanta by Jean-Louis Nou Bibliography

Ms AMINA OKADA, who has written the scholarly text, is a curator in the Guimet National Museum of Asian Art in Paris. Brilliant photographs are the contribution of JEAN-LOUIS NOU, one of the foremost photographers of India, who has provided photographs for for many books on Indian and Asian art. The introduction is written by ANDRE BAREAU who is a Buddhist scholar, and teaches Buddhist Studies at the College de France. All these facts make the book an excellent reference work on Ajanta paintings. The book is published in 1996 by Brijbasi Printers Private Ltd, New Delhi. The original French edition was published in 1992.

## Author: Dieter Schlingloff, published by Munshiram Manoharlal Publishers Pvt Ltd (1999);

The author was the head of the Department of Indology and Iranian Studies at the University of Munich. For more than 30 years, Ajanta was the subject of his reasearch.

This excellent first volume presents line drawings of all narrative paintings, together with a short description of each scene. The line drawings are by Monika Zin, based on preliminary drawings by Matthias Helmdach. These drawings, the book informs us, do not represent the pictures in their present state of preservation, but are based on Griffiths' copies, made in the second half of the 19th century, (and in some cases on even older copies by Gill).

This is the only book I found which gives the location of the various scenes. Since it is pictorial, it is easy to follow. As per the book, the scenes are identified on the basis of their literary source. But such identification does not agree with what is generally accepted.

The second volume is under preparation and is on the Devotional and Ornamental Paintings by Monika Zin.

## Author: Lady Herringham, with Introductory Essays by Various Members of the Indian

## Society; Published by Aryan Books International, New Delhi (1998); Price Rs. 1800

Contents: Table of Plates; The Jatakas referred to in the Table of Plates; Notes on the Previous Copyings of the Frescoes; The Expedition (by Sir Wilmot Herringham); Notes on the History and Character of the Paintings (by Lady Herringham); Note on the Manner of Taking the Copies (by Dorothy M Larcher); The place of the Ajanta Paintings in Eastern Art (by Laurence Binyon); The Import of the Ajanta Paintings in the History of Art (by William Rothenstein); Buddhist Cave Temples and their paintings (by FW Thomas); Short Bibliography

Lady Herringham was one of the pioneer artists who visited the Ajanta caves and had made many reproductions of the paintings. She made three visits: 1906-1907, 1909-1910 and 1910-1911. Abhinindranath Tagore of the Calcutta School of Arts sent some students to assist her. They include Nandlal Bose, Asit Kumar Haldar and Samarendranath Gupta. In addition Syed Ahmad and Muhammad Fazl ud Din, both from the erstwhile Hyderabad State of Nizam and Miss Dorothy Larcher from England assisted her.

The book contains reproductions in both in colour and in mono-chrome by Lady Herringham and the artists who assisted her.

Author: Charles Fabri; Published by Affiliated East-West Press (P) Ltd., New Delhi; 1970

3. Art and Architecture in Ancient and Medieval Periods, published by Publication Division;

4. 5000 Years of the Art of India, by Marrio Bussagli and C. Sivaramamurti, Published by Harry

3. J. Griffiths, The Paintings in the Buddhist Cave-temples of Ajanta, Khandesh (India), in two

4. J. Burgess, The Ancient Monuments, Temples and Sculptures of India, portfolio illustrations

5. J. Fergusson, History of Indian and Eastern Architecture, revised, edited and enlarged by
